# Fundamentals and Foundations of Constitutional Law

## Limited Government – Core Principle of Constitutionalism

Constitutionalism at its most fundamental level embodies the principle of limited government. It is fundamentally about imposing constraints and restraints on government power, **preventing** the concentration of authority in any single individual or institution and is associated with the protection of individual rights against the state (Whittington, 2008).

*   While seemingly simple, this concept carries profound implications for how one understands the relationship between the state and its citizens and between different branches of government.
*   In essence, constitutionalism is the rejection of totalitarianism (power being concentrated within any one person) – it requires commitment to political democracy and a representative government, while the people remain sovereign (Henkin, 1993).
*   It is concerned with curbing oppressive government, preserving individual freedom while retaining a realm for the exercise of legitimate government power.

### Relationship between constitutionalism and the written constitution

**_The relationship shows the significance of a constitution_**

A constitution is a document – it is a framework that establishes government, allocates powers, sets out procedures and articulate a nation’s broad goals and objectives. It provides the formal structure for governance and establishes the legitimacy and authority to govern.

*   However, without the overarching principle of constitutionalism, a constitution remains merely words on paper.

Without constitutionalism – the normative commitment to limited government – government power can become unconstrained in practice. Even countries like Saudi Arabia and North Korea possess written constitutions, but these documents primarily serve to enable rather than constrain government action. The absence of a genuine commitment to constitutionalism allows government to act against citizen’s interests or national welfare with impunity. Fundamental liberties may exist on paper but lack meaningful enforcement.

*   This is why constitutionalism matters – it transforms constitutional provisions from aspirational text into operative constraints on power.

### What is the opposite of constitutionalism?

Constitutionalism is NOT (a) a mere existence of a constitution (Henkin, 1993), (b) strict compliance with procedure regardless of substantive content, as constitutional restraints can be employed to rationalise the arbitrary use of power, (c) liberalism, as constitutionalism is the commitment to a supreme law that limits power – it is sited where national history, custom, religion, social values and assumptions above government meet positive law, whereas liberalism is just one specific ideology that prioritizes individual rights.

### Written constitution without constitutionalism (and vice versa)?

It is possible to have constitutionalism without a written constitution. There is a distinction between constitutionalism as a system of restraints or “higher law” and the physical existence of a written document.

#### Generally yes, possible

Patterson argues that constitutionalism existed in ancient Greece and Rome, even though it was not embodied in a document. For example, in Greece, the constitution was the “general nature or character of the state”, the “organic character of the state”, rather than a “written fundamental law”. Greece still nonetheless maintained a distinction between ordinary laws (nomoi) and fundamental law (politeia) (Patterson, 1948). Similarly, the ancient Romans contributed the “principle of a higher law” and “limited government” without relying on a written statute to validate it. For the Romans, the distinction between supreme and ordinary law was “one of substance not form” (Patterson, 1948).

Further, Friedrich argues the roots of constitutionalism is in the “medieval heritage of natural-law doctrine” rather than in written charters (Friedrich, 1974). He argues that medieval constitutionalism functioned through a systems upheld by groups like the church and barons, who used “secularized versions of natural law” to check the power of royal bureaucrats (Friedrich, 1974). This demonstrates that the essential idea of restraints predates the modern written constitution.

Klarman provides a modern analytical defense of unwritten constitutionalism, describing it as “constitutionalism in the British sense” (Klarman, 1998). He argues that a written constitution is “not only unnecessary... but it is also insufficient” because a text cannot logically validate itself without creating an “infinite regress problem”. For Klarman, the validity of any legal order, whether written or unwritten, relies on the “bare fact of political acceptance” (Klarman, 1998).

However, Grimm notes a shift in the modern era, arguing that a “constitution in the modern sense” is distinct because it is explicitly a “set of legal norms” rather than a “philosophical construct” (Grimm, 2013). In this specific modern context, the constitution is viewed as “prescriptive... supreme law” that emanates from a specific political decision – it is created by the will of the people . This suggests that in the modern era, to have a command that is prescriptive usually should require it to be written down so it can be enforced against the government. Further, if the constitution comes from a specific decision by the people to limit power, that decision is almost essentially a written act.

## Core Features of Constitutionalism

Constitutionalism operates through four core interconnected features, each serving as a mechanism to limit and check governmental power – (1) Separation of Powers, (2) Popular Sovereignty, (3) Rule of Law, (4) Protection of individual rights and freedoms (including minorities); ofc we also have consti. supremacy, representative democracy etc…

### Separation of Powers

The doctrine of separation of powers represents the first and most fundamental mechanism for operationalising constitutionalism. This principle divides governmental authority among three distinct branches – the legislature (making law), the executive (implementing and executing law) and the judiciary (which interprets and applies the constitution and other laws) (Locke, developed by Montesquieu).

Separation of powers does not merely bring about the division of functions but also creates a system of **checks and balances**.

*   Each institution possesses the capacity to scrutinise and constrain the others’ exercise of power. The judiciary interprets laws and can review executive and legislative action for constitutional compliance. The legislature can hold the executive accountable through parliamentary processes. These mutual checks prevent any single branch from accumulating tyrannical power.

The judiciary’s role in this system goes beyond mere adjudication. As the branch tasked with interpreting and upholding the law, the judiciary **ensures that governmental exercises of power remain constrained by legal limits**, including constitutional boundaries. This brings us to the closely related concepts of **judicial independence and judicial review**, both essential for effective constitutionalism.

*   The judiciary cannot perform its checking function without independence from political pressure, and it cannot enforce constitutional limits without the power to review governmental action against constitutional standards.

### Popular Sovereignty

Popular sovereignty represents the foundation of legitimate government in democratic systems. This principle establishes that sovereignty (ultimate political and legal authority) resides not in any ruler, but in the people themselves. Government authority emanates from the will of the people, expressed through regular, free and fair elections where citizens exercise universal suffrage to choose their representatives.

*   Popular sovereignty functions as a **political check** on government power. When citizens can vote out a government they disapprove of, elected officials remain accountable to the electorate. This accountability mechanism constrains how a government exercises its authority.
*   A government that acts contrary to popular will risks electoral defeat – this **political check** complements the legal and institutional checks provided by separation of powers, creating multiple layers of constraint on governmental action.

### Rule of Law (Pre)

For both separation of powers and popular sovereignty to function, the rule of law must be operational. When each branch of government is guided by rule of law principles, the government understands that it cannot exercise power arbitrarily. All governmental action must conform to legal limits prescribed by the constitution and other laws.

*   **All power has legal boundaries and everyone including government officials stands equally subject to the law.**

### Protection of Individual Rights and Freedoms (incl. minority rights)

This represents a possibility rather than a universal requirement of constitutionalism. The emphasis on the protection of rights varies significantly across different constitutional systems, and this leads us into different _types_ of constitutionalism.

*   Where present, constitutional rights serve as devices or tools to restrain governmental exercises of power. The government cannot violate constitutionally protected rights without legal consequences.
*   Additionally, rights protection aim to prevent the tyranny of the majority against minorities – a critical concern in diverse, democratic societies where majority rule might otherwise marginalise minority interests.

## Types of Constitutionalism

Understanding that constitutionalism is an elastic concept proves essential for sophisticated constitutional analysis. While the core idea of limited government remains constant, multiple variants exist that differ in how limitation is operationalised and applied.

*   The features of constitutionalism vary across different jurisdictions. Recognizing these variations enables us to evaluate governmental actions and judicial decisions with nuance, rather than applying a one-size-fits-all standard.

### Liberal vs Non-Liberal constitutionalism

#### Liberal Constitutionalism

Liberal constitutionalism embraces all four core features of constitutionalism – SOP, popular sovereignty, rule of law and robust protection of individual rights and freedoms including minority rights. Two distinct characteristics of liberal constitutionalism sets it apart from its alternatives.

1.  Liberal constitutionalism adopts an intensely individual-centric orientation. It prioritises protection of individual liberties – rights to property, private choice, free contract, and personal autonomy. The individual stands at the centre of constitutional concern, with the state’s primary purpose being to protect individual freedoms from encroachment.
2.  Liberal constitutionalism rests on an underlying assumption of **distrust in government**. This distrust stems from the view that humans possess unlimited ambitions and therefore must be controlled. Political authority cannot be trusted to exercise power benevolently without constraint. Consequently, constitutional mechanisms exist to cabin governmental authority within strict boundaries, preventing abuse of power that would otherwise inevitably occur – it envisions robust regulation of state power (_Thio, Treatise_).

It is characterised by the promotion of individual autonomy, rights as trumps, and a neutral state that facilities individual autonomy by taking a _laissez-faire_ approach to issues of morality instead of a shared conception of a common good (_Thio, Treatise_).

Overall, liberal constitutionalism “gives its heart to liberalism,” prioritising private choice to facilitate the “unrestricted individual” (Walker, 1997). In this model, the state acts as a “neutral arbiter,” forswearing concern with the citizenry’s character and leaving normative orientations to individuals (Walker, 1997). This aligns with the requirement that government respects individual rights – generally those in the Universal Declaration of Human Rights – and functions strictly as a limited government to prevent “government by decree” (Henkin, 1993).

*   Basically, the government is neutral and let individuals choose their own values and their way of life.

#### Non-Liberal Constitutionalism

Non-liberal variants of constitutionalism share the commitment to limited government but operationalise it differently. Generally, non-liberal constitutionalism positions the individual within the community rather than treating the individual as an isolated rights bearer (Walker, 1997). Here, the individual is viewed not as a source of value but as a “participant in a given system of meaning to which allegiance is naturally owed” (Walker, 1997). The state expressly promotes the vision of common good.

*   Some systems attempt to balance individual interests against community welfare, while others subordinate individual interests to collective goals entirely.

This has a **communitarian emphasis**, manifesting in attention to the relationship between citizens and government, with reciprocal duties on both sides. Unlike liberal constitutionalism, which focuses primarily on what the government _cannot_ do to citizens, non-liberal constitutionalism articulates what citizens owe to the state and community, alongside what the state owes to citizens.

*   This basically promotes certain favoured patterns of life such that the individual is not the source of value but rather a participant in a given system which gives them value. Consequently, the state’s institutions are designed to promote “favoured patterns of life or attitude” rather than remaining cosmopolitan (Walker, 1997).
*   This includes Buddhist constitutionalism (practiced in Thailand, where govt. power is constrained by Buddhist principles and teachings > Liberal rights) and Confucian Constitutionalism (found in East Asian countries like Japan, Korea. China) where constraints on govt. power derive from universal truths embodied in Confucian teachings.

_NB_: Non-liberal constitutionalism must still be “systemically non-tyrannical” and “non-totalist” to constitute true constitutionalism – the state must still place restraints on how far they promote “even the best way of life” (Walker, 1997) – basically the government may promote certain shared values but must still limit its power and avoid oppression.

##### Confucianist Constitutionalism

In Confucianist Constitutionalism, there is an emphasis on morality of leadership, and trust in the leader who has the mandate from heaven. Law is also seen as an instrument of effective governance rather than a constraint on government (Ginsburg, 2002).

*   Instead, the Confucian principles/teachings of morality, based on rites and rules are what constrains the leader/government.

### Singapore’s Model of Constitutionalism

Singapore is clearly situated within the non-liberal model of constitutionalism, but SG has still developed its own distinct brand of constitutionalism, characterized by certain key features. This can be seen in the expressly endorsed various “Singaporean” shared values, including nation before community and society over self, consensus over contention and racial and religious harmony (_Shared Values White Paper_). There is thus a clear emphasis on communitarian over individualist values and has also been labelled as communitarian constitutionalism (_Menon CJ, The Path to Exceptionalism_).

1.  SG’s constitutionalism attempts to balance community and individual goals. The system permits increasing political participation and debate, allowing diverse views to be aired. However, these freedoms operate within boundaries defined by concerns for social purity and social cohesion. The operating principle is “society above self” but not to the complete exclusion of individual expression.
2.  Singapore’s governing ethos emphasises **trust in government** rather than distrust. This aligns SG with Confucian constitutional systems that similarly rest on trust in political authority. The model assumes governance by honourable individuals with a duty to act rightly for the people, contrasting sharply with the liberal assumption of unchecked governmental ambition requiring constant constraint. This cultural bedrock in Asia aligns with a “Confucian” constitutional tradition where power is viewed as “indivisible” rather than separated (Ginsburg, 2002). In this worldview, the state and society form an “organic unity”, and law functions primarily as an “instrument of state power” rather than a constraint upon it.
3.  Singapore grants the State a substantial role in shaping national and individual character. Rather than emphasising free and private choice, Singapore’s model embraces a more paternalistic, top-down approach where the state actively moulds citizen behaviour and values to serve collective goals.

##### Trust in government is also reflected in case law

Trust in government is also reflected in the Singaporean courts’ approach towards political defamation (and award of damages (_Roy Ngerng_) – due to the premium placed on the moral authority of our leaders, any defamatory statements that damage this moral authority is harshly dealt with. Courts have emphasised that our political leaders’ reputation is of utmost importance to ensure trust in our public institutions (_Jeyaretnam_; _Review Publishing_).

SG’s distinct constitutional identity derives partly from historical context – SG’s identity: Racial, Religious, Cultural existed from inception, not as a product of later immigration.

*   SG ranks consistently as the most religiously diverse national globally. This foundational diversity shaped constitutional development different than in countries like the UK and USA, which began more homogeneously and diversified over time.

#### Distinctive features of a SG brand of constitutionalism.

The SG brand is defined by a prioritisation of political stability, economic performance, and a cultural preference for strong, effective authority over the liberal preoccupation with limiting state power.

*   While Asian constitutionalism often incorporates Western forms like written constitutions, it frequently diverges in substance. Beer (1979) notes that Asian systems often reject American notions of absolute freedom of expression or property rights, emphasising instead economic justice and self-government. This reflects a broader cultural orientation described by Pye (1985), where authority is not viewed with the inherent suspicion common in the West. Instead, for many Asians, “acceptance of authority is not inherently bad but rather is an acceptable key to finding personal security” (Pye, 1985). Unlike the Western search for autonomy, the Asian search for identity often involves locating an “appropriate paternalistic form of authority”, leading to a lack of enthusiasm for limiting power (Pye, 1985).

#### Evolving recognition of political pluralism in Singapore

PM Wong’s speech during the 2025 GE aligns closely with the functionalist and paternalistic views of Pye (1985) and Lee Hsien Loong (2006). By stating that a vote for the opposition “weakens the PAP team” and asserting that minsters’ losses “can’t be replaced”, PM Wong is reinforcing the idea that concentrated authority is necessary for “effectiveness” in facing “serious challenges”. This echoes Huntington’s (1968) prioritization of political strength and stability over the proliferation of “alternative voices,” which PM Wong characterises as a potential distraction from the “burden” of responsibility.

However, PM Wong’s post-election speech does reflect an evolution of the “consensus” model found in the Shared Values White Paper 1991. While the SVWP rejects contention, PM Wong instead welcomed the opposition, saying that their presence would keep his party “on our toes” and “scrutinise” policies. This presents a slight shift towards a check and balance system, but is overall still rooted in Confucian logic. The opposition has a “duty to serve”, and legitimacy is not intrinsic but must be “earned”, by “delivering results” (regardless of party). This reaffirms the centrality of performance legitimacy highlighted by SM Lee, over procedural democracy.

PM Wong also maintains the SVWP’s ethos of “trust” and “integrity” as the bedrock of how politics should be conducted, and that the “privilege given today can easily be taken tomorrow”. This suggests that while Singaporean constitutionalism is indeed evolving to accommodate distinct voices, it retains **its core insistence that political legitimacy stems from the responsibility, integrity and seriousness of the leadership** rather than Madison’s (1788) structural antagonism.

#### SVWP on Contemporary SG Political Culture

The Shared Values White Paper 1991 (SVWP) sheds significant light Singapore’s political culture, by portraying it as deeply communitarian and paternalistic. Singapore’s political culture seeks to anchor a Singaporean identity against “external influences” and “untested fashions” from the West, such as “alternative lifestyles”.

The SVWP enshrines the value of “Nation before community and society above self”, arguing that Singapore’s success depends on the willingness of its citizens to **make “temporary individual sacrifices for the sake of the group”**, **rather than insisting on one’s individual prerogatives**. The SVWP instead looks to promote a culture of **“consensus instead of contention”,** viewing national unity as requiring compromise to avoid tearing the “social fabric”. Furthermore, the White Paper posits a paternalistic role for the state, suggesting that the present generation must “consciously develop” cultural values to guide future generations who might otherwise “lose their bearings”.

##### Theoretical Conflict with Montesquieu & Madison

Montesquieu and Madison argue that the union of powers is dangerous precisely because “apprehensions may arise, lest the same monarch or senate should enact tyrannical laws” (Montesquieu, 1748). For Montesquieu, liberty is impossible without the separation of powers because power must be checked. Similarly, Madison (1788) premises his constitutional design on the fallibility of man, as if “men were angels, no government would be necessary”. Madison’s view on constitutionalism relies on “auxiliary precautions” where “ambition must be made to counteract ambition”, rejecting the reliance on “better motives” or honourable men that the SVWP embraces.

Meanwhile, the SVWP presents a fundamental theoretical conflict with the liberal constitutionalism of Montesquieu and Madison regarding the nature of power and human fallibility. The **SVWP at paragraph 41 explicitly endorses the Confucian concept of government by _junzi_, stating that leaders have a “duty to do right” and should possess the “trust and respect of the population”**. Crucially, it also argues that this model fits Singapore better than the Western idea that a government “should be given as limited powers as possible” and “treated with suspicion”.

#### There is a presumption of constitutionality

SG’s subscription to the concept of _junzi_ is reflected in how the courts have adopted a presumption of constitutionality for Executive Acts and legislation. In _Ramalingam_, the court held that there was a presumption of constitutionality in relation to prosecutorial power; noting that it was an application of the “established principle” that the acts of high officials in SG “should be accorded a presumption of legality or regularity, especially where such acts are carried out in the exercise of constitutional powers”.

*   This is highly reminiscent of the _junzi_ reasoning, since it suggests that one should trust in the morality of our leaders.
*   However, this does not mean the courts blindly follow this presumption – in _Jolovan Wham_ and affirmed in _Tan Seng Kee_, it was held that the presumption of constitutionality is “no more than a starting point”, as relying merely on the presumption would entail presuming the very issue being challenged – the presumption is rebuttable. The court disagreed that such a presumption would not be lightly displaced – the determination of whether a law is constitutional or not is firmly and solely within the ambit and competence of the SG courts. Nonetheless, the SVWP at \[42\] mentions that “Confucianism has no monopoly on virtue. It needs to be brought up-to-date and reconciled with other ideas which are also essential parts of our ethos”.

### Political vs. Legal Constitutionalism

Political constitutionalism entails constraining the government through the political process, such as elections, parliamentary debate and the free press. This is different from legal constitutionalism, which entails the judiciary to constrain the government through the constitution by constitutional interpretation and judicial review (_Thio, Treatise_).

#### Singapore follows political constitutionalism

Overall, Singapore appears to subscribe to aspects of both legal and political constitutionalism.

Judicial power in SG is exclusively vested in the Supreme Court and its subordinate courts as may e provided by any written law for the time being in force, by virtue of Art. 93 (_Mohammad Faizal_). Read together with Art. 4, courts have judicial review jurisdiction, which is the power to pronounce on the constitutionality of statutes passed by Parliament.

*   This is legal constitutionalism.

However, courts in SG have generally been adverse towards striking down the acts of the legislature or giving rights oriented judgements. Rather, they issue suggestive constitutional interpretations and recognise that the ultimate interpretive authority lies in the political system and process – political constitutionalism.

*   In _Teo Soh Lung_, _Tan Seng Kee_ and _Lim Meng Suang_, the court has expressed wariness of becoming a “mini-legislature” and usurping legislative functions. In particular, they emphasised that the courts may not be the appropriate forum to resolve moral or socio-political issues, due to their lack of competence, political legitimacy and democratic accountability, unlike Parliament (_Tan Seng Kee_). The courts therefore generally defer questions they seem as “extra-legal”, such as those concerning social mores or national security to Parliament.
*   A key detail mentioned in _Tan Seng Kee_ was that courts overstepping their boundaries would cause the public to lose faith in their legitimacy.
*   Furthermore, “soft” constitutional laws such as White Papers have influenced judicial interpretation of the constitution – they are deliberately articulated by authoritative government actors (_Thio, Treatise_) and while not legally binding, the courts have afforded them significant weight 🡪 shows that ultimately, the courts prefer to follow the results of constitutional interpretation by Parliament.
*   This judicial deference reflects a broader governance philosophy where, as Cherian George observes, ‘comfort is achieved through control’ - Singapore has shown that ‘liberal politics is not the only possible handmaiden to liberal economics’ (Cherian George, 2020). The PAP’s notion of trusteeship, where it ‘exercises independent judgment on what is in the long-term interests of the people’ rather than responding to expressed preferences at any given time (Cherian George, 2020), provides the ideological foundation for why political processes, not judicial review, serve as the primary constitutional constraint – hence justifying why SG leans towards maintaining political constitutionalism.

However, Singapore is moving towards legal constitutionalism as seen from the rise of cases in the 21st century that is focused on reading the fundamental liberty broadly and interpreting the restrictions narrowly. After all, to meaningfully give effect to constitutionalism, the country should give effect to legal constitutionalism as well.

_Tan Seng Kee_: Case about whether s 377A is constitutional. The court emphasized that extra-legal questions were beyond the purview of the courts. In the case of “highly contentious social issues” such as 377A, it was the “more obvious choice” for such matters to be politically managed rather than being judicially resolved. This is because litigation is simply about an application of the law, while politics was about democratic engagement and engaging with the voters’ wants and thoughts. In fact, an advantage of the political process is that it can accommodate divergent interests and opinions, something which is “never” the constitutional role of the courts. (at \[4\]). The judiciary is for zero sum games, while politics is for a middle ground where “no one side has to lose all” (at \[4\]). Here, politics aims to “forge consensus” while litigation is a win-lose scenario, which makes Parliament the better option. So, it is Parliament who is best placed to “devise a pluralistic vision” that will maximize everyone’s interests. The courts are also unwilling to overstep their boundaries as if not, they risk a diminution in their legitimacy as perceived by the public – they should not enter into matters that “call for resolution by discussion, consensus and debate”. So, if the court overstepped thir boundaries, it would be “injurious to public confidence” (at \[7\]).

_Vellama_: Case about whether the Prime Minister had to call a by-election to fill a vacant SMC seat. The SGCA held that (despite the applicant lacking standing), the Prime Minister should call a by-election to fill a vacancy in an SMC in a reasonable time, with the determination of what a “reasonable time” to be left to the PM. This indicated a suggestive interpretation of Art. 49, encouraging the executive to engage in political deliberation as to the proper constitutional demands for the calling of a by-election.

_Lim Meng Suang_: Another case on whether s 377A was constitutional. Applicant brought in extra-legal arguments. Phang JA reasoned that the applicant’s extra-legal arguments on the unconstitutionality of s 377A was one that was better reserved for Parliament, and that the responsibility for interpreting the constitution ultimately vests in the political process.

#### Is political constitutionalism good for SG?

Given Singapore’s diverse society, coupled with its constantly evolving concerns and interests, political constitutionalism is necessary to achieve a constant negotiation and re-negotiation of constitutional balances. This responsiveness is not something that legal constitutionalism may be able to achieve, given the requirement of a _lis_ and the system of precedent (Dowdle & Tan, Legal or Political Constitution? at 368).

*   Indeed, the Court’s findings in _Tan Seng Kee_ support Dowdle & Tan’s approach. As the court held, the advantage of the political process (and hence political constitutionalism) is that it can accommodate divergent interests and opinions, where “no one side has to lose all”, unlike litigation which is a zero-sum game (at \[4\]).
*   _Tan Seng Kee_ has made reference to the US case of _Roe v Wade_ to illustrate why legal constitutionalism might not be the best way to resolve social controversies. The U.S. Supreme Court’s decision abruptly halted the gradual legislative liberalization of abortion laws. This judicial intervention provoked a strong counter-movement, delaying a stable resolution and entrenching the issue in ongoing legal and political battles. Even decades later, Roe v Wade continues to shape legal discourse and judicial appointments, illustrating the lasting consequences of bypassing democratic debate.
*   However, **for political constitutionalism to serve as an effective constraint, the political process itself must be robust and competitive**. Cherian George in _Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_ critiques that the PAP’s dominance has systematically weakened the non-state institutions that serve as watchdogs in other countries, rendering the public sphere “antiseptically clean, but also devoid of vibrant life”. Because the PAP leadership operates with an “obsession with protecting the state from the unpredictability of democratic politics,” the playing field for political constitutionalism is uneven. Without independent election commissions or an unfettered press to provide the necessary “demand and supply sides” of democratic choice, political constitutionalism in Singapore risks functioning merely as an endorsement of the ruling party’s agenda rather than a genuine check on its power.

Moreover, courts risk a diminution in their legitimacy if they are perceived as having overstepped their boundaries. (_Tan Seng Kee_)

*   This risk may materialise if the courts wade into matters that call for resolution by discussion, consensus and debate rather than by a judgement handed down by a court. If polycentric matters are not debated and resolved by the many but summarily adjudicated by the few, it can be injurious to public confidence.
*   Judicial restraint should be prioritised in the face of disagreements that stem from incommensurable conceptions of the good.
*   Certain issues call for continued discussion and open-ended resolutions rather than win-lose outcomes recorded in a court judgement.

However, a critical limitation must be acknowledged. George argues that Singapore’s system of checks and balances is “weak” not because of Singapore’s size, but because of deliberate structural design choices — and that “no matter how strong its mandate, an elected government’s power to do harm needs to be limited by independent institutions and civil liberties” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). This gives normative bite to the descriptive analysis of political constitutionalism: the same structural features that insulate governance from short-term populism also weaken the checks needed to prevent abuse. The court’s reasoning in Tan Seng Kee — that political processes are better placed to forge consensus on contested social issues — is therefore not self-evidently correct; it presupposes that those political processes are themselves sufficiently open and accountable to produce legitimate outcomes. Where the conditions for genuine democratic contestation are structurally constrained, deferring constitutional questions to Parliament may simply entrench rather than resolve them.

## Rule of Law

At the normative level, the rule of law embodies three fundamental ideas that overlap substantially with constitutionalism’s core commitment to limited government – (1) government limited by and conducted according to law, (2) supremacy of law over arbitrary power and (3) equality before the law.

*   Need to note that SG Courts and decision-makers invoke rule of law principles constantly when evaluating governmental action and justifying constrains on power.

The rule of law entails that all persons, including the government, should be bound by and entitled to the benefit of laws (CJ Menon, 2016) – nobody is above the law.

*   The rule of law is “the bedrock on which our society was founded and on which it has thrived” (_Tan Seet Eng_ at \[1\]).

### Government Limited by and Conducted According to Law

Law constrains governmental power. The government must act according to law, not arbitrary will. This principle protects against tyranny and holds the government accountable for its actions – the government must act according to the legal authority they possess, the law must grant them the power **– ROL principle that all power also has legal limits (_Chng Suan Tze_).**

*   Practically, this idea generates the question (that applies to nearly any scenario) – can the government really do that?
*   E.g. Can the government direct an opposition party to choose a new Leader of the Opposition? This implicates rule of law because we must ask whether government possesses the legal authority for such action. Does law grant government this power? Has government followed proper procedures in exercising whatever authority it claims? These questions about legal authority, process, and substance all flow from the principle that government must be limited by and conducted according to law.

The key questions to examine are – does the law grant the government this power; has the government followed proper procedures in exercising its alleged authority.

### Supremacy of Law vs. Arbitrary Power

The law is supreme over arbitrary power. Governments and legislators **cannot change law or exercise powers arbitrarily**. Even those positioned to make law face restraints on their law-making authority – either from natural law principles, or from prescribed processes for legal change.

*   This is important in analysing constitutional amendments. The legislature possesses the power to make law, including constitutional amendments. But the question remains on whether the legislature really can amend the constitution in any manner it wishes, whether it must follow particular procedures, or whether substantive limitations constrain amendment power 🡪 these questions connect RoL to Constitutional Supremacy.

Arbitrariness can manifest in various forms.

*   E.g. a law imposing excessive punishment disproportionate to an offence exemplifies arbitrary law-making.
*   Arbitrariness also appears **when laws or rules are vague or unclear**, leaving citizens unable to understand how to conduct themselves lawfully. When legal standards remain so uncertain that citizens cannot know whether contemplated actions might be criminal, the law fails to meet rule of law requirements.
*   Basically, can they foresee the consequences of their actions? How should they conduct themselves? If this is unclear, it leads to a rule of law issue.

### Equality Before the Law

Rule of law requires that everyone stands equal before the law as administered by courts. No exceptions exist. Even government officials remain bound by the same laws as ordinary citizens. All laws apply equally to everyone across the board, regardless of position, wealth, or status.

### Thin vs. Thick Conceptions of Rule of Law

#### Thin Rule of Law

The thin rule of law is a procedural, formalistic conception focused on process compliance and equal application. The thin rule of law demands that the government act according to the law, follow prescribed procedures and apply legal rules equally to all persons. It concerns itself primarily with form and process rather than substance or content.

*   Same as what was discussed above – the principles of equality before law, supremacy of law > arbitrary power and government limited by law are the main principles of the “thin” rule of law.
*   This helps to minimise arbitrariness but does not completely eliminate it. It does not remove the legal accountability of governmental actions – the government must follow the right procedures and provide justifications for the particular law and/or actions.

#### Thick Rule of Law

The thick rule of law extends beyond procedure to embrace substantive justice. A thick conception incorporates protection of individual liberties and fundamental rights into the rule of law framework.

*   Under thick rule of law, procedural compliance alone does not suffice, laws must also conform to substantive standards of justice and rights protection.

#### Thin vs Thick

The distinction between the thin and thick conceptions parallels the distinction between liberal and non-liberal constitutionalism.

*   Liberal constitutionalism naturally gravitates towards thick rule of law with its emphasis on individual rights.
*   Non-liberal constitutionalism more readily embraces the thin rule of law, focusing on procedural regularity and equal application without necessarily extending protection to substantive individual liberties.

Understanding which conception of the rule of law a jurisdiction adopts is essential for evaluating whether governmental actions comply with that jurisdiction’s rule of law standards.

##### _Relationship with ouster clauses?_

On a formalistic, thin conception of the rule of law, adherence to ouster clauses may be justifiable as the rule of law views with suspicion judicial intransigence in face of the clear words of the statute.

For the thick rule of law, ouster clauses may be detrimental to the rule of law since it allows the executive or legislature to override judicial authority.

### Rule of Law in SG – Formalistic

SG’s approach to rule of law aligns clearly with the thin, formalistic conception. Several cases illuminate how Singapore courts and government officials conceptualise and apply rule of law principles.

*   So from _Teo Soh Lung_’s approach – you can see that procedural compliance suffices to satisfy the rule of law. It emphasises procedural compliance and equal application rather than substantive justice. The formalistic rule of law demands that governmental action proceed according to law, follow prescribed procedures, and apply legal rules equally to all persons regardless of status.
*   A thick rule of law would scrutinise whether amendments eliminating judicial review for executive detention decisions, even if procedurally valid, violates rule of law’s deeper commitment to preventing arbitrary government power. The government looks at the substantive impact on liberty.
*   Instead, SG’s approach treats RoL as substantially satisfied when (1) government acts pursuant to legal authorization rather than exceeding legal powers; (2) procedures for legal change are followed; (3) laws apply equally rather than discriminating arbitrarily among persons.
    *   Substance and content – e.g. whether laws are just, whether they protect fundamental rights, fall outside RoL’s domain and are political questions for legislative resolution.

| Case | Findings |
| --- | --- |
| Chng Suan Tze v Minister for Home Affairs [1988] | The SGCA articulated what appeared to be a robust rule of law principle – subjective or unfettered discretion is contrary to the rule of law.The court declared that all power has legal limits and that courts should possess the ability to examine exercises of discretion to ensure those limits are respected.NB: The promise of this principle must be understood in context. While asserting that discretion requires limits and judicial oversight, the court’s actual willingness to scrutinise executive discretion (particularly in politically sensitive areas like national security) has proven limited. The principle exists, but its practical application depends heavily on the nature of the power being exercised. |
| Daniel De Costa v Public Prosecutor [2020] | The Court of Appeal emphasized that for the right to vote to be meaningful in a representative democracy, elections must be free and fair.NB: This principle establishes baseline standards: electoral processes must be conducted fairly, without manipulation, coercion, or fraud that would undermine the authenticity of electoral outcomes.This principle connects voting rights to representative democracy’s legitimacy. If elections are not free and fair, the resulting government cannot claim legitimate mandate from the people. |
| Vellama d/o Marie Muthu v Attorney General [2013] | The case concerned the Prime Minister’s discretion to call by-elections when parliamentary seats became vacant.The court began with the broad principle from Chng Suan Tze – all discretionary power is subject to legal limits. Applied to the Prime Minister’s power to call by-elections, this meant the Prime Minister did not possess absolute discretion.This reflects the rule of law principle that govt. power must be legally constrained.However, identifying the actual legal limits proved more challenging. The constitutional provision Art. 49 and the relevant statute provided little clarity about temporal constraints on the Prime Minister’s discretion. The court therefore turned to the IA, which prescribes a standard of “all convenient speed” – itself a vague formulation that provides minimal concrete guidance.To add substance to this indeterminate standard, the court invoked the common law doctrine of a “reasonable time”. The Prime Minister must call by-elections within a reasonable time, and the courts can examine whether this limit has been exceeded in exceptional cases. In the case itself, only two weeks had elapsed since the seat became vacant, clearly within reasonable time. |
| Teo Soh Lung v Minister for Home Affairs [1989] | The case involved constitutional amendments that authorised changes to the ISA, effectively dispensing with judicial review for ISA detention orders except regarding procedural compliance. The amendments dramatically curtailed judicial power to scrutinise executive detention decisions.In other words, this restricted judicial review.The Court upheld these amendments, reasoning that they merely ensured Parliament’s clear intent would not be disregarded. According to the court, Parliament had done “no more than to enact the rule of law relating to the law appliable to judicial review” – Parliament had followed proper amendment procedures, the amendments were “enacting the rule of law relating to judicial review”Parliament passed a new law that clarifies the rules for ISA cases. The judges can no longer review the merits (the reasons) for the government’s decision; they are strictly limited to checking if the government followed the correct procedures. The judges are respecting the Rule of Law by following this new, narrower standard set by Parliament.Parliament has clearly said X is the rule – and the Court will stick by what Parliament wrote and uphold the certainty of the written law. By refusing to override Parliament’s policy choice, they are respecting the rule of law.From the formalistic perspective, procedural compliance suffices for rule of law satisfaction – since Parliament had exercised law-making authority according to prescribed processes, therefore rule of law was respected regardless of the amendment’s substantive impact on judicial checking power or individual liberty.But this goes against thick RoL – since it incorporates substantive justice and rights protection. The thick rule of law would scrutinise whether the amendments eliminate judicial review |

From **_Vellama_**, the problem from a rule of law perspective becomes obvious – the standard remains highly indeterminate. We know two weeks qualifies as reasonable from the case, but then what about the other durations? The “reasonable time” standard provides limited _ex ante_ guidance. Citizens cannot know with certainty what temporal limits apply.

*   This vagueness creates rule of law concerns because **law should provide clear standards allowing people to plan conduct and predict legal consequences**.
*   Nevertheless, **_Vellama_** demonstrates that even within Singapore’s formalistic framework, courts will identify and enforce some legal limits on executive discretion. The limits may be vague, but they _do exist_ and can be judicially enforced. This represents a meaningful, if modest, constraint on government power.

The reasoning by the court in **_Teo Soh Lung_** is also conceptually problematic (and may even be circular).

*   This was akin to saying that Parliament enacted law restricting judicial review, and this enactment constitutes “rule of law” because it is law that Parliament enacted. From a thin RoL perspective, focused solely on procedural compliance, the decision appears defensible, since Parliament followed constitutional amendment procedures – these amendments apply equally to all persons subject to ISA detention, with no procedural irregularities marring the legislative process 🡪 the rule of law requirements were satisfied.
*   However, from a thick rule of law perspective that emphasises substantive justice and rights protection, _Teo Soh Lung_ raises serious concerns. The amendments eliminated meaningful judicial oversight of executive detention power, a fundamental safeguard against arbitrary deprivation of liberty. The substantive impact of this on individual rights proves severe, even if procedures are followed.
    *   This exemplifies how different conceptions of RoL yield different evaluations of the same governmental action.

#### Characteristics in SG’s RoL framework

There are several defining features of SG’s rule of law conception:

1.  **Independent Judiciary**: Singapore emphasises judicial independence as essential to rule of law, particularly for protecting commercial interests and attracting foreign investment. A strong, independent judiciary provides the legal certainty and reliability that businesses require. As CJ Menon’s speeches show, there is a commercial-economic dimension to the rule of law.
2.  **Certainty and Clarity of Laws**: Singapore’s RoL prioritizes legal certainty in both enforcement and articulation. Laws should be clear, and their application should be predictable. This emphasis on certainty reinforces the formalistic character of Singapore’s RoL – what matters is that legal rules are articulated clearly and applied equally, not necessarily that they protect particular substantive rights. The tension between legal certainty and governmental control is most acute in Singapore's approach to media and speech regulation. George describes POFMA as “Singapore's most sweeping media legislation” since 1986, noting that it is “globally unprecedented” in its scope (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). From a rule of law perspective, the concern is whether legislation conferring broad ministerial discretion to determine what constitutes a “falsehood” — and to order corrections or takedowns — provides sufficiently clear and foreseeable legal standards for citizens to know how to conduct themselves lawfully. This connects directly to the arbitrariness concern identified in **_Chng Suan Tze_**: where the legal standard is so indeterminate that its application depends heavily on executive judgment, the law risks failing the rule of law requirement of clarity and predictability. Singapore's thin RoL framework may be formally satisfied if POFMA is applied consistently and with procedural safeguards — but the breadth of ministerial discretion it confers sits uncomfortably with the principle that unfettered discretion is contrary to the rule of law.
3.  **Communitarian Values over Individual Rights**: Individual rights do not figure centrally in SG’s RoL conception. Former Minister for Law Shanmugam and CJ Menon’s speeches have explicitly stated that Singapore emphasises communitarian values over individual liberties (Menon, 2016). This reflects the broader non-liberal constitutional framework. Notions such as dialogue, tolerance, compromise and placing community above self, have modulated court’s approach in ensuring that the rule of law rules.
4.  **Judicial role as Articulating Rules and Policing Breaches**: CJ Menon has described the judiciary’s role as articulating clear rules for government to follow and determining **whether those rules have been breached as the last line of defence** – the court plays a supporting rule to good governance (Menon, 2016). This describes a relatively narrow, procedural judicial function – ensuring government colours within legal lines, but not questioning whether the lines themselves serve substantive justice.
    1.  Underlined by the belief that a court which is respected by the other branches of government can effectively shape the debate and ensure the legality of government actions by setting out its concerns openly and potentially obviating a binary clash between the judiciary and the executive – Chan CJ has characterised as a **“green light” approach to administrative law**. On this view, constitutional law is not primarily to stop bad administrative practices but to encourage good ones: good government should be sought through the political process and public avenues, rather than by redressing bad government through the courts. The courts are not seen as the first line of defence against administrative abuses of power; instead, control comes internally from Parliament and the Executive in upholding high standards of public administration (in other words, they check each other). This contrasts with the “red light” approach in the United Kingdom, where the courts exist in an adversarial relationship with the executive, functioning centrally as a check on administrative power. Singapore’s communitarian culture, as emphasised by CJ Menon, stresses dialogue, tolerance, compromise, and community above self. The “green light” approach fits this ethos: courts support good governance by articulating principles and serving as the last line of defence, but work collaboratively rather than adversarially with other branches.
        1.  There is indeed a distinct pattern of judicial decision-making in which the court elaborates as to rights, government powers, but eventually does not rule against the government. A **charitable view** of this approach is that this is the court’s strategy to engage the government in a dialogue, thereby reflecting our communitarian values. Judgments tend not to be hostile against the government but instead offer guidance to the government. For examples, the courts repeatedly struck down efforts to invalidate s377A, but in their reasoning, they identified aspects in which it may be discriminatory and aspects in which it may not be discriminatory, thereby engaging the government in a conversation. Eventually, the government stopped enforcing the law and later in 2023 went one step further to repeal s 377A.
    2.  Though confrontation in some scenarios is inevitable, in which case the judiciary must stand firm as the last line of defence. Judicial review is the sharp edge that keeps government action within the form and substance of the law like in _Tan Seet Eng_. The commitment of the executive to comply with and abide by law as pronounced by the judiciary is critical to rule of law and good governance.
5.  **Permissible Derogations with Procedural Safeguards**: Singapore’s RoL permits derogation from ordinary legal processes (e.g. under the ISA) provided that procedural safeguards exist. As long as derogations are done according to legal principles, are explained to the public and to affected persons, and follow prescribed procedures, they comport to the rule of law. This reflects SG’s procedural, formalistic approach – concern centres on _how_ extraordinary powers are exercised (with procedural regularity) rather than whether these powers should exist at all.

##### Shah’s tips:

When evaluating whether governmental action complies with rule of law in Singapore, focus primarily on procedural questions: Did government follow prescribed processes? Did it act pursuant to legal authority? Are laws being applied equally? Is there clarity about legal requirements?

*   Arguments based on substantive justice or rights protection, while normatively compelling, may carry less weight in Singapore’s rule of law framework than they would in jurisdictions embracing thick rule of law. This does not mean such arguments are irrelevant or should not be made—they address important questions about whether Singapore should adopt a thicker conception of rule of law. But analyzing what rule of law currently means in Singapore requires attending to the formalistic, procedural emphasis that characterizes the existing framework.

#### Judiciary’s Role in Upholding Rule of Law

The SGCA in _Chng Suan Tze_ upheld the rule of law, noting that the rule of law demands that courts should be able to examine the exercise of discretionary power. The unfettered discretion of a Minister to detain an accused under the ISA was therefore held to be contrary to the rule of law.

*   Following this, the government retaliated by enacting a set of constitutional amendments to take away power from the judiciary to evaluate or adjudicate national security cases, under Art. 149.

Thus, after _Chng Suan Tze_, the courts have taken a more cautious approach towards striking down legislature or executive acts, seemingly adopting the thinner conception of the rule of law.

*   The SGCA in _Teo Soh Lung_ rejected the _Kesavananda_ basic features doctrine and expounded the view that Parliament could amend any provision of the constitution so long as procedures are followed; the court’s role was only to interpret and apply the law as Parliament makes it – it could not usurp Parliament’s legislative function to make laws 🡪 this accords with the positive, formalistic RoL and with a deferential conception of SoP.
*   In _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\], the SGCA suggested that the judiciary may not violate the rule of law by reading unenumerated rights into the constitution (arbitrariness), thereby becoming a super legislature and imposing their own views onto the law.
    *   So – they should just apply the law as it clearly says, respecting RoL.
*   In _Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\] regarding s 377A, the court echoed judicial understanding of RoL and SoP in _Teo Soh Lung_ – they tried to demarcate the role of judiciary and Parliament, based on whether the question was a legal one or a socio-political one.
    *   Arbitrariness: If judges decide cases based on “social values” rather than strict legal text, the law becomes unpredictable (violating the certainty required by the Rule of Law).

##### Evaluation

The idea that courts can become a super-legislature and violate the rule of law is questionable, especially since there is a fine line between judicial lawmaking and the functioning as a check. After all, all judicial decisions involve some sort of lawmaking – when courts interpret vague constitutional terms such as “equality”, they are necessarily giving content to abstract principles, while courts also routinely develop legal principles such as tort and contract.

So, if courts refuse to adjudicate on issues for fear they would be dabbling in “law-making”, it makes it very difficult for decisions of other branches to be checked. This then raises questions as to whether limits on government powers are being effectively enforced.

It is also unclear as to the boundaries of what constitutes “law-making”, and what should probably be regarded as within or outside the scope of the court’s rule – this makes it inevitable that reasonable people will disagree about where legitimate interpretation ends and impermissible lawmaking begins.

*   This uncertainty means that labelling judicial decisions as “lawmaking” is often a contested political judgment rather than an objective legal determination.

##### Overall, SG follows a thin conception

Nonetheless, the court’s general reluctance to consider “extra-legal arguments” or issues of “moral conscience” which are regarded as exclusively within the purview of the legislature, has not stopped it from ruling the acts of the Executive as unconstitutional – hence, the judiciary does still support the rule of law, though it is a thin one. For example in _Tan Seet Eng_, a match fixing case under the CLTPA, the court emphasised that the judiciary is tasked with ensuring that all arms of the government are subject to legal limits in accordance with the rule of law.

*   In _Tan Seet Eng_, the Court of Appeal held that detention under the CLTPA was unlawful because the match-fixing activities were executed abroad, and the Minister’s power under section 30 CLTPA was limited to activities that were detrimental to Singapore - interpreted as requiring a sufficient territorial nexus to Singapore.
*   However, this decision was ultimately overruled once the Minister amended the terms for detention to show how the activities indeed had harmful effects in Singapore.

##### Issues with the presumption of constitutionality - ROL

Adopting a presumption of constitutionality (like in _Taw Cheng Kong_, _Chee Siok Chin_) seems to raise rule of law issues. Under the rule of law, all powers should have legal limits (_Chng Suan Tze_). Imposing a strong presumption of constitutionality appears to undermine this principle of limiting government powers, as it places a significant burden on challengers to demonstrate unconstitutionality and creates a strong tilt in favour of upholding government action.

*   However, this approach can be understood within Singapore’s distinctive conception of the rule of law, which coexists comfortably with a prominent feature of the cultural substratum – an emphasis on communitarian over individualist values, including notions such as dialogue, tolerance, compromise and placing community above self. These values have modulated the court’s approach in ensuring the rule of law is upheld (Menon, 2016).
*   The presumption of constitutionality reflects a degree of trust in the democratic process and the elected branches to balance competing interests in a diverse society. At the same time, the rule of law remains essential in this context – As CJ Menon observed, the rule of law is particularly important in less homogenous societies like Singapore, as it makes expectations transparent and provides a common framework transcending individual moral beliefs.
*   This creates a tension – the presumption of constitutionality reflects communitarian deference to the political branches but may risk undermining the rule of law’s function in constraining government power and preventing arbitrary action. The balance struck is that courts maintain formal legality review (as in _Tan Seet Eng_) while being reluctant to second-guess substantive policy choices reflected in legislation. Whether this adequately safeguards the rule of law depends on whether one accepts a thin (formal legality) or thick (substantive justice) conception of the rule of law.

##### Tension between checks and efficiency

In SG, the rule of law is a qualified, as opposed to an absolute, virtue (Menon CJ, _The Path to Exceptionalism_). This pulsates the strongest in the inherent tension between law being used a as a tool of effective governance, and being used as a form of restraint to ensure accountability. In Singapore, the balance has been struck in favour of a need to be “effective, efficient and speedy” due to the size and nature of its society (Shanmugam, Speech at the NY State Bar).

*   While adherence to the rule of law is important to avoid abuses of state power, one must “temper doctrinaire logic with practical wisdom”, and accept that certain trade-offs must be made in important areas such as national security instead of adhering inflexibly to the ordinary processes of the law, no matter the exigencies of the situation (Shanmugam, _The Rule of Law_).
*   To me – looks like this coheres exactly with what they propound exactly in _Tan Seng Kee_, demarcate the role of judiciary and parliament, judiciary _does review_, just not extra-legal questions – thus it still maintains the thin rule of law. Follow _Tan Seet Eng_ – the judiciary is tasked with ensuring that all arms of the government are subject to legal limits in accordance with the rule of law.

## Separation of Powers

Separation of powers is one of the most fundamental structural principles in constitutional law. Separation of powers intersects with virtually every constitutional question we encounter, from the legitimacy of institutional arrangements to the validity of legislative amendments, to the proper scope of judicial review.

### Foundational Theory and Function

The SOP doctrine originates in political theory associated with Locke and Montesquieu, though the specific institutional form varies across jurisdictions.

SOP at its core divides government authority among three branches of legislative, executive and judicial, with each branch performing distinct functions. The legislature makes laws, the executive implements and executes laws, while the judiciary interprets and applies the constitution and other laws.

However, SOP is not merely about division of labour or functional specialisation. Its deeper purpose is to create a system of checks and balances that prevents any single branch from accumulating excessive power. Each institution possesses the capacity to check the other’s exercises of authority. The judiciary reviews legislative and executive action for constitutional compliance. The legislature holds the executive accountable through parliamentary processes and can pass laws constraining executive action. The executive implements laws but cannot exceed its delegated authority.

This mutual checking serves the ultimate goal of preventing tyranny. When power is distributed across multiple institutions with overlapping oversight responsibilities, no single entity can dominate.

*   The ambition of one branch checks the ambition of others.
*   This follows the Madisonian insight that “ambition must be made to counteract ambition”.

### SOP in Singapore’s Constitution

All Constitutions based on the Westminster model incorporate the principle of SOP as part of their constitutional structure in order to diffuse state power among different organs of state (_Mohammad Faizal_ at \[12\]).

*   This is to prevent the concentration of power in a single individual or institution.

The principle of SOP, though it is not expressly stated in the SG constitution, is a part of the basic structure of the Singapore Constitution, given that Singapore’s constitution is based on the Westminster model, where SOP is a universal characteristic (_Mohammad Faizal_).

*   The purpose of this arrangement is to diffuse state power across different institutions, preventing any single branch from accumulating unchecked authority.

SOP is inherent in Singapore’s constitution (_Tan Seet Eng_).

*   Art. 38 vests legislative power of Singapore in the Legislature – to make laws;
*   Art. 23(1) vests executive power in the President (and is exercisable by the Cabinet or any Minister authorised by the Cabinet) – governing the country according to those laws;
*   Art. 93 vests judicial power in courts – the responsibility for adjudicating individual cases by pronouncing on the meaning of the laws.

At \[15\] of _Mohammad Faizal_, the courts noted that Art. 4’s use of “any law inconsistent with this constitution” rather than “any provision” reinforces that Parliament may not enact laws and government may not take actions inconsistent with SOP to the extent embodied in the Constitution.

*   This broader language includes not just the express provisions, but the Constitution as a whole. This encompasses the Constitution’s basic structure and fundamental principle, including principles that are implied rather than explicitly stated.
*   The Constitution protects more than just its text - it protects its basic structure and organizing principles.

#### Nature of Judicial Power

At \[16\] of _Mohammad Faizal_, the court distinguished Singapore from the UK: While UK judicial power vests in courts either at common law or by statute, Singapore’s judicial power is vested by Art 93 in the Supreme Court and subordinate courts. The Supreme Court’s judicial power is constitutional in nature, derived directly from Art 93, making it co-equal in constitutional status with legislative and executive power, subject only to limitations expressed in the Constitution. By contrast, judicial power of subordinate courts is statutory.

### Fusion between legislature and executive

Singapore operates under the Westminster model. Thus, there is no strict separation between the legislature and executive and it is characterised by the fusion of the two branches.

The executive is drawn from the legislature. Members of Cabinet are simultaneously members of Parliament. The Prime Minister leads both the government (executive) and commands majority support in Parliament (legislature). This fusion creates an executive that typically dominates Parliament, particularly when the governing party holds a substantial majority.

*   Because executive and legislative branches overlap substantially in personnel and leadership, the **primary separation exists between these fused political branches on one side and the independent judiciary on the other**. The judiciary stands as the principal institutional check on the political branches’ exercise of power.
*   There is a degree of shared responsibility in policymaking and governance – the executive proposes and implements policies, but the legislature scrutinises, amends and approves them.

This fusion heightens the importance of opposition representation in Parliament. In Westminster systems with fusion, parliamentary checks on executive power largely depend on opposition MPs who are not part of the government. Without robust opposition representation, the risk of executive dominance or executive aggrandizement increases substantially.

#### Constitutional Provision Reflecting Fusion

As per Art. 25(1) of the Constitution, the President shall, acting in accordance with the advice of the Prime Minister, appoint other Minsters from among the Members of Parliament to form the cabinet.

*   This is contrasted with the strict separation of powers in presidential systems like the USA, where Cabinet secretaries are appointed separately by the President; the President is also separately elected from Congress.
*   There is a degree of shared responsibility in policymaking and governance – the executive proposes and implements policies, but the legislature scrutinises, amends and approves them.

#### Constitutionalism within the original Westminster Design

Parliament keeps the executive accountable through question time, no confidence votes and Parliamentary committees.

Subject to the provisions of the Constitution, the Cabinet has the general direction and control of the government but is collectively responsible to Parliament, Art. 24(2).

*   It enhances accountability, as the government is directly responsible to the legislature and can be removed from power if it loses its confidence.
*   It can lead to more efficient and responsive government, as the executive and legislature are closely aligned and can work together more effectively.

Following Art. 26(1)(b), if the majority of members of parliament lose faith in the Prime Minister, the President is allowed to declare the office of the Prime Minister vacant – this gives Parliament a powerful tool to control the Executive.

#### Fusion weakens Parliamentary Checks

However, the fusion of powers limits Parliament’s ability to check the Executive:

*   Fusion of powers potentially weakens checks and balances, as the close relationship between the executive and legislature may reduce scrutiny and oversight.
*   Can lead to a concentration of power in the executive branch, especially if the government has a strong majority in parliament.
*   The fusion of powers can make it difficult for individual members of parliament to act independently or criticize the government, especially if they belong to the governing party (anti-hopping provision and party whip).

Cherian George provides an empirically grounded assessment of what this fusion looks like in practice: while “Singapore upholds the formal separation of powers — among the legislature, the executive and the judiciary — to prevent abuse by any one arm of the state,” in practice “politics has been characterised by an extraordinarily powerful executive, in the form of an inner cabinet.” Parliament is dominated by a ruling party whose leadership is composed of cabinet ministers; significant political decisions are not subject to judicial review and instead remain at ministers’ discretion; and non-state institutions that serve as watchdogs in other countries “such as the press and academe, are relegated to subordinate positions” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_) – this **contradicts Thio’s argument** that in Singapore’s model of constitutionalism, the trust in government and following “soft” constitutional laws is justified through the weight given to **constraining the government through the political process, _including_ the _free press_** (Thio, Treatise) . The aggregate effect is a system in which the three-way formal separation is substantially compressed into a binary: the fused legislature-executive on one side, and the judiciary on the other. This is why the judiciary’s independence bears disproportionate constitutional weight in Singapore’s SOP framework — it is structurally the only remaining institutional check on a fused political branch with a supermajority track record.

### Extra-Legal Arguments – what are the institutional boundaries?

_Lim Meng Suang_ presented the SGCA with the question of whether courts may consider extra-legal arguments when adjudicating constitutional challenges.

*   Extra-legal arguments encompass philosophical contentions such as claims that particular govt. actions facilitates tyranny of the majority, empirical evidence from social sciences (such as tudies demonstrating increasing societal acceptance of previously stigmatised groups) and normative assertions grounded in moral theory rather than positive law.

The appellant in _Lim Meng Suang_ challenged s 377A PC as an unconstitutional violation of equality principles, supporting the legal argument with extra-legal contentions.

*   They argued that societal attitudes homosexuality had evolved towards greater acceptance, and scientific research has established sexual orientation as an immutable characteristic rather than chosen behaviour.
*   The court definitively rejected consideration of such extra-legal material, reasoning from a separation of powers principle that extra-legal considerations are inherently political and therefore fall within legislative purview, not judicial competence.

#### What does Lim Meng Suan mean for SOP?

The SGCA’s position in _Lim Meng Suang_ reflects a formalistic separation of powers conception, emphasizing distinct institutional roles and rigid jurisdictional boundaries.

*   If the judicial role comprises solely adjudication of legal questions and interpretation of legal texts, then extra-legal material – which by definition falls outside legal sources – exceeds judicial institutional competence.

The court then acknowledged that if considering extra-legal arguments became “absolutely necessary” for applying relevant legal principles, such consideration might occur to that limited extent, but this narrow exception preserves the general rule excluding extra-legal material from judicial analysis.

*   This approach maintains clear separation between judicial and legislative functions – courts decide legal questions using legal materials and methods, while legislatures address political questions using political processes including consideration of philosophical arguments, empirical social science, and normative moral claims.

##### For – Judicial exclusion is justified

The strongest argument supporting judicial exclusion of extra-legal considerations emphasises the slippery slope risk.

*   If courts may consider extra-legal arguments, what principled limits constrain such consideration? How much weight should philosophical contentions receive, relative to constitutional text?
*   May courts rely primarily on empirical social science, using constitutional provisions merely as frameworks for applying social scientific findings? Could judicial decision making become 80% extra-legal analysis, 20% legal interpretation, or even reverse the proportions entirely?

Without clear boundaries, permitting any extra-legal consideration potentially transforms adjudication into free-ranging policy analysis indistinguishable from legislative deliberation. Judges would effectively make law based on their assessment of philosophical arguments and empirical evidence rather than interpreting existing legal materials, violating the _fundamental premise that courts interpret law rather than create it_.

Moreover, judges lack expertise in philosophical argumentation and empirical social science. When presented with competing philosophical frameworks or conflicting social scientific studies, judges possess no special qualification to evaluate which philosophical position proves more compelling or which empirical methodology more sound.

*   Accepting extra-legal arguments therefore requires judges to make determinations outside their institutional competence, potentially producing decisions grounded in amateur philosophy or misunderstood social science.
*   The alternative – accepting expert testimony on philosophical and empirical questions merely displaces the problem. Judges must still evaluate the competing expert claims without expertise enabling informed evaluation.
*   On the other hand, legislative process can incorporate expert input through committee hearings, commissioned research and deliberative procedures designed to synthesize diverse expert perspectives into policy judgements.

The fact that Parliament is vulnerable to the political check provides additional support for exclusion. In representative democracy grounded on popular sovereignty, elected legislators answer to voters who may remove them if dissatisfied with legislative outputs, _including_ being unhappy with constitutional amendments and ordinary legislation.

*   This electoral accountability mechanism constrains legislative consideration of philosophical and empirical arguments – legislators face political consequences for adoption positions voters reject. Judges on the other hand enjoy insulation from political pressure through the security of tenure provided, precisely to enable independent legal judgement unconstrained by popular opinion. If judges rely on extra-legal arguments including philosophical contentions about justice or empirical claims about social attituded, they are making essentially political judgements without political accountability.
*   From this perspective, SOP demands that politically-laden questions requiring extra-legal analysis remain in the politically accountable legislative branch rather than migrating to the insulated judiciary.
*   So does this mean this SOP is justified as it prevents tyranny by the judiciary for example? Making sure the judiciary does not go unchecked.

##### Counter – Judicial exclusion is not justified

Courts already routinely admit expert evidence on technical questions, ranging from medical causation in tort cases to economic analysis in antitrust legislation and scientific validity in patent disputes.

*   Expert testimony on empirical questions, including social scientific research on societal attitudes or psychological studies on immutability of characteristics, fits comfortably within existing evidentiary frameworks.
*   If courts can evaluate competing expert claims about medical causation or economic effects, the institutional competence argument for excluding expert social scientific testimony proves unpersuasive.
*   So, the key distinction might involve **how** extra-legal material (related to these social matters) enters judicial consideration – if expert testimony is subject to cross examination and evidentiary standards rather than as free-standing philosophical arguments advanced by parties or accepted by courts without evidentiary foundation.
*   When contained within expert evidence procedures, extra-legal empirical material could supplement legal analysis without expanding the judicial role inappropriately.

The purported distinction between and extra-legal reasoning proves less sharp than exclusionary approaches assume. Legal interpretation necessarily involves philosophical judgements about meaning, purpose and value.
