When courts interpret equality provisions for instance, they inevitably confront philosophical questions such as what equality means and what entails equal treatment.

*   These kinds of questions admit no purely legal answers derived mechanically from constitutional text, and instead require philosophical analysis of equality’s nature and demands.
*   These provisions (such as the equality provisions) embody philosophical commitments that the courts must understand philosophically to apply sensibly. For example, the underlying philosophical framework explaining why equality matters (to prevent tyranny of the majority) necessarily informs interpretation of what equality requires.

Excluding philosophical extra-legal arguments artificially therefore proves either impossible (since courts engage inevitably with philosophical questions) or pernicious (courts engage philosophy without acknowledging they do so, preventing transparent evaluation of their philosophical premises).

*   This creates a **rule of law problem**. The thin rule of law requires that legal reasoning be transparent, reasoned and publicly justifiable, not just a decision that is reached, but the grounds for it be articulable and open to scrutiny. When courts smuggle in philosophical premises without acknowledgment, they violate this requirement because the actual basis for the decision is hidden. Litigants, scholars, and the public cannot evaluate, critique, or predict outcomes based on reasoning that is not surfaced.

Vigorous separation of powers requires robust judicial checking of legislative and executive action, not merely formal adjudication staying strictly within a narrowly-defined lane.

*   Note – but since SG follows a formalistic SOP, this is technically “fine”.
*   If courts function as constitutional guardians ensuring government respects constitutional limits, they must be willing to examine whether governmental action genuinely serves legitimate purposes or instead violates constitutional principles and values.
*   Such examination may require considering whether legislative justifications withstand scrutiny given current empirical evidence and philosophical understanding. A legislature might defend discriminatory legislation by claiming immutable social attitudes demand such discrimination, yet empirical evidence demonstrates attitudes have shifted. Should courts ignore that evidence when evaluating whether the legislation serves legitimate governmental purposes?
*   From this perspective, excluding extra-legal evidence hamstrings judicial checking function, permitting government to offer pretextual justifications for unconstitutional action that courts cannot effectively scrutinize because relevant evidence falls outside “legal” materials.

##### Evaluation – so what?

Simply put – this debate has no conclusive resolution because it reflects a deeper disagreement about the appropriate judicial role within SOP frameworks.

Shah: Recognise this fundamental contestability while developing clear analytical positions. When evaluating whether courts should consider extra-legal arguments, the analysis should proceed through several steps.

1.  What counts as “extra-legal” – empirical social science only, or also philosophical reasoning?
2.  Identify potential limiting principles – expert testimony framework, relevance to legal question actually presented, prohibition on arguments completely displacing legal analysis.
3.  Connect the position to broader SOP theory – does the judicial role primarily involve interpretations staying within legal materials, or does checking of the legislature by the judiciary require engagement with the full range of relevant considerations by the legislature?
4.  Acknowledge competing values and arguments rather than pretending one position obviously prevails.

Recognise the debate’s complexity, articulate competing positions fairly, and defend their evaluative conclusion with explicit reference to SOP theory rather than asserting one position as self-evidentially correct.

The current position seems to be that courts follow a mainly formalistic position – hence the conception of SOP as a deference.

*   Thus, extra-legal arguments are still excluded except when it is absolutely necessary for applying legal principles. This once again reflects the coherent SOP vision emphasising distinct institutional roles, judicial restraint and channelling political questions to politically accountable institutions.
*   There is a debate between whether this vision optimally serves constitutional supremacy and constitutionalism’s limiting function, or instead unduly constrains judicial capacity to check governmental overreach – this is open to reasoned disagreement.

### Overall, SG has a Formalistic Constitutional Framework

Given how SG follows a formalistic, thin RoL and a deferential formalistic SOP, combined with the judicial role, it reveals a consistent formalistic orientation across interconnected constitutional principles.

*   Formalism in constitutional interpretation and application emphasises adherence to explicit textual provisions, procedural regularity, and institutional boundaries rather than pursuing substantive justice, philosophical principles, or vigorous checking of governmental power.
*   This formalistic approach manifests distinctly in each constitutional domain while reflecting the underlying philosophy about the appropriate judicial role and constitutional function in SG’s political system.

### Three Conceptions of SOP

There are three competing conceptions as to how SOP should operate.

*   The relationship between SOP and fundamental liberties is a central tension in Singapore. The way courts conceptualise SOP directly determines the scope and robustness of judicial protection afforded to individual rights guaranteed under Part IV of the Constitution.
*   Overall, case law has shown there are three broad conceptions of SOP. SOP as control (the judiciary actively checks other branches), SOP as autonomy (each branch operates independently within its own sphere without intruding upon the others) and SOP as deference (the judiciary restrains itself from interfering with legislative and executive decisions, particularly on policy matters).
*   TLDR – we now are in a position where while case law has had the dominant posture of deference, more recent jurisprudence has shown that there is a movement towards the conception of SOP as autonomy and even with hints of SOP as control at some times. The key challenge for SG courts is to maintain their institutional autonomy and their checking function without overreaching into policymaking sphere.

#### SOP as Control (Functionalist)

This is associated with constitutional supremacy. Someone, or something needs to maintain constitutional supremacy – and that someone is the judiciary through judicial review.

There must be an institution to “enforce” the constitution. The conception of SOP as control focuses on the functions of each branch, particularly the function of the judiciary as an effective check on the powers exercised by the other 2 branches of government.

*   Robust conceptions prioritize judicial capacity to scrutinize legislative and executive action for constitutional compliance, requiring judicial willingness to examine substance of governmental decisions rather than merely verifying procedural compliance.
*   From the robust perspective, courts serve as constitutional guardians actively checking governmental overreach, necessitating consideration of whatever evidence or arguments prove relevant to determining constitutional compliance.
*   This coheres with the principle of constitutional supremacy enshrined in Art. 4 of the Constitution, which provides that any law inconsistent with the Constitution is void to the extent of the inconsistency. If constitutional supremacy is to be meaningful, there must be an institution empowered to enforce it – and that is the judiciary.

#### SOP as Autonomy

SOP as autonomy focuses on the division of functions, where each branch has a “corresponding identifiable function”, and is confined to the exercise of its own functions, with no branch allowed to encroach onto the functions of the other. Each branch is hence separate and co-equal, and no single branch has absolute power. It is on this basis that judicial power is exclusive and cannot be transferred to other branches of government (Neo, _Autonomy, Defence and Control_ at 464).

#### SOP as Deference (Formalist)

SOP as Deference is a formalist conception that the branches of the government have their own, exclusive sphere of power; the judiciary should defer to the legislature and executive in certain decision-making matters where those matters are within the expertise/competence of the other branches. Deference is often justified either based on expertise, or democratic legitimacy.

*   In other words, this emphasises distinct institutional roles and strict jurisdictional boundaries.
*   Each branch possesses defined constitutional functions – legislature makes law, executive implements law, judiciary interprets and applies the law.
*   _Lim Meng Suang_’s exclusion of extra-legal arguments follows directly from this formalistic vision. Extra-legal considerations such as philosophical arguments and empirical social science are political matters falling within legislative competence. Courts are confined to legal interpretation and adjudication and must not stray into political terrain by weighing philosophical contentions or assessing empirical evidence about social attitudes. Such encroachment violates separation of powers by usurping legislative function.

This posits that the Judiciary should defer to the Legislature and the Executive in realisation of the separation of powers. Deference refers to the idea that the acts of the decision maker “should command respect because of the differentiation of functions and institutions” (_Dunsmuir v New Brunswick_). This underpins the presumption of constitutionality and legality in Singapore (Neo, _Autonomy, Defence and Control_ at 464).

*   Neo notes that deference is not monolithic but exists on a spectrum. She distinguishes between “minimal deference” where courts assign some presumptive weight to decisions of elected bodies without it being a strong presumption, and “substantial deference,” warranted only where judges themselves suffer from particular institutional shortcomings in competence or legitimacy (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 465). She further draws on Dyzenhaus's distinction between deference as “submission,” where judges suspend their own judgment entirely in favour of another branch, and deference as “respect,” where courts give weight to legislative opinion but reach their own independent conclusion on. This distinction matters because Singapore's presumption of constitutionality, prior to _Jolovan Wham_, arguably operated closer to deference as submission — courts did not merely give weight to Parliament's judgment but effectively treated it as presumptively correct, placing the full burden on the challenger.
*   Deference unpins the presumption of constitutionality and legality, as it means the judiciary accepts that legislative and executive decisions are made within their sphere of competence and that they should be treated as valid unless proven otherwise. If the judiciary were to genuinely defer to the democratic legitimacy and expertise of the other branches, it cannot approach their decisions with scepticism or neutrality – they must approach it from a position of respect and thus, there is a presumption of legality and constitutionality.

So, this formalistic conception contrasts with the robust (functional) SOP emphasising judicial checking of governmental power as the core constitutional function.

*   The judicial role is confined to its narrow adjudicative and interpretive functions, treating vigorous checking as inappropriate judicial activism exceeding proper institutional boundaries.

##### SG’s RoL supports the formalistic Separation of Powers

SG Courts explicitly connect the formalistic separation of powers to rule of law values. In _Yong Vui Kong_, the SGCA warned that “where the courts act as a super-legislature and enact their personal views as to what is just and desirable into law, then the judges too could violate the rule of law”.

*   This reflects how the formalistic rule of law conception reinforces why we need a formalistic SOP. If rule of law means stability, predictability and certainty in legal standards, then judicial overreach into legislative territory undermines these values by introducing instability (where courts constantly second-guess legislative judgements), unpredictability (uncertain when courts will intervene) and uncertainty (unclear what legal standards courts will impose based on their philosophical or policy preferences).
*   Basically, formalistic RoL reinforces why we need a formalistic separation of powers. Rule of law creates the normative pressure that makes separation of powers here necessary and justifiable. rule of law gives you the reason to insist on separation of powers. Without that underlying value commitment, separation of powers would just be a formal constitutional rule with no deeper justification. Rule of law is what explains why you should care about maintaining it. Each branch staying within its lane, such as respecting jurisdictional boundaries defined by formal constitutional role allocations, promotes legal certainty and institutional predictability that formalistic rule of law values.
*   If you’re committed to rule of law — meaning you want law to be stable, predictable, certain — then you need to ask: what institutional arrangement best produces those qualities? The answer is: one where law is made by a fixed, identifiable body (the legislature) through a known process, and courts simply apply what that body enacts. That way, the content of the law is anchored to something external and determinate — the enacted text — rather than floating with whoever is currently interpreting it.
*   So rule of law generates a demand for that kind of institutional structure. It tells you: you must have a body whose job is just to make law and another whose job is just to apply it, because if you collapse those roles, you lose the very stability and certainty rule of law requires. Courts making law as they go is incompatible with rule of law because it means the standard you’re held to depends on the views of the judges you happen to appear before
*   Conversely, judicial willingness to scrutinise legislative substantive policy judgements, second-guessing executive political decisions or considering extra-legal arguments introduces judicial discretion and reduces predictability.
*   From a formalistic perspective, such discretionary power exercised by unelected judges threatens RoL more than governmental action following prescribed legal processes even if substantively problematic.
*   The formalistic framework treats procedural regularity and institutional boundary-respect as paramount RoL values, while sceptical of substantive review as a potential source of arbitrary judicial power.

#### Advantages of Formalistic Approach

The primary advantage of a formalistic framework is predictability and institutional clarity. When constitutional principles reduce to formal requirements – following procedures, respecting institutional boundaries and applying laws equally, governmental actors and citizens alike can predict legal consequences with reasonable confidence.

*   Ambiguity and contestation decrease.
*   The legal system achieves stability through clear rules about what constitutes compliance. For a nation emphasising legal certainty to attract investment, a formalistic RoL proves particularly valuable by ensuring commercial actors can rely on predictable legal frameworks.

Additionally, formalism arguably suits Westminster parliamentary democracies with executive-legislative fusion, where the governing party typically commands parliamentary majority.

*   Here, parliamentary checks on the executive prove weak because majority of MPs support executive leadership through party discipline such as the party whip. If judicial checks are equally constrained by a formalistic SoP limiting courts to a narrow interpretive role, governmental power faces minimal effective constraint.
*   The formalistic framework arguably acknowledges this reality by channelling accountability primarily through electoral mechanisms – the political check where voters replace governments at elections – rather than pretending robust institutional checks exist when fusion undermines their effectiveness.
    *   This to me seems to suggests that the formalistic framework acknowledges that there are weak checks within the branches of the government itself – the judiciary’s power is limited, but this is ok to maintain effectiveness, and either way, there _is indeed a political check: elections_.
    *   So, formalism is actually _honest_ about how power works in Westminster systems. So if you then also have a substantive, interventionist judiciary, you’re not actually adding a meaningful check, you’re just introducing unpredictability and uncertainty into the system, which undermines rule of law values, without the compensating benefit of genuine accountability.

#### Problems with Formalistic Approach (Deference)

Just like how Singapore follows a formalistic rule of law, Singapore follows a formalistic approach to SoP. However, the formalistic approach is not without its problems.

##### Exclusivity is difficult to achieve in practice

One cannot neatly separate the functions of the different branches. There are necessarily some overlaps between each other.

*   E.g. the court may have to engage in some law-making when they interpret the law. This can lead to disputes over jurisdiction and authority.
*   Within the government, competition between the branches including departments and agencies for power and influence can create tension and conflict, hindering effective governance.

##### Will of the majority

The formalistic conception over-emphasizes enabling the will of the majority (through deference to the democratic legislature) and under-emphasizes protecting individuals against that majority will – which is the core function of fundamental liberties.

*   Allan offers a particularly forceful critique of deference as a constitutional doctrine. He argues that a doctrine of deference is “empty” if it purports to implement the separation of powers, because the proper application of legal principles already independently secures institutional boundaries. More damningly, he contends that deference becomes “pernicious” if it permits the judiciary to abdicate its responsibility in favour of reliance on the good faith or expertise of public officials, whose judgments about the implications of rights in specific cases may well be wrong (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 466, citing Allan). In other words, invoking separation of powers and subsidiary claims of institutional competence or democratic legitimacy can operate as an assertion of, rather than a justification for, judicial non-intervention — the court announces that it will not review, but does not explain why the particular circumstances warrant restraint (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 467).

##### Gridlock and Inefficiency

**Coordination failures**

Institutional fragmentation – different branches operate independently with separate spheres of competence, leading to siloed policy-making that lacks holistic, cross-cutting approaches to complex problems requiring integrated solutions.

Policy Incoherence – the absence of a single coordinating authority can produce fragmented and inconsistent policies as different branches pursue their own institutional agendas and priorities without adequate alignment.

**Deliberative costs**

Delayed decision-making: The need for inter-branch consensus, negotiation, and cooperation slows the legislative and policy-making process, making it difficult to respond swiftly to urgent crises or time-sensitive issues.

Democratic gridlock: When different branches are controlled by opposing political parties or factions, institutional separation can facilitate deadlock and inaction, with each side exploiting constitutional checks to block the other’s initiatives for partisan advantage rather than substantive policy reasons.

**Accountability diffusion**

Blurred responsibility: When authority and decision-making power are distributed across multiple branches, it becomes difficult to pinpoint who bears ultimate accountability for policy failures, inaction, or adverse outcomes—each branch can deflect blame to the others.

#### How to critique formalism?

Recognise formalism as a unifying framework. When courts or government officials invoke rule of law, separation of powers or constitutional principles, it is important to understand the formalistic interpretation of these principles in Singapore’s context to accurately analyse what the invocations actually mean and what limitations they impose.

Formalism represents one coherent constitutional philosophy amongst several possibilities, and neither are self-evidently correct nor demonstrably wrong, but are defensible through particular values (such as certainty, stability, institutional clarity) while vulnerable to particular critiques (inadequate substantive protection, insufficient checking). Evaluate the tradeoffs.

#### Deference 🡪 Control

The judicial attitude in Singapore has shifted from the conceptualisation of SOP as deference to that of control. This can be seen in the disavowal of the presumption of constitutionality in _Jolovan Wham_, where the SGCA held that the presumption of constitutionality was no more than a starting point.

Further, a modest “sea change” in the judicial attitudes of the courts vis-à-vis judicial review has been observed in Singapore, particularly since Chan CJ’s bench. Under this “third wave” of communitarian judicial review in Singapore, Courts have demonstrated a culture of “elaborated reason-giving, as opposed to the statist, cursory judgements of a former age”. Courts have displayed a greater willingness to engage in international legal arguments and academic opinions (_Thio, Principled Pragmatism_) and it is suggested that this may lead to greater control and scrutiny over other governmental branches.

### What is the Judicial Attitude towards SOP?

#### Initial – SOP as Control

Under the functionalist approach of SOP as control, the judiciary’s role is understood as going beyond the mere interpretation of statutes. The court is an institution whose constitutional function is to supervise and constrain the exercise of power by the other branches, particularly where fundamental liberties are at stake.

*   This coheres with the principle of constitutional supremacy enshrined in Art. 4 of the Constitution, which provides that any law inconsistent with the Constitution is void to the extent of the inconsistency. If constitutional supremacy is to be meaningful, there must be an institution empowered to enforce it – and that is the judiciary.

In _Ong Ah Chuan_ \[1981\], the UKPC opined that Part IV of the Constitution, which guarantees fundamental liberties, should be given a “generous interpretation” so as to afford individuals the full measure of the rights enshrined therein. Conversely, any restrictions on those liberties were to be construed narrowly and restrictively.

1.  This establishes an interpretive principle that favours the individual over the state when fundamental rights are in contest.
2.  It coheres with a constitutional supremacy scheme – if fundamental liberties form the core of the Constitution, and if any law inconsistent with the Constitution is void, then the judiciary must adopt a posture that is vigilant in scrutinising legislation and executive action for consistency with those liberties.

The generous interpretation from _Ong Ah Chuan_ thus ensures that the protective scope of Part IV is not hollowed out by restrictive judicial readings.

#### Chng Suan Tze asserts the conception of SOP as control

_Chng Suan Tze_ is another case that asserts the conception of SOP as control. The SGCA held that the objective test should be applied to review the exercise of discretion under s 8 and 10 of the ISA. This meant the court would independently assess whether there existed objective grounds justifying the exercise of the detention power, rather than merely accepting the Executive’s subjective assertion that such grounds existed.

The court justified this conclusion on two principal bases:

1.  The rule of law and judicial function: The court reasoned that the boundaries of a decision-maker’s jurisdiction, as conferred by an Act of Parliament, are a question solely for the courts to decide. The notion of a subjective or unfettered discretion is fundamentally contrary to the rule of law, which demands that all power, however broadly conferred, has legal limits, and that the courts must be able to examine the exercise of discretionary power. If a discretionary power were truly beyond judicial review, it would in effect be arbitrary, regardless of the ostensible statutory constraints on its exercise.
2.  The protection of fundamental rights: s 8 and 10 of the ISA are exceptions to the fundamental rights guaranteed by Art. 9, 13 and 14 of the Constitution. As exceptions to constitutional rights, they should be narrowly construed so as to derogate as little as possible from those fundamental freedoms. The court reasoned that these provisions would only be consistent with Art. 12 (equality) and Art. 93 (judicial power) if the powers they confer do not authorise arbitrary detention, and if the courts can review the exercise of those powers. The court warned that if the discretion was not subject to judicial review, it would in actual fact be arbitrary as if the provisions themselves imposed no restrictions on its exercise, and to suggest otherwise would be naïve.

_Chng Suan Tze_ is a significant case as it establishes the principle that even in the sensitive domain of national security, the Executive’s exercise of detention powers is subject to objective judicial scrutiny. It is a clear example of the Singapore judiciary adopting a functionalist, checking posture vis-à-vis the other branches.

*   However, the political aftermath of **_Chng Suan Tze_** illustrates the institutional risks courts face when asserting the control conception in a dominant-party state. Neo documents that after the Court of Appeal invalidated the detention orders, the executive re-issued the orders and detained the same persons shortly after their release. Parliament then amended both the Constitution and the relevant statute to oust judicial review of future detention orders except for procedural compliance (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 481). It took 27 years before the Court of Appeal again invalidated executive detention orders, in _Tan Seet Eng_. Significantly, there was no comparable political backlash to _Tan Seet Eng_ — the government revised its detention orders to comply with the Court's interpretation and even released other detainees on the same basis (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 481). This contrast **may signal a tentative evolution in the constitutional culture governing the relationship between the political branches and the judiciary**.

_Tan Seet Eng_ \[2016\] SGCA also reaffirms the principles established in _Chng Suan Tze_ (although arising in the context of the CLPTA). The SGCA held that the courts are the final arbiters of the lawfulness of all actions, including government actions. This means the judiciary possesses both the authority and the responsibility to determine the boundaries of the power vested in the Executive, and to assess whether the Executive has acted within the ambit of that power. This principle reflects the core of SoP as control – the judiciary does not merely interpret statutes in the abstract, but actively polices the boundaries of executive and legislative authority.

The SGCA in _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2011\] SCGA also offered a rare statement at \[31\] that aligns with SOP as control. The court observed that “\[i\]n any modern State whose fundamental law is a written Constitution based on the doctrine of separation of powers, i.e. where the judicial power is vested in an independent judiciary, there will or should be few, if any, legal disputes between the State and the people from which the judicial power is excluded”.

*   This accords with the functionalist understanding of what SoP is – the judiciary’s role is to serve as a check on the other branches, and the exclusion of legal disputes from judicial cognisance should be the _rare exception_ rather than the norm. At the very least, it reflects an adoption of SOP as autonomy, affirming that the judicial power is co-equal and independently significant.

#### Historically, SG still favours a conception of SOP as Deference

Under this approach, the courts adopt a restrained posture, giving substantial latitude to the legislature and executive, particularly on matters deemed to be of a policy, socio-political or polycentric nature. This conception gives the legislature a greater role in defining and protecting fundamental liberties, and correspondingly reduces the judiciary’s willingness to intervene. Several key themes and cases illustrate this.

##### Textualism and Legislative Primacy in Constitutional Interpretation

In _Rajeevan_ \[1998\], the SGHC adopted a strictly textualist approach to constitutional interpretation. The SGHC opined that the judiciary’s role is limited to ensuring that Parliament’s intentions are adhered to; the courts could not expand the scope of legislation or imply into it their own interpretation contrary to Parliament’s intent.

*   This reflects a formalist conception of separation of powers: the primary role of making laws belongs to the legislature, not the judiciary. Any proposition to broaden the scope of rights falls within the legislature’s power alone.
*   Bring in week 4 stuff later for constitutional interpretation to improve this point!

In the 2015 iteration of _Yong Vui Kong_, the SGCA held that the common law prohibition of torture would be entitled to no greater weight than any other law. Any inconsistency between common law and statute should be resolved by applying principles of statutory interpretation.

*   This approach strictly confines the role of the courts to interpreting the laws laid down by Parliament and resists any suggestion that the court might elevate certain common law norms to a supra-legislative status. The court also expressed concern about the judiciary becoming a “super-legislature”, a characterisation that reflects the courts’ commitment to legislative primacy.
*   In other words, the courts turn to statutory interpretation and discerning Parliamentary intent when looking to resolve an inconsistency between common law and statute. In other words, the court turns to Parliament for guidance on what they intended and wanted, they refuse to make law and defer to Parliament for it. Similarly, the court in _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] is trying to make sure Parliament’s intentions are adhered to.

##### Courts are reluctant to control the executive through judicial review

Courts have generally been reluctant to intervene via judicial review where broad discretionary powers have been conferred upon the Executive. The cases that mainly illustrate this is _Vellama_ \[2013\] SGCA and _Teo Ghim Heng_ \[2022\] SCGA.

In _Vellama_ \[2013\], the SGCA held that the timing of holding a by-election was a “polycentric” matter, and that the Prime Minister should accordingly be accorded a measure of latitude. Judicial intervention would only be warranted in exceptional cases. The court’s reasoning reflected the concern that matters involving multiple competing considerations – political, logistical and social, were better left to the elected branches of government.

*   Integrate what was said above about extra-legal arguments.

In _Teo Ghim Heng_, the court articulated a clear division of sentencing related functions. The legislature has the power to proscribe punishment, and the Executive has discretion to select the offence to charge, and the judicial power is limited to exercising sentencing discretion as conferred by the Statute.

*   This demarcation reinforces the view that the courts are bound within the framework established by the other branches, and the court is unwilling to intervene in this case, such as by charging the defendant under a different offence.

##### The (formerly) strong presumption of constitutionality reflects formalism

Until the case of _Jolovan Wham_ \[2021\] SGCA, there was a strong presumption of constitutionality applied by the courts. This presumption, articulated by _Chee Siok Chin_ \[2005\] SGHC and _Ramalingam_ \[2012\] SGHC, was rooted in the belief that the legislature and the executive possess democratic legitimacy, moral authority and greater institutional competence. It is an interpretive tool whereby courts _prima facie_ lean in favour of constitutionality and support impugned legislation if it is reasonable to do so (_Lim Meng Suang_).

*   In practical terms, it meant that any person challenging the constitutionality of a law bore a heavy burden to displace the presumption that Parliament had acted within its constitutional remit. This presumption significantly tilted the balance in favour of the state and made successful constitutional challenges rare.
*   The party attacking validity must place relevant materials and evidence before the court to show legislation was enacted arbitrarily or had operated arbitrarily. Unless the law is plainly arbitrary on its face, postulating examples of arbitrariness would ordinarily not be helpful in rebutting the presumption (_Taw Cheng Kong_).
*   This shows that the judiciary gives plenty of latitude to the Executive on their actions, with this presumption needing to be rebutted.

Though, note there are some justifications – separation of powers here may be informed by the socio-political-cultural context of trust in government, influenced by Confucian principles (_junzi_) regarding the moral authority of leaders and Parliament’s democratic legitimacy. Parliament knows best the needs of the people and legislates to address their problems (_Saravanan_).

*   The acts of high officials should be seen as carrying authority. Unless it can be shown that the exercise of power has been carried out unlawfully, acts of high officials should be accorded a presumption of legality, especially where acts are carried out in the exercise of constitutional powers (_Ramalingam_).

However, such a strong reliance on the presumption arguably affords too high a degree of power to the legislative branch. Art. 4 of the Constitution makes it clear that the Constitution is the supreme law of Singapore and should be regarded as such; and therefore all exercises of powers should be examined by the courts to ensure compliance with the Constitution, rather than operating on an assumption that the other branches of the legislative and executive are exercising their powers consistently and appropriately. As such, it is submitted that determining the constitutionality of a law should strictly come under the purview of the courts, rather than risking over-reliance on a mere presumption.

*   Neo observes that the presumption of constitutionality runs counter to the “culture of justification,” which posits that governments should be required to provide substantive justification for their actions and links legitimacy to the government's capacity to justify its exercises of power. Instead, the presumption reflects a “culture of authority,” where the legitimacy and legality of government action is based on the fact that the actor is authorised to act (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 476). This is particularly problematic in Singapore's context because decision-makers are not generally required to disclose reasons for their decisions (as Cherian Geroge points out, is an issue inherent in POFMA orders), meaning challengers must somehow obtain cogent evidence of unconstitutionality without freedom of information legislation granting such access. The evidential deck is thus structurally stacked against the applicant; Nonetheless, the SVWP’s suggestion that Singapore supports _junzi_ and trust in government might mitigate this.

##### Reluctance to Consider Extra-Legal Arguments and Socio-Political Questions

A recurring theme in the jurisprudence of SOP as deference is the judiciary’s reluctance to engage with arguments that it regards as extra-legal – arguments rooted in moral, social or political considerations rather tian in strictly legal or textual analysis.

*   The courts have consistently taken the position that such matters are exclusively within the purview of the legislature, which has both the democratic mandate and the institutional competence to address them.

In _Tan Seet Eng_ \[2015\] SGCA, the SGCA acknowledged that courts are not best equipped to scrutinise decisions concerning policy and security, which call for consideration of polycentric political conditions. Such matters were better left to the political branches.

In _Lim Meng Suang_ \[2014\] SGCA (concerning s 377A PC), the SGCA held that the court’s duty was **only to interpret statutes enacted by the legislature, not to amend or modify them**. The SGCA emphasised that it could not consider “extra-legal arguments” and could not usurp the legislative function or substitute its own views for those of Parliament, as doing so would violate SOP. The court stated it could not become a mini-legislature.

*   Importantly, the court adopted a somewhat cautious middle-path – it did not prohibit litigants from raising extra-legal arguments before the court, but made clear that the court would not make its decision on the basis of such arguments, as doing so would exceed the court’s proper role. The vindication of rights must be based on the constitutional text alone – a firmly formalist approach. These arguments are also not wholly irrelevant in all contexts, but are merely appropriate for a legislative debate, which is beyond the remit of the court.
*   All the court can be concerned with is whether any fundamental rights under the Singapore constitution has been violated, in situations where the arguments are on what is in substance a legislative divide and are intensely controversial, with the relevant empirical evidence ambiguous at best.
*   The courts _do consider_ extra-legal arguments. When the constitutionality of a statute is challenged under Art 9 or Art 12 of the Constitution, the court **_must_** consider extra-legal arguments (such as scientific evidence, social data, or empirical facts) because the nature of these provisions demands it. Art.s 9 and 12 are framed in broad, abstract terms such as “personal liberty” and “equal protection”, and to determine whether a statute violates them, the court needs to understand the real-world context of what the law does and who it affects. For example, under Art 12, assessing whether a legislative classification is reasonable or arbitrary may require looking beyond purely legal material. The court is therefore not choosing to entertain extra-legal considerations out of generosity; rather, it cannot properly apply Arts 9 and 12 without them.
*   However, the court will only engage with extra-legal material on a very limited basis — strictly to the extent necessary to resolve the constitutional question before it. It will not use the case as an occasion to make broad pronouncements on social policy or morality beyond what is needed to determine whether the statute is consistent with Arts 9 and 12.
*   If extra-legal material is needed to apply the legal test — for example, factual evidence showing whether or not a classification rationally connects to a legislative purpose — the court will consider it. But if the argument is broader than that — for instance, “s 377A is morally wrong,” “society has evolved,” or “this law causes emotional harm” — those are arguments about whether the law should exist as a matter of policy. They don’t help the court answer the specific legal question under Art 12, and so the court treats them as matters for Parliament.
    *   In other words, the court looks at the _reason_ Parliament allegedly had for drawing the distinction, and then asks whether the way Parliament sorted people actually makes logical sense in light of that reason. To answer that question, the court may need to look at extra-legal material — factual, scientific, or empirical evidence — because without it, the court cannot determine whether the link between the classification and the purpose is rational.

In _Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\] SGCA, the SGCA further developed the reasoning in _Lim Meng Suang_, advancing several justifications for why the political process is better suited than litigation for the resolution of moral and socio-political issues:

1.  Parliament, not the courts, is best placed to devise a pluralistic vision that accommodates divergent interests in society, this is unlike litigation which provides a black and white yes/no answer;
2.  The legislative process provides a forum for broader and more democratic deliberation than the adversarial structure of litigation;
3.  Issues touching on morality, social values and public policy involve many competing considerations and stakeholders, and should be debated and resolved by the many, not summarily adjudicated by a few judges.

Accordingly, the SGCA refused to consider the question of whether s 377A should be retained or repealed, or to pronounce upon the moral worth of homosexual individuals or the fundamental nature of sexual orientation. It deemed these to be extra-legal questions beyond the court’s purview, leaving such matters for the legislature to decide. The court limited itself to the strictly legal question of whether s 377A was inconsistent with the Constitution.

*   The court was clear that the only question within its remit was the legal one: is s 377A inconsistent with Arts 9, 12, or 14 of the Constitution? Whether or not it ultimately reached a conclusion on that question, that was the only question it was permitted to consider.
*   All the broader arguments — whether s 377A was morally wrong, whether society had evolved, whether the law caused suffering, whether Singapore should follow other countries in decriminalising homosexuality — were irrelevant to the court. Those are questions about whether the law should exist, and that’s Parliament’s business. The court’s sole task was the narrow legal inquiry of whether the law was constitutionally valid.

##### Does SG’s SOP as Deference Make Sense?

The court’s sharp demarcation between the socio-political question of the acceptability of homosexual conduct and the legal question of whether s 377A is unconstitutional is open to serious criticism.

*   These two questions are _not neatly separable_; there are significant overlaps. The question of whether a criminal provision violates the fundamental right to equality and non-discrimination necessarily requires some engagement with the social and moral dimensions of the conduct being criminalised. If the court insists that its role is merely to interpret the law and always to leave social issues to the government, its effectiveness and ability to enforce limits on government power becomes severely restricted.

Moreover, the source of limitation on government power that the courts seem to rely upon in these cases is political accountability, not a legal or constitutional constraint. This is a significant departure from the principle of constitutional supremacy. The court appears to suggest that issues of national security, homosexuality and similar contested topics fall outside the court’s competence because they are political questions for the government, and not something courts – as interpreters of the law – can decide.

*   This effectively cedes the role of constitutional enforcement to the very branches whose actions are being challenged. This leads to a rule of law issue, as the constitutional rights exist on paper but offer no real protection, because the institution meant to enforce them has declined to do so. That undermines the rule of law, which demands that rights are not merely declared but are capable of being judicially enforced against the state.
*   The court is the institution tasked with enforcing the hierarchy that the Constitution is the supreme law of the land. If the court declines to review whether a statute violates Arts 9 or 12 on the basis that the subject matter is “political”, it is effectively saying that Parliament’s judgment on these matters is final. But that collapses the distinction between constitutional supremacy and parliamentary supremacy. The Constitution ceases to be a meaningful constraint on legislative power if the very body meant to enforce it steps back and defers to the legislature on contested issues.

Therefore, the concern expressed in _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] and _Lim Meng Suang_ \[2014\] that the courts might become a “super-legislature” or “mini-legislature” and thereby violate the rule of law is arguably overstated.

*   There is a fine line between judicial lawmaking and the judiciary’s legitimate function as a check on the other branches.
*   If courts refuse to adjudicate on issues for fear they would be “dabbling in law-making”, it becomes very difficult for the decisions of the other branches to be checked at all. Furthermore, it remains unclear where the boundaries of what constitutes as “law-making” lies, and what should be regarded as falling within or outside the scope of the court’s proper role.

In fairness, there is a credible counter-argument in favour of judicial deference on policy questions. The experience of the United States in the seminal case of _Roe v Wade_ is an oft-cited cautionary example. In _Roe v Wade_, the U.S. Supreme Court’s decision to constitutionalise the right to abortion abruptly halted the gradual legislative liberalisation of abortion laws that had been underway across the states. This judicial intervention provoked a strong counter-movement, delayed a stable resolution, and entrenched the issue in ongoing legal and political battles. Even now, _Roe v Wade_ continues to shape the legal discourse and judicial appointments, illustrating the lasting consequences of bypassing democratic debate.

*   The court in _Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\] implicitly drew on this reasoning – litigation may not always be the best mechanism for resolving these deep social controversies, and premature judicial resolution can fracture the polity rather than heal it.
*   This suggests that there may be pragmatic merit in allowing certain issues to be resolved through the political process, even if this entails a temporary limitation on the judicial protection of rights.

#### Recent Shift from Deference to Autonomy (and hints of control)

In recent years, there has been a discernible shift in the judiciary’s conception of SOP. Courts have moved along the spectrum from deference towards autonomy, and in some instances, have shown hints of the more robust conception of SOP as control. This evolution is marked by a greater willingness to engage with international legal arguments and academic commentary, and by a more assertive articulation of the judiciary’s constitutional role.

In _Tan Seet Eng_ \[2015\] SGCA, the SGCA made a landmark statement that the judiciary is “one of three co-equal branches” of government. Each branch as a separate and distinct responsibility, but they are all ultimately subject to the Constitution. The court articulated several foundational principles.

*   The power of the state is vested in the various arms of government, and such power is subject to legal limits.
*   The specific responsibility for pronouncing on the legality of government actions, and for ensuring that any exercise of state power is done within those legal limits, falls upon the judiciary.
*   The courts are the **final arbiters** of the lawfulness of all actions, including government actions. They possess the authority to determine the boundaries of the power vested in the Executive, and to assess whether the Executive has acted within the ambit of those boundaries. In other words, the ultimate responsibility for maintaining a system which ambits by the rule of law lies with the judiciary.

_Tan Seet Eng_ acts as an endorsement of the judiciary’s constitutional significance and an important corrective to the more deferential posture of earlier cases.

In _Jolovan Wham_ \[2021\] SGCA, the SGCA expressly disagreed with _Chee Siok Chin_ and held that there is no presumption of constitutionality that would not be lightly displaced.

*   This was a case where Jolovan Wham invited a speaker from Hong Kong to speak at a rally without a permit, he was a non-citizen and this thus required a permit. Wham argued that s 16(1)(a) of the Public Order Act was not a constitutionally valid derogation from Art. 14(1) of the Constitution.

The court’s reasoning was that relying on a presumption of constitutionality would entail presuming the very issue that is being challenged. The presumption, therefore, is not an answer to a constitutional challenge, it merely begs the question. At most, the presumption of constitutionality can serve as a starting point: legislation will not presumptively be treated as suspect or unconstitutional, but neither will its constitutionality be assumed without scrutiny.

Thus, the court adopted a clear conception of SOP as autonomy – the enactment of laws lies within the competence of Parliament, but the determination of whether a challenged law is or is not constitutional lies exclusively within the ambit and competence of the courts. The court identified several guiding principles:

1.  Each branch of government has its own role and sphere, as the separation of powers is part of the basic structure of the Westminster constitutional model.
2.  In relation to Art. 14, the Constitution both refers a constitutional right (freedom of speech, assembly and association) and permits that right to be derogated from for the purposes listed under Art. 14(2)(b). While it is undeniably Parliament that acts to derogate from the right, it is unequivocally for the judiciary to determine whether that derogation falls within the relevant permitted purpose.

This decision significantly strengthened the judiciary’s hand in constitutional adjudication and signalled the courts would no longer automatically defer to Parliament on questions of constitutional validity.

_Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\] represents the clearest articulation of SOP as autonomy in Singapore’s recent constitutional history. At \[12\], the SGCA emphasised that SOP is premised on the notion that the branches of government are “co-equal”. Accordingly, the court stated unequivocally that it “will not defer to the other branches” where the constitutionality or legality of a law or executive act is challenged, because it is the function of the judiciary to make such determinations. Echoing _Jolovan Wham_ \[2012\], the court again disagreed with the presumption.

The court also articulated the principle of institutional non-intrusion. Each must be allowed to exercise “fully and fairly” the powers allocated to it but must “not intrude” upon the institutional space that belongs to other branches (at \[15\]). This dual principle ensures both independence and accountability.

*   Aka, what the court seems to want is that everyone fully and fairly exercises their powers and don’t disturb each other, but if there is a situation where judicial review is necessary, the court will step in – hence SOP as autonomy is what they are propounding.

However, the court was careful not to go so far as to adopt the full functionalist conceptualisation of SOP as control. It remained cautious in drawing a line between legal issues and issues of an extra-legal, socio-political natured. The SGCA maintained extra-legal issues were still beyond the court’s purview, and the court’s role is limited to determining the constitutionality or legality of the other branches’ acts – it does not extend to evaluating the moral or policy merits of those acts.

Neo makes a subtle but important observation about _Nagaenthran_ \[2018\] SGHC. This is perhaps her most interesting analytical move. The High Court used explicitly deferential language, stating that the judiciary ought to "defer to the intention of the legislature" and "respect the relative institutional competence of the executive." Yet Neo observes that the actual holding — preserving judicial review on constitutional grounds and holding that jurisdictional errors of law render ouster clauses ineffective — effectively denudes the ouster clause of real effect. She concludes that "the actual approach effectively upholds the idea of separation of powers as autonomy, notwithstanding the deferential tone" (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 474)

##### Regarding ouster clauses

In _Nagaenthran_ \[2019\], the SGCA addressed the constitutional status of ouster clauses – provisions that purport to exclude judicial review of executive decisions. In _obiter_, the court appeared to endorse the suggestion made by former Chief Justice Chan Sek Keong in his 2010 Lecture _Judicial Review – From Angst to Empathy_ that ouster clauses may inherently be inconsistent with Art. 93 of the Constitution, which vests the judicial power of Singapore in the courts.

*   The court rejected the contention that it would be powerless to act even if the Public Prosecutor had considered irrelevant matters under s. 33B(4) of the MDA. Such a reading would entail ousting the court’s power of judicial review, rendering the provision “constitutionally suspect for being in violation of Art. 93 of the Singapore constitution as well as the principle of the separation of powers”.
*   The court emphasised that the power of judicial review is a “core aspect of the judicial power and function” under Art. 93 and would not ordinarily be capable of being excluded by ordinary legislation as a result of Singapore’s constitutional supremacy scheme. Citing _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2011\] and _Chng Suan Tze_ \[1988\], the court reasoned that there should be “few, if any, legal disputes between the State and the people from which judicial power is excluded,” and that “the rule of law demands that the courts should be able to examine the exercise of discretionary power”.
*   This decision reflects elements of both SOP as autonomy and SOP as control (a functionalist conception) and strongly supports the principle of constitutional supremacy.
*   If some authority’s decisions are immune from judicial review, then they have the final power to determine what their legal limits are. If someone has the final power to determine what their legal limits are, then they, in effect, exercise judicial power. In other words, if a statutory provision prevents judicial review of some executive authority’s decision, then its effect is to confer judicial power on that executive authority. That would run contrary to Art. 93, which, properly interpreted, vests judicial power exclusively in the courts — for an executive authority is not a court (**Benjamin Ong, 2019**).

### Balancing SOP and the Principle of Legality

There is a recurring challenge in constitutional adjudication in how to balance fidelity to SOP (respecting each branch’s institutional sphere) with the principle of legality, which demands that all exercises of power have a lawful basis and are subject to legal limits. The courts generally try and uphold the principle of legality by intervening only in exceptional cases, ensuring that the executive discretion operates within legal bounds while still respecting the executive’s institutional competence on matters of political judgement.

In _Vellema_ \[2013\], Vellama was a voter in the Hougang SMC. The MP for the seat resigned and his seat was declared vacant. The question arose as to the Prime Minister’s obligation and discretion in calling a by-election.

The SGCA struck a carefully calibrated balance. It acknowledged that calling a by-election is a polycentric matter and that the Prime Minister should be accorded a measure of latitude in deciding when to call one. However, the court also held that this latitude is not unlimited and the Prime Minister must act in good faith – the fact that he is accorded discretion as to timing does not mean that he can defer the calling of a by-election indefinitely, or simply declare that he would not advise the President to issue a writ of election, unless he intends to advise dissolution of Parliament in the near future. Leaving a vacancy unfilled for an unnecessarily prolonged period raises a serious risk of disenfranchising the residents of that constituency.

*   The court invoked the principle from _Chng Suan Tze_ \[1988\] that all discretionary power is in the end subject to legal limits. The PM’s discretion as to the timing of an election to fall a casual vacancy is subject to judicial review, but only in exceptional cases.
*   This demonstrates how the courts can uphold the principle of legality – ensuring that executive discretion operates within legal bounds, while still respecting the executive’s institutional competence on matters of political judgement.

_Mohammad Faizal_ \[2012\] SGHC was a case that dealt with whether s 33A(1)(a) MDA – which directed courts to treat Drug Rehab Centre admissions (directed by the executive) as prior convictions (which is a judicial order) for the purposes of enhanced minimum punishments – constitutes an impermissible legislative or executive intrusion into the judicial power and thereby violates SOP.

The SGHC affirmed several foundational principles (in general):

1.  Under the Westminster model of constitutional government, the sovereign power of the state is distributed amongst the legislature (Art. 38), Executive (Art 23(1)) and the Judiciary (Art. 93). The principle of separation of powers constitutes the basic structure of the Singapore constitution. The purpose of incorporating this principle is to diffuse state power amongst different institutions.
2.  The court also observed regarding Art. 4 that since the provision uses the words “any law inconsistent with this Constitution” rather than “any provision of this Constitution”, this reinforces the principle that Parliament may not enact a law, and the Government may not perform an act which is inconsistent with SOP to the extent that the principle is embodied in the constitution.

Regarding whether s 33A(1)(a) MDA was constitutional:

The SGHC acknowledged that all common law courts, including SG, have consistently accepted or assumed that the punishment of offenders is _part of_ the judicial power. However, it noted that legal scholarship has shown there exists little historical or doctrinal support for the proposition that the sentencing power is essentially or exclusively a judicial power.

The court traced the historical development of sentencing direction, observing that it was initially assumed that the judge’s role was simply to pronounce the punishment that the law inflicted for the offence. Judicial discretion to determine sentences is a relatively modern legislative development.

*   It was the legislature that, through statute, vested courts with the discretionary power to punish offenders in accordance with a prescribed range of sentences. In the absence of contrary constitutional provisions, the legislature may prescribe whatever punishment it thinks fit for the offences it creates, since legislature is better equipped to consider the policy considerations involved.

Thus, it was held that s 33A MDA did not constitute an impermissible intrusion into the judicial power – the legislative prescription of factors for courts to consider in sentencing does not trespass on the judicial power.

*   Aka – to me this seems to say that if it is the statute saying that oh for X, it will be considered a conviction, or for Y, it will come with a minimum Z year sentence, it is valid.
*   The legislature can validly prescribe what counts as a prior conviction for sentencing purposes and can set mandatory minimums or other fixed sentencing parameters, because sentencing power was never exclusively judicial to begin with — it was the legislature that originally granted courts their discretionary sentencing power via statute. So, the legislature retaining some control over sentencing parameters isn’t an intrusion into judicial power; it never fully surrendered that domain.

However, the court did identify three scenarios in which legislative provisions would unconstitutionally intrude upon the sentencing function:

1.  Legislation enabling the Executive to select the sentence to be imposed _after_ an accused person has been convicted by a court of law.
2.  Legislation enabling the Executive to make administrative decisions directly related to the charges brought against a particular accused person, which have an impact on the actual sentence imposed by the court.
3.  Legislation enabling the Executive to make administrative decisions not directly related to the charges, but which impact the actual sentence imposed by the court pursuant to legislative directions treating those decisions as conditions limiting or eliminating the court’s sentencing discretion.

Essentially, while courts typically hold the sentencing power, this power is not exclusively or inherently vested in the judiciary. It was conferred upon the courts by the legislature, and therefore the legislature may also constrain or redistribute that discretion, so long as the impermissible scenarios are not triggered. The sentencing power falls within the jurisdiction of both Parliament and the courts, and an intrusion only arises in specific circumstances.

*   Neo highlights an important refinement from _Prabagaran_, where the Court of Appeal moderated _Mohammad Faizal_’s framing by holding that the sentencing function is not “the exclusive constitutional province of any one Branch” and could therefore fall within the functions of any branch (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 473). This is doctrinally significant because it moves away from a rigid essentialist division of powers toward a more flexible understanding where overlapping competences are constitutionally permissible, provided the integrity of the judicial function is not compromised.

### Summary and Synthesis on SG’s position on SOP

The development of Singapore’s constitutional jurisprudence on the separation of powers and fundamental liberties reveals a complex and evolving picture. The early case law, exemplified by _Ong Ah Chuan_ and _Chng Suan Tze_, adopted a robust conception of SoP as control, affirming the judiciary’s role in actively checking the other branches and defending fundamental liberties through generous interpretation and objective judicial review.

For much of the subsequent period, however, the dominant posture was one of deference. Cases such as _Rajeevan_, _Lim Meng Suang_, and _Tan Seng Kee_ reflected a judiciary that was deeply cautious about overstepping its perceived boundaries, reluctant to engage with extra-legal or socio-political arguments, and inclined to accord the legislature a presumption of constitutionality. The concerns animating this deference—fear of becoming a “mini-legislature,” recognition of the legislature’s democratic legitimacy, and awareness of the court’s institutional limitations on polycentric issues—are not without merit. The cautionary tale of _Roe v Wade_ illustrates the real risks of premature judicial resolution of deeply contested social questions.

Nevertheless, the more recent jurisprudence marks a significant and welcome shift. _Tan Seet Eng_ \[2015\], _Jolovan Wham_ \[2021\], _Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\], and _Nagaenthran_ \[2019\] collectively signal a movement towards the conception of SoP as autonomy—and at times, even hints of SoP as control. The courts now more clearly assert their co-equal constitutional status, reject the presumption of constitutionality as a substantive barrier to challenge, and insist that the determination of constitutional questions falls exclusively within the judicial domain.

The key tension that remains is the boundary between legal questions (which the courts accept as their own) and extra-legal or socio-political questions (which the courts still regard as belonging to the legislature). The exact location of this boundary is contested and, as the section 377A cases demonstrate, may sometimes be impossible to draw cleanly. As the jurisprudence continues to evolve, the challenge for Singapore’s courts will be to maintain their institutional autonomy and their checking function without overreaching into the policy-making sphere—a balance that is difficult but essential to the health of a constitutional democracy.

As Neo emphasises, there is a **need to overcome the claim arising from Singapore's prevailing constitutional culture that judicial review constitutes illegitimate “activism.”** This requires convincing judges of their authority and responsibility to scrutinise governmental decisions without compromising their impartial status. As Ginsburg observes, judicial review “may be countermajoritarian but is not counterdemocratic” (Neo, _Autonomy, Deference and Control_ at 483, citing Ginsburg). This framing usefully reorients the debate: the concern should not be whether courts are overriding majority preferences, but whether they are fulfilling their constitutional function within a system of separated powers under a supreme constitution.

### Key Cases Table (SOP)

| Case | Key Principle | SoP Conception |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Ong Ah Chuan [1981] UKPC | Generous interpretation of Part IV; restrictions to be narrowly construed | SoP as Control |
| Chng Suan Tze [1988] SGCA | Objective test for ISA review; all power has legal limits | SoP as Control |
| Rajeevan [1998] SGHC | Textualist approach; courts cannot expand scope of legislation | SoP as Deference |
| Tan Seet Eng [2015/2016] SGCA | Courts are co-equal branch and final arbiters of lawfulness | SoP as Autonomy/Control |
| Lim Meng Suang [2014] SGCA | Courts cannot consider extra-legal arguments or become mini-legislature | SoP as Deference |
| Yong Vui Kong [2015] SGCA | Common law has no greater weight than statute; courts not super-legislature | SoP as Deference |
| Jolovan Wham [2021] SGCA | No presumption of constitutionality; courts determine constitutionality | SoP as Autonomy |
| Tan Seng Kee [2022] SGCA | Co-equal branches; courts will not defer on legality questions | SoP as Autonomy |
| Nagaenthran [2019] SGCA | Ouster clauses potentially inconsistent with Art 93; JR is core judicial power | SoP as Autonomy/Control |
| Vellama [2013] SGCA | PM discretion on by-elections subject to good faith and judicial review | Balanced |
| Faizal [2012] SGHC | Sentencing power not exclusively judicial; SoP is basic structure | Balanced |

## Constitutional Supremacy

Constitutional Supremacy is the notion that the constitution is the supreme law of the land (Limbach, 2001). Under this principle, courts may strike down laws or executive actions that are inconsistent with the constitution – the SGHC in _Colin Chan_ \[1994\] has laid out that the court has the **power and duty** to ensure that the provisions of the Constitution are observed and to declare invalid any exercise of power which exceeds the limits of the power conferred by the Constitution (at \[50\]).

*   This is already provided by Art. 4 of the Constitution anyways – which basically has the effect of buttressing the supremacy of the written constitution through a supremacy clause (_Thio and Tan_ at 3.008).

So, the constitution operates as a legal limit in government. All institutions, laws and practices established by or under the constitution must conform to its requirements. This principle distinguishes constitutional democracies from systems of parliamentary sovereignty where Parliament can pass any law without constitutional constraint and the only checks on Parliament are political, not legal.

The **essence** of constitutional supremacy goes beyond strict adherence to the text of the constitution (the literalist view). While procedural compliance matters, constitutional supremacy embodies the deeper idea that a supreme law governs all institutions established by that constitution, distributing powers across governmental branches and establishing fundamental principles that bind all actors. The courts serve as final arbiters determining whether institutions have conformed to constitutional provisions and principles.

#### What makes a Constitution Supreme?

There are three aspects to explain what makes a constitution supreme:

1.  The logical explanation – If a constitution constitutes the polity that details the rules by which its institutions operate, it logically follows that these rules must be supreme. The constitution is logically prior to and more fundamental than any law made under it.
2.  The procedural explanation – the constitution is the product of a body that has the power to make a supreme law, essentially a Constituent Assembly exercising constituent power. SG itself has no Constituent Assembly – the Republic of Singapore Independence Act (RSIA) was enacted by Parliament. This difficulty was resolved in _Taw Cheng Kong_ \[1998\] SGCA, where the SGCA held that the political fact of SG’s independence and sovereignty had the consequence of vesting the Legislative Assembly with plenary powers, including the power to enact the Constitution of Singapore (Amendment) Act and the RSIA. So, since the Constitution was the product of the Legislative Assembly, the constitution is supreme law.
3.  The moral authority explanation – the constitution can and ought to command obligation and obedience since it is superior by nature over any ordinary law – it is hierarchically superior and paramount.

In addition, Limbach (2001) identifies three traits that primarily characterise the principle of constitutional supremacy:

1.  The possibility of distinguishing between constitutional and ordinary laws;
2.  An institution with the authority to check the constitutionality of government legal acts in the event of conflict (usually constitutional judicial review);
3.  The legislator’s being bound by constitutional law, which presupposes special procedures for amending constitutional law – which has a different amendment procedure from ordinary legislation.

#### The Text of the Constitution suggests Consti. Supremacy

The presence of Art. 4, the supremacy clause, does prima facie suggest that Singapore operates under a constitutional supremacy scheme. Art. 4 provides that the Constitution is the “supreme law of the Republic of Singapore and any law enacted by the Legislature after the commencement of this Constitution which is inconsistent with this Constitution shall, to the extent of the inconsistency, be void”.

Art. 162 complements this by providing that all existing laws shall continue in force but “shall be construed as from the commencement of this Constitution with such modifications, adaptations, qualifications and exceptions **as may be necessary to bring them into conformity with this Constitution**”.

As noted by the SGCA in _Tan Eng Hong_, all laws must be construed in conformity with the Constitution – and Art.s 4 and 162 share the overarching aim of upholding constitutional supremacy.

### Is there Constitutional Supremacy in Singapore?

In practice, it seems like there is a difference between the _de jure_ and _de facto_ reality.

#### Singapore has features resembling Parliamentary Supremacy

Although Singapore is _de jure_ a constitutional supremacy system, there are pertinent features of the Singaporean constitutional regime that resemble a de facto parliamentary supremacy. These include the _de facto_ flexibility of the written constitution and the weakness of the courts as a restraint on executive and legislative powers.

Singapore courts appear to be adverse to exercising their powers of judicial review to directly strike down Parliament’s actions, adopting a fairly deferential attitude towards the legislature.

*   Though, we should note there has been a recent shift towards SOP as autonomy that might reflect that Singapore might be heading back towards constitutional supremacy.
*   Also, it is important to note that parliamentary primacy need not be equated with parliamentary supremacy, since the former is consistent with the existence of a written constitution serving as an ultimate source of authority (Kenny Chng, 2019).
*   Parliamentary primacy is the concept where in a legal system governed by constitutional supremacy, with the written constitution still being the highest law of the land, Parliament is not above the constitution but still retains “primacy” as the central, most legitimate democratic institution for shaping policy and law. Parliament acts as the primary engine of the State and the voice of the electorate, but its law-making power is still limited by the boundaries set in the written constitution and is still subject to judicial review, though there is still judicial deference and they acknowledge that Parliament is better equipped to weigh public policy than appointed judges.

The SGHC in _Lo Pui Sang_ expounded a very restricted view of constitutional supremacy that does not truly reflect the spirit of what having a supreme constitution means – the SGHC contrasted America’s approach – where the State may not make any law depriving a person of personal liberty “without due process of law” – with Singapore’s, where the Constitution expressly permits deprivation of personal liberty if it is “in accordance with law”.

*   The court opined that even if the words “save in accordance with the law” in Art. 9 inclined “liberally in favour of legislative power”, the clear words could not be altered by the court; the court had to obey the Constitution as it was.
*   This was a formalistic view of SOP as deference and constitutional supremacy. If taken to its logical conclusion, it suggests that personal liberty may be deprived so long as it is in accordance with any law passed by Parliament – affording immense power to the legislative branch and significantly weakening the protective dimension of constitutional supremacy.

#### The Presumption of Constitutionality has Tension with Constitutional Supremacy

The adoption of the presumption of constitutionality in cases like _Ramalingam_, _Taw Cheng Kong_ and _Chee Siok Chin_ contrives a constitutional supremacy mindset (and leans in favour of Parliamentary Supremacy). If the Constitution is truly supreme law, there is a case to be made that all powers should be actively examined to ensure adherence to and consistency with the Constitution.

*   Courts should not assume that the other branches are exercising their powers consistently with the Constitution; rather, they should independently determine whether laws and actions are constitutional, instead of readily concluding using the presumption that whatever the other branches do is constitutional, placing the burden on applicants to prove otherwise.
*   This line of thinking appears to have been implicitly adopted by the SGCA in _Jolovan Wham_ \[2021\] and reaffirmed in _Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\] SGCA, where the court opined that the presumption of constitutionality is “no more than a starting point”, since to rely on the presumption would entail presuming the very issue being challenged. The SGCA expressly disagreed with _Chee Siok Chin_ that such a presumption “would not be lightly displaced”. The determination of whether a law is constitutional, the court emphasised, is firmly and solely within the ambit and competence of the courts – this represents a welcome shift to a more robust understanding of constitutional supremacy.

If there is a broad deference to discretion of the executive and legislature underpinned by presumption, **it goes against the rule of law principle that all powers have legal limits** (_Chng Suan Tze_), since it actively provides the other two branches powers to act that is prima facie unfettered.

*   Which then leads to the constitutional supremacy issue, as courts have a constitutional role as guardians of constitution; they should perform active role scrutinizing other branches’ acts to ensure constitutional compliance and not infringe upon fundamental liberties.

Such a strong reliance on the presumption arguably affords too high a degree of power to the legislative branch. Art. 4 of the Constitution makes it clear that the Constitution is the supreme law of Singapore and should be regarded as such; and therefore all exercises of powers should be examined by the courts to ensure compliance with the Constitution, rather than operating on an assumption that the other branches of the legislative and executive are exercising their powers consistently and appropriately. As such, it is submitted that determining the constitutionality of a law should strictly come under the purview of the courts, rather than risking over-reliance on a mere presumption.

#### The Presence of Ouster Clauses Contradicts Constitutional Supremacy

The presence of constitutional ouster clauses such as Art. 149 appears to weaken and contradict constitutional supremacy. By giving the executive exemptions from being subject to judicial review (Art. 149(3)) and thus constitutional limits, ouster clauses create a vacuum, as there is nobody to check the government’s power when it comes to national security decisions.

However, on a charitable view, these clauses may be justified on some bases. (1) They apply only in cases of exceptional necessity relating to national security, (2) procedural safeguards remain in place, as while the court no longer has the power to review or question a decision of the Minister to detain under the ISA, it can still adjudicate regarding procedural safeguards, (3) there is a competence argument, as courts might not have the institutional competence to decide on such cases, and the executive is arguably in a better position to determine national security issues, so it may be acceptable to take certain decisions out of the court’s jurisdiction.

*   Nonetheless, Art. 93 says that the Supreme Court (part of which is the High Court) have judicial power. The power to perform judicial review is, by definition (through conventional usage), part of the High Court’s judicial power. (_Tan Seet Eng_ at \[1\] says so: “Judges are entrusted with the task of ensuring that any exercise of state power is done within legal limits.”) So, an ouster clause would stop the High Court from exercising the judicial power that the High Court, by definition, has.
*   If some authority’s decisions are immune from judicial review, then they have the final power to determine what their legal limits are. If someone has the final power to determine what their legal limits are, then they, in effect, exercise judicial power. In other words, if a statutory provision prevents judicial review of some executive authority’s decision, then its effect is to confer judicial power on that executive authority.
