That would run contrary to Art. 93, which, properly interpreted, vests judicial power exclusively in the courts — for an executive authority is not a court (Benjamin Ong, 2019).
*   Even legislation that purports to oust judicial review on some grounds but not others are not valid – the SGCA in _Nagaenthran_ only says that a statute that totally prevents the court from engaging in judicial review on any ground would be “constitutionally suspect”. At \[43\], the Court of Appeal noted that “the \[PP’s\] position is that aside from the grounds of bad faith, malice and unconstitutionality, the supervisory jurisdiction of the courts over the PP’s determination under s 33B(2)(b) of the MDA is excluded by s 33B(4)”. At \[74\], the Court of Appeal said that “\[i\]f the respondent’s submission on the effect of s 33B(4) were accepted, then to the extent that this ousted the court’s power of judicial review, s 33B(4) would be constitutionally suspect…” One could read this as saying that not only a complete ouster of judicial review, but even the notion that s 33B ousts judicial review on some grounds, would be “constitutionally suspect”.

#### Judicial Deference Points Away from Constitutional Supremacy

In _Vellama_ \[2013\] SGCA, the SGCA held that “judicial intervention would only be warranted in exceptional cases”. This deferential posture, combined with the restricted view of constitutional supremacy in _Lo Pui Sang_ and the presumption of constitutionality, suggests that the philosophy of judicial decisions in Singapore is often coloured by principles associated with Parliamentary supremacy, even within a formal constitutional supremacy framework.

#### SG However is not a Parliamentary Supremacy System

Nevertheless, the fact remains that SG is not a _de jure_ parliamentary supremacy. As Kenny Chng (2019) argues, parliamentary primacy need not be equated with parliamentary supremacy, since the former is consistent with the existence of a written constitution serving as an ultimate source of authority.

*   Parliament’s intent is a _de facto_ primary recourse in constitutional interpretation, but it is only important to the extent that it relates to the constitution. As expounded by the SGCA in _Tan Cheng Bock_, the intent will generally be discerned from the text of the constitution, so the text is still the first consideration for the courts. This reflects the idea that it is ultimately the Constitution, not Parliament, that is supreme in Singapore.

On one view, the primacy that Singapore courts have accorded to the constitution’s text and parliamentary intent reflects a judicial concern to maintain a proper separation of powers, rather than an acknowledgement of Parliament’s _de jure_ supremacy (Kenny Chng, 2019).

#### So overall stance

Constitutional supremacy is the notion that the Constitution is the supreme law of the land and ranks highest within the legal order and hierarchy of laws (Limbach, 2001). In Singapore, this is expressly encapsulated in Art. 4 of the Constitution and thus any the enaction of any law contrary to the constitution will be void.

As a matter of institutional design, it is submitted that constitutional supremacy is necessary as it “ensures that the institutions created by the Constitutions are governed by the ROL, and that the fundamental liberties under part IV of the Constitution are guaranteed” (_Tan Seet Eng_). The doctrine of CS thus (1) restraints the exercise of power by setting the boundaries of the powers vested in the institutions that it creates and (2) prevents legislative incursion on the fundamental liberties except in accordance with the Constitution (eg where the case concerns national security as provided for in Art 149 of the constitution)

It is essential to note that constitutional supremacy in Singapore does not guarantee that individual liberties will prevail against legislative encroachment, **reflecting Singapore’s non-liberal conception of constitutionalism**. However, to prevent unfettered exercise of Government power, acceptance of CS requires that the terms on which liberties may be limited are themselves constitutionally prescribed and publicly known, forming a clear legal limit to the exercise of powers. This is illustrated in Art. 149 which expressly authorises Parliament to legislate in a manner inconsistent with certain fundamental liberty provisions where national security considerations are engaged. What constitutional supremacy demands, on Singapore’s non-liberal conception, is **not the triumph of individual rights but the constitutional licensing of their limitation**, ensuring that the **balance between individual liberty and collective welfare is struck within a framework that is legally entrenched**, democratically accountable, and judicially supervised at its boundaries.

### Constitutional Supremacy in SG’s Political Context – with Dominant Parties

_To understand constitutional supremacy in SG – give attention to the political context that shapes how the theoretical principles operate in practice_.

Singapore’s dominant party system profoundly affects constitutional amendment dynamics. The PAP has governed continuously since independence in 1965, consistently commanding parliamentary supermajorities well exceeding the two-thirds threshold required for constitutional amendment under Art. 5(2). This political reality transforms constitutional amendability from a theoretical possibility into a practical routine – the government possesses a reliable capacity to amend the Constitution whenever the political will exists to do so, unconstrained by the difficulty of assembling supermajority coalitions across parties as occurs in more competitive political systems.

Moreover, Westminster parliamentary fusion combines with strict party discipline to ensure government control over parliamentary processes. Cabinet members are simultaneously MPs, and party whip enforcement means government backbenchers vote with party leadership on critical matters, including constitutional amendments. The separation between the executive and the legislature (proposing and approving amendments respectively) becomes largely formal – the same political organisation controls both institutions.

*   Consequently, procedural limits on amendments – requiring two-third approval – functions as low barriers in Singapore’s political context. The government can navigate these procedural requirements easily, rendering the procedural limitations relatively weak constraints on amendment power.

This political context makes **substantive limits on amendment power particularly important for constitutional supremacy**. If procedural requirements provide minimal practical constraint because the governing party easily commands the necessary supermajorities, then substantive limits on _what_ can be amended, regardless of procedural compliance, becomes critical safeguards preventing wholesale constitutional transformation.

*   Without such substantive limits, dominant party control combined with Westminster fusion and party discipline could permit constitutional revolution through amendment, transforming fundamental constitutional arrangements while satisfying formalistic procedural requirements.
*   **Constitutional supremacy in SG’s context demands more than procedural compliance; it requires that certain essential constitutional features remain protected against amendment regardless of the political will to change them**.

### Amenability of the Constitution

The amendability of the constitution raises a fundamental tension between flexibility and rigidity. There are two competing views. **The amendability shows how robust constitutional supremacy is in practice**. If Parliament can amend anything provided it follows the right procedure, then constitutional supremacy is ultimately reducible to whatever the current parliamentary majority wants it to be. Substantive limits on amendability — like the basic structure doctrine — protects constitutional supremacy from self-destruction, since without them Parliament could theoretically amend away the very features that make Singapore a constitutional democracy.

*   So it helps us determine whether constitutional supremacy is genuinely entrenched or merely formal.

#### Flexibility

Even if the constitution is the supreme law of the land and above all other ordinary laws, it should be flexible – that is, relatively easy to amend. Flexibility ensures that the Constitution can reflect the prevailing social mores and aspirations of Singapore society (_Lim Meng Suang_ \[2015\] at \[92\]). There is a need to meet evolving societal needs and ensure the continued relevance of the constitution – constitutions need to be flexible to remain stable over time, and there is a need to build attachment and familiarity to the constitution in subsequent generations.

*   However, the flexibility argument must be treated carefully in the Singapore context. Cherian George observes that the PAP’s successive constitutional amendments have not generally been driven by evolving societal consensus, but rather by the government’s desire to “protect Singapore governance from the vagaries of public opinion and the popular vote” – amendments are enacted to “create structural fixes” that pre-empt future political challenges, rather than to reflect shifting social mores (Cherian George, _Air Conditioned Nation Revisited, 2020_). This suggests that in practice, constitutional flexibility in a single-party dominant state may operate less as a mechanism for keeping the constitution in step with society, and more as a mechanism for keeping the constitution in step with the governing party’s institutional interests. The theoretical justification for flexibility — that it allows the constitution to reflect prevailing social aspirations — therefore **depends on assumptions about democratic responsiveness that may not hold** where the amendment process is controlled by a single dominant party.

#### Rigidity

Constitutions should be inflexible because they contain certain norms and values that cannot be easily changed or derogated from. If any constitutional provision is easily amendable, then constitutional supremacy will lose its meaning. Making amendments difficult prevents hasty changes driven by temporary political pressures or majoritarian passions, and protects minority rights and core constitutional values from erosion.

Rigidity ensures that constitutional change occurs only when broad, sustained consensus exists – not merely when the government of the day commands sufficient votes.

*   In the SG context of a single-party dominated state, it is arguably even more important to have a rigid constitution, to ensure that the government (which easily has the requisite numbers) cannot easily change the values and norms enshrined in the constitution.
*   This concern is given empirical force by Cherian George’s observation that since independence, constitutional amendment in Singapore has functioned as a tool of executive enrichment rather than as a safeguard against it. George notes that “since independence, PAP ideology has been contemptuous of liberal democratic checks and balances that impede the important work of cabinet”, and that POFMA – like earlier legislative innovations – “signifies an entrenching of the principle of executive dominance”, formally elevating the interests of government bodies to the “public interest” (Cherian George, _Air Conditioned Nation Revisited_). Critically, this pattern operates through constitutional amendment itself – the post _Chng Suan Tze_ constitutional amendments curtailing judicial review of ISA detentions, the amendments creating and subsequently narrowing the elected presidency and the anti-hopping provisions that tether MPS to their party under threat of losing their seats – were all achieved by a government commanding the requisite parliamentary supermajority with relative ease. This is precisely the scenario that rigidity norms are designed to prevent: where the amending party and the governing party are effectively the same, procedural supermajority requirements lose their counter-majoritarian function. The SG experience therefore illustrates that procedural limitations on amendment power are necessary but not sufficient – without substantive limitations such as the basic structure doctrine, procedural requirements can be satisfied by the very concentrated political power they are meant to check.

#### Overall, how?

If the constitution is too rigid, it might break – but if the constitution is too malleable, it undermines the spirit of having a written constitution. Most constitutional systems therefore strike a balance, typically through the limitations on amendment power – both procedural limitations and substantive limitations.

### Procedural Limitations on Amendment

#### General Amendment Procedure – Art. 5

Under Art. 5(2), the Constitution can generally be amended by the votes of a two-thirds majority of all elected MPs in Parliament, excluding NMPs. This makes Singapore’s constitution moderately rigid – more difficult to amend than ordinary legislation, which only requires a simple majority, but not as rigid as systems requiring referendum approval or super-majorities.

#### Entrenchment Framework of 2016 – Art 5A, 5B and 5C

The 2015 constitutional amendments introduced a far more elaborate entrenchment framework under Art. 5A, 5B and 5C. Although the Bill has been passed and Art. 5 is in force, all other provisions of this framework are held in abeyance and have not yet taken effect. The framework establishes a tiered system of protection, with different actors and procedures involved depending on the nature of the provision being amended.

#### Tier 1 – “Foundational” Provisions under Art. 5A

Art. 5A applies to provisions establishing the EP’s function as Head of State and the principle of custodial function, as well as the entrenchment framework itself.

The amendment procedure under Art. 5A operates as follows: A Bill to amend a Tier 1 provision can be introduced in Parliament. If the EP concurs (Art. 5A(1)(a)), the Bill proceeds and requires a two-thirds parliamentary majority (Art. 5A(5)).

If the EP disagrees, the outcome depends on the CPA’s position. If the CPA majority agrees with the EP’s refusal, a national referendum with a simple majority of 50% is required, under Art. 5A(1)(b), along with a two-thirds parliamentary majority (Art. 5A(5)).

*   The President’s grounds for refusal and the CPA’s recommendation must be published in the Gazette (Art. 5A(3)).

If the CPA disagrees with the EP (aka the CPA concurs with the amendment bill), the Bill may proceed with a two-thirds parliamentary majority under Art. 5A(2) and Art. 5A(5), but the President’s grounds must be published in the Gazette (Art. 5A(4)(a)) and the CPA’s recommendation must be presented to Parliament (Art. 5A(4)(b)).

#### Tier 2 – “Framework” Provisions Under Art. 5B

Art. 5B applies to discrete instances where the EP’s powers are exercised – including Art. 17(3) (a provision relating to the Presidency and the Election Judgement) and Art. 154A (authorising the President to act in his discretion) and other Art.s.

If the EP concurs, the Bill proceeds with a two-thirds parliamentary majority (Art. 5B(5)). If the EP says no and the CPA agrees with the EP’s refusal, the Bill can still pass through one of two routes: (1) A three-quarters parliamentary majority override (Art. 5B(7)), or (2) a national referendum with a simple majority followed by a two-thirds parliamentary majority (Art. 5B(1)(b) and 5B(5)).

*   A Bill introduced under the referendum route may be amended during its passage through Parliament if the Speaker is of the opinion that the amendment is minor, purely drafting in character, or does not affect the substance of the Bill supported at referendum.
*   The President’s grounds must be published in the Gazette (Art. 5B(8)(a)) and the CPA’s recommendation presented to Parliament (Art. 5B(8)(b)).

If the CPA disagrees with the EP (Art. 5B(2))—i.e., the CPA concurs with the amendment—the Bill proceeds like a normal constitutional amendment, still requiring a two-thirds parliamentary majority (Art. 5B(5)), but the President’s grounds must be published in the Gazette (Art. 5B(4)(a)) and the CPA’s recommendation presented to Parliament (Art. 5B(4)(b)).

#### Tier 3 – Indirect Circumvention Under Art. 5C

Art. 5C addresses constitutional amendment bills (of other provisions) that circumvent or curtail any discretionary power of the President conferred by a Tier 2 provision (those listed in Art. 5B(9)). The purpose of Art. 5C is to provide an additional safeguard against attempts to **indirectly weaken or bypass** the EP’s discretionary powers. If Parliament attempts to circumvent the structured safeguards of Art. 5B by making changes to unrelated sections of the Constitution that affect the EP’s powers in a roundabout way, or introduces amendments that do not explicitly fall under Art. 5B(9) but still limit the President’s powers, Art. 5C empowers the President to withhold assent.

The President’s withholding here can be overruled. If the CPA supported the President’s withholding (Art. 5C(4)(a)), it can be overridden by either a national referendum (simple majority) or a three-quarters parliamentary majority. If the President withheld assent contrary to the CPA’s recommendation (Art. 5C(4)(b)), a two-thirds parliamentary majority suffices to override.

In essence, Art. 5B controls direct amendment to the EP’s discretionary powers while Art. 5C ensures Parliament cannot indirectly weaken these powers through backdoor amendments,

#### Rationale for Different Tiers

The rationale for having different actors and procedures involved for amending different provisions is multi-layered.

1.  It operationalises checks and balances by distributing the amendment power across multiple institutions;
2.  Referendums ground political legitimacy in the people’s right to exercise democratic choice.
3.  Varying amendment difficulty is an expression of the constitutional values of the State – the difficulty in amending a provision usually reflects its hierarchy of importance.
4.  Procedural threshold determine how entrenched a provision is.

The framework seeks to achieve a workable balance between preserving the adaptability of entrenched provisions and preventing the easy removal or amendment of the EP.

### Basic Structure Doctrine – Substantive Limitations

While procedural limitations constrain how constitutional amendments are made, substantive limitations constrain what amendments can do. The BSD represents the primary framework for analysing substantive limitations on Parliament’s amendment power.

#### Origins of BSD from Kesavananda

The BSD originated in the Indian Supreme Court’s landmark decision in _Kesavananda_ (1973). The Court established that Parliament can amend any provision of the constitution, provided that the result of the amendment does not alter the basic structure of the constitution.

*   Under this doctrine, features such as the supremacy of the constitution, republican and democratic forms of government, the secular character of the constitution, separation of powers, the federal character of the constitution, and the dignity and freedom of the individual are considered _so_ fundamental that even a procedurally valid amendment cannot destroy them.

Several critical features characterise the _Kesavananda_ formulation:

1.  The doctrine posits that the constitution possesses a basic structure that can be judicially protected. The locus of protection lies with the judiciary, contrasting with procedural limitations where Parliament itself determines compliance. This places courts in the position of potentially blocking parliamentary amendments – an admittedly controversial role that raises SOP concerns.
2.  The Indian Supreme Court went beyond articulating the general principle to identify specific features comprising India’s basic structure. This specificity provided concrete content to the doctrine but also raised questions about judicial overreach.
3.  The Court derived these features through three mechanisms – (a) inference or deduction from the constitutional text itself, (b) examination of constitutional history and (3) analysis of the constitutional preamble. By grounding its identification of basic features in constitutional sources, the Court attempted to demonstrate that it was interpreting rather than creating constitutional law. The argument for the doctrine thus stemmed from the Preamble and the aspirations of the people expressed within it – the Indian judges argued that it was the intent of the drafters that there are fundamental characteristics that cannot be changed.

Such an interpretive approach gives judges considerable latitude in determining what constraints to place on parliamentary amendment power.

_NB: This approach is actually a basic features doctrine, according to Kevin Tan (2017)_

#### The BSD was initially rejected

In _Teo Soh Lung_ \[1989\] SGCA, the SGCA categorically rejected the _Kesavananda_ doctrine and any application of basic structure ideas to Singapore. The Constitutional amendment at issue – which authorised ISA amendments restricting judicial review – was upheld as valid despite its significant impact on judicial power.

The SGCA grounded its rejection in SOP and ROL arguments derived from constitutional history. The framers of SG’s Constitution, the court reasoned, did not contemplate limiting Parliament’s amendment power.

*   If they had wanted such limitations, then they would have included express provisions to that effect. Since no such provisions exist, no limits apply.

This reflects a formalistic rule of law reasoning – the Constitutional text prescribes procedures for amendment but includes no substantive restrictions, therefore, Parliament’s amendment power is unlimited subject only to procedural compliance.

The Court also invoked SOP, arguing that Parliament’s law-making authority (including constitutional amendment) should not be encroached upon by the judiciary. Each branch possesses distinct powers, and judicial enforcement of substantive limits on amendment would improperly intrude into legislative territory.

*   This reflects the courts’ perennial concern about transgressing into the law-making powers of the legislature and thereby violating SOP.

#### Current Position – Cautious Acknowledgement

The SG position has evolved from outright rejection to cautious acknowledgement. The general tenor of more recent cases has left it open whether the basic features doctrine is, or will be accepted, as part of the law in Singapore. At the very least, **courts have recognised that a basic structure does exist**, though the full doctrine remains more questionable.

**_Mohammad Faizal bin Sabtu v PP_** \[2012\] SGHC

The Court’s position began shifting in _Mohammad Faizal_. While not endorsing the _Kesavananda_ doctrine itself, the Court accepted that Parliament’s amendment powers can be limited by the Singapore Constitution’s basic structure.

*   This represents an important distinction – rejecting the Indian formulation in _Kesavananda_ while acknowledging the possibility of substantive limits grounded in Singapore’s own constitutional framework.

The court’s reasoning drew heavily on constitutional supremacy principles. Singapore, unlike the UK, operates under constitutional supremacy rather than parliamentary supremacy. Under this, the constitution serves as supreme law governing all institutions and laws, and the judiciary acts as the final arbiter determining constitutional conformity.

*   This judicial role would be undermined if Parliament could amend the constitution to destroy the very framework that grants Parliament its authority.

The court emphasised that SOP – “whether conceived as a sharing or a division of sovereign power between the 3 organs of the state” – forms part of Singapore’s constitutional basic structure. Parliament cannot enact laws destroying the separation of powers principle embodied in the Constitution, as this would undermine the constitutional framework itself.

**_Yong Vui Kong v PP_** \[2015\] SGCA

In _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\], the SGCA refined and polished its approach. The SGCA again rejected the formulation of the doctrine in _Kesavananda_, but nevertheless acknowledged that **Singapore’s Constitution possesses a basic structure**. Crucially, the Court articulated the key test for identifying features of that basic structure – it must be “fundamental and essential to the political system that is established thereunder” at \[71\]).

*   This standard provides a filtering mechanism for determining what counts as basic structure. Not every constitutional provision or principle qualifies. Instead, the feature must be so fundamental to the Westminster parliamentary democracy model that Singapore adopts that removing or altering it would fundamentally transform the system.

Applying this standard, the Court identified SOP as meeting this threshold, and accepted that the right to vote may possibly also be a part of the basic structure, as it is fundamental to representative democracy.

*   However, the Court notably avoided directly answering whether the full basic features doctrine is accepted in Singapore, instead referring to the idea that extra-legal arguments are outside the court’s jurisdiction.

**_Wong Souk Yee v AG_** \[2019\] SGCA

Applying the _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] test, the SGCA held that there was no particular _form_ of representation (ie representation by a full slate of elected MPs) that was part of the basic structure, but left open whether the right of representation itself was part of the basic structure.

**_Ravi s/o Madasamy v AG_** \[2017\] SGHC

The SGHC held that the “right to stand for public office” could not be said to be fundamental and essential to the political system established under the Constitution. Since any right to stand for the office of President was contingent on a person meeting the right qualifications, it cannot be a basic structure that every person has a right to stand for public office. The fact that there was no pre-existing right to stand for the office of President to 1991 when the EP came into force was a further reason that the right to stand for office was not fundamental and essential.

*   Importantly, the SGHC noted that at this point in time, the basic feature doctrine from _Kesavananda_ **had not been accepted in Singapore**. At most, there was a “minimalist” or “thin” conception of the basic structure doctrine mentioned in _Mohammad Faizal_, holding that the Constitution rests on an overarching principle or framework embracing the precepts of the rule of law and the separation of powers. The court described this thin conception as “no more than a specific application of the general principle of purposive statutory interpretation”.
*   Notably, the court at \[55\] acknowledged that “there may be valid distinctions” between the terms “basic structure doctrine” and “basic features doctrine”, but declined to explore them since neither party argued the distinction mattered. The court thus used both terms interchangeably throughout. This is significant because the basic structure / basic features distinction that the court flagged but left unexplored is precisely the distinction that Kevin Tan develops in his academic commentary — cited by the court at \[58\] — where he argues that Chan CJ in _Mohammad Faizal_ was doing something more modest than the _Kesavananda_ basic features doctrine. The distinction is therefore academic rather than judicial in origin, and the court’s willingness to elide the two terms reflects the very conceptual looseness that Tan sought to correct.

The trajectory from _Mohammad Faizal_ through _Yong Vui Kong_ to _M Ravi_ and _Wong Souk Yee_ confirms a key distinction: the difference between (a) the _Kesavananda_/Indian basic structure doctrine (which has been consistently rejected in Singapore) and (b) the concept that Singapore’s Constitution has a basic structure (which has been acknowledged and accepted). Singapore has not adopted the Indian approach wholesale. Rather, Singapore courts have developed an indigenous doctrine grounded in Singapore’s own constitutional framework, history, and political system.

#### Basic Structure vs Basic Features

Kevin Tan (2017) has argued that Chan CJ’s reference to “basic structure” in _Mohammad Faizal_ was not a reference to the basic features doctrine, as noted in _Ravi_. Tan argues that Chan CJ’s “basic structure” argument derived from the Westminster model, which separates or distributes state power between the three functional branches of government, and is grounded in history, legal precedent and the logic of legal continuity. So long as Singapore purports to have a constitution founded on the Westminster model, such a basic structure must exist.

The approach to the basic structure in Singapore courts therefore differs from _Kesavananda_. In _Kesavananda_, the argument for the doctrine stemmed from the Preamble and the aspirations of the people expressed within it – **making it a substantive, rights-based approach**. **However**, the logic in _Mohammad Faizal_ stemmed from the Westminster model that Singapore follows, a **structural** approach.

It is therefore possible between basic features (related to individual rights and liberties, with more substantive content) and basic structures (taking a more structural POV, laying the foundation essential to operationalise the constitution). Recognised basic structures in Singapore include SOP and the right to vote as fundamental to representative democracy.

*   By adopting the “basic structure” argument rather than the full “basic features” doctrine, the court puts **minimal constraints** on Parliament’s powers to amend the Constitution. Provided the requisite procedures are followed and the necessary majorities obtained. The Constitution may be amended. This contrasts with the much more expansive basic features doctrine, under which a constellation of judicially determined extra-constitutional ‘features’ would serve to limit the amendment power – an interpretive approach that gives judges much greater latitude.

### What are the Justifications for Substantive Limits on Amendment Power?

The SG courts’ acknowledgement of basic structure rests on compelling constitutional justifications rooted in the core principles of constitutionalism:

#### Constitutional Supremacy Justification

The primary justification emerges from constitutional supremacy itself. In a system of constitutional supremacy (as opposed to parliamentary sovereignty), the constitution stands as supreme law governing all institutions, including Parliament. The constitution enables Parliament; it grants it powers and defines its functions.

*   For Parliament to amend the constitution in ways that destroy the fundamental framework that creates Parliament would be conceptually incoherent – it would be using constitutional power to destroy the constitution itself.
*   Moreover, constitutional supremacy requires that courts serve as the final arbiters of constitutional meaning and compliance. If Parliament possessed unlimited amendment power, it could simply amend away judicial review, separation of powers, or other fundamental features, effectively undermining the entire framework of constitutional supremacy. Substantive limits prevent this self-destruction.

#### Separation of Powers Justification

Whether SOP is conceived as power-sharing or power-division, it is fundamental to Singapore’s Westminster parliamentary democracy. Parliament cannot legitimately use its amendment power to destroy this fundamental principle for three reasons:

1.  Doing so would concentrate all power in Parliament, eliminating the checks and balances essential to constitutional government;
2.  It would undermine the judiciary’s constitutional role as interpreter and guardian of constitutional principles; and
3.  It would violate the principle that each branch’s powers are limited by the constitution that creates them.

#### Prevents Constitutional Destruction

A more pragmatic justification recognises that without substantive limits, a government commanding sufficient parliamentary votes could remake the constitution entirely, potentially transforming Singapore from a constitutional democracy into an authoritarian state – all whilst following proper amendment procedures. The BSD prevents such fundamental transformation by identifying constitutional features that are unamendable or subject to heightened protection.

### Critiques of BSD

Despite the justifications for why we would need a basic structure doctrine, it faces substantial critiques as well, particularly from separation of powers and democratic accountability perspectives.

#### Problem with Judicial Identification of Basic Features

The most fundamental critique is on who gets to decide what is “fundamental and essential” for a Westminster parliamentary democracy model. To operationalise substantive limits on amendment, judges must engage in identifying which constitutional features qualify as basic structure. This identification process raises serious SOP concerns (as well as ROL).

1.  There is the problem of judicial law-making – when courts read limitations into Parliament’s amendment power, they engage in something resembling law-making rather than interpretation. They are creating constraints not explicitly stated in the constitutional text, arguably usurping the legislative function. This is a SOP issue, and also leads to a rule of law issue, since this introduces arbitrariness in how judges decide what are the constraints.
2.  The process of selecting constitutional features involve value judgements about constitutional importance and hierarchy. Why is separation of fundamental but not the specific electoral system or particular rights? These judgements involve policy choices traditionally within the legislative domain, something which even after shifting to SOP as autonomy, the courts are still unwilling to engage in.
3.  There is the concern of constitutional rewriting – In _Kesavananda_, dissenting Justice Ray argued that the Indian Supreme Court, by imposing substantive limits on amendment power, was effectively “laying down a new constitution”. This critique applies equally to Singapore – courts may be remaking constitutional arrangements beyond their proper interpretive role. Ray J further agued that there should be no restraint on the power to amend the constitution, since Parliament as a representative body is given consent by the people, which should not be constrained by judicially implied limitations. It is a matter of judicial modesty not to overstep its limits by reading a basic structure into the constitution – it is likely that this view would align with the Singapore judiciary’s culture of deference and subscription to the green-light theory of judicial review.

#### There must be democratic accountability

A related critique emphasises democratic accountability. Parliament represents the people through elections. If the people, through their elected representatives commanding a two-thirds majority, wish to amend the constitution fundamentally, there is little basis for unelected judges to block them.

*   The BSD allows judges to thwart the will of democratically elected legislators. This tension between judicial protection of constitutional fundamentals and democratic choice has _no easy resolution_.

#### It leads to uncertainty and unpredictability

The “fundamental and essential” standard provides limited practical guidance. Until courts actually adjudicate cases, we cannot know with certainty what features qualify as basic structure beyond separation of powers (which has been confirmed). This creates uncertainty: Parliament cannot reliably predict whether contemplated amendments would pass judicial scrutiny. From a **rule of law** perspective, this unpredictability is problematic, as the law should be sufficiently clear that those governed by it can anticipate its application.

### Singapore should adopt a “thin” basic features doctrine

To strike an appropriate balance, it is suggested that a “thin” basic features doctrine would be desirable in Singapore. Kevin Tan (2017) proposes this as a middle path addressing the competing concerns. It is important to situate Tan’s proposal correctly. It is not a mere description of Singapore’s current position, but rather a normative argument for what Singapore should adopt — and crucially, it operates as a **step up from the current position**. As established in _Ravi_, Singapore’s existing conception is so minimalist that it amounts to little more than a principle of purposive statutory interpretation, providing almost no genuine substantive constraint on Parliament’s amendment power. At the same time, the full _Kesavananda_ basic features doctrine — with its expansive, preamble-grounded, rights-based approach — has been consistently rejected. Tan’s thin basic features doctrine therefore occupies a deliberate middle ground between these two poles: it would replace Singapore’s current near-vacant position (which Tan argues is not a basic features doctrine but a basic structure doctrine) with something that has real substantive teeth, while stopping well short of the broad judicial discretion that the Indian approach confers. This distinction is particularly significant in Singapore’s context as a dominant party state with a relatively low amendment threshold — where political conditions make constitutional entrenchment through electoral competition unlikely, even a modest judicially enforced substantive floor serves a function that procedural safeguards alone cannot fulfil.

The thin approach would operate in three ways:

1.  Protect only minimal constraints – Rather than allowing courts to identify numerous features as basic structure, only a narrow set of absolutely fundamental features would be protected. This limits judicial discretion while preserving protection for truly core constitutional elements.
    1.  BUT: Democratic accountability concern remains — even a thin doctrine allows unelected judges to block constitutional amendments supported by a two-thirds parliamentary majority, which still represents a significant counter majoritarian intervention regardless of how modest the doctrine is framed.
2.  Ground in Westminster structural matrix – Basic features would be identified by reference to the structural requirements of Westminster Parliamentary democracy. This grounds judicial identification in observable institutional features rather than abstract value judgements.
    1.  BUT: It still faces the core judicial overreach problem — even a thin basic features doctrine requires judges to identify what counts as a basic feature. Tan proposes grounding this in the Westminster structural matrix, but this still involves judicial value judgements about what is essential to Westminster governance. The line between legitimate interpretation and judicial law-making does not disappear just because the doctrine is thinner.
3.  Focus on separation of powers and the Bill of Rights – According to Tan, these two features represent the minimum basic structure for Westminster systems. Parliament should not, and cannot, amend the Constitution in ways that would destroy the Westminster form of government based on SOP and the Bill of Rights. Protecting these prevents constitutional destruction while limiting judicial overreach.
    1.  BUT: Tan includes the Bill of Rights as one of the two minimum basic features, but this could open the door to a much broader range of rights-based challenges. Once you protect the Bill of Rights as a basic feature, the doctrine could expand beyond its intended thin scope, potentially approaching the Kesavananda approach it was meant to avoid.

This approach occupies a middle ground. It avoids the expansive judicial power that a full _Kesavananda_ style basic features doctrine would confer on the courts, while also providing a substantive floor beneath which no amendment – however procedurally valid – may go. It acknowledges that some substantive limits on amendment power are necessary to preserve constitutional supremacy and prevent wholesale constitutional destruction, while constraining judicial discretion to identify basic features by focusing on a demonstrably structural, system-defining elements.

### Singapore’s Constitutional Supremacy is Formally Established but Practically Qualified

Constitutional Supremacy in SG presents a complex picture of formal commitment and practical qualification.

The textual basis is clear – Art. 4 and 162 establishes the Constitution as the supreme law. The theoretical justifications as well – logical, procedural and moral – are well established. Limbach’s three traits of constitutional supremacy – the distinction between constitutional and ordinary laws, constitutional judicial review and special amendment procedures – are all present in Singapore’s framework.

Yet, in practice, Singapore’s constitutional supremacy is significantly qualified. The judiciary has historically adopted a deferential posture, with stances such as the presumption of constitutionality (until recently) tilting the balance towards the legislature, ouster clauses exempting certain executive decisions from judicial review and the restricted reading of constitutional provisions like in _Lo Pui Sang_ has sometimes afforded immense practical power to Parliament. The political context of dominant-party governance further weakens procedural amendment constraints, since the governing party routinely commands the supermajority required to amend the Constitution.

On amenability, Singapore occupies an intermediate position. The two-thirds requirement under Art. 5 provides moderate procedural rigidity, but the elaborate three-tiered entrenchment framework under Art. 5A to 5C, designed to protect the EP, remains in abeyance and has not yet taken effect. As for substantive limitations, the courts have recognised that a basic structure of the Constitution exists (including SoP and possibly the right to vote), but have not adopted the full Kesavananda basic features doctrine. The prevailing position is a “thin” or “minimalist” conception that imposes only minimal constraints on Parliament’s amendment power. Whether this thin safeguard is sufficient to protect the essential character of Singapore’s constitutional order – particularly in the context of a dominant party state – is still an open and contested question.

The recent shifts in _Jolovan Wham_ \[2021\] and _Tan Seng Kee_ \[2022\] which weakened the presumption of constitutionality and more clearly asserted the judiciary’s exclusive competence to determine constitutional questions, represent a welcome movement towards a more robust understanding of constitutional supremacy. But whether this jurisprudential trajectory will continue and whether it will ultimately extend to a more substantive engagement with the BFD remains to be seen.

#### Synthesis

Singapore’s evolving SOP stance supports the thin doctrine — Singapore has moved from SOP as separation to SOP as autonomy (as evidenced in _Tan Seng Kee_), meaning each branch is recognised as having its own exclusive sphere of competence. This actually supports judicial enforcement of basic features, because protecting SOP and the Bill of Rights as basic features is precisely what courts are institutionally competent to do — it falls squarely within their role as constitutional interpreters and guardians of the constitutional framework.

But SOP as autonomy also explains the limits of the doctrine — because SOP as autonomy simultaneously recognises that courts should not stray into social or political questions that fall within Parliament’s sphere. This is why Singapore courts have consistently refused to adjudicate on policy questions, and why the full _Kesavananda_ approach is inappropriate for Singapore — it would drag courts into exactly the kind of substantive value judgements about constitutional importance that belong to the political sphere.

Tan’s thin doctrine coheres perfectly with this balance — by limiting basic features to SOP and the Bill of Rights, it keeps courts within their legitimate institutional competence. Protecting SOP is quintessentially a judicial function. It does not require courts to make policy choices or social value judgements — it simply requires them to ensure the constitutional framework within which all three branches operate remains intact.

The courts are already moving in this direction — if Singapore courts are now asserting co-equal constitutional status, rejecting the presumption of constitutionality, and insisting that constitutional questions fall exclusively within the judicial domain, then adopting a thin basic features doctrine is a natural and coherent next step. It would be somewhat inconsistent for courts to assert exclusive competence over constitutional interpretation while simultaneously refusing to protect the constitution’s most essential features from amendment.

The hints of SOP as control are particularly significant — SOP as control implies courts actively checking the other branches rather than merely staying within their own lane. A thin basic features doctrine is precisely the kind of checking mechanism that SOP as control contemplates — courts using their constitutional competence to ensure Parliament cannot amend away the framework that grants all branches their authority.

But the thin doctrine also explains why courts have not gone further — despite these developments, Singapore courts still refuse to adjudicate social and political questions. Tan’s thin doctrine respects this boundary because it limits basic features to SOP and the Bill of Rights — structural and rights-based fundamentals rather than policy choices. So even as courts become more assertive, the thin doctrine provides a principled stopping point that coheres with their continued reluctance to venture into political terrain.

The overall coherence of this position is therefore that Tan’s thin doctrine is not a departure from Singapore’s constitutional tradition but an extension of it — it gives judicial teeth to constitutional supremacy in a way that respects the boundaries that SOP as autonomy establishes.

### Key Cases Table (CS)

| Case / Provision | Key Principle | Significance |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Art 4 | Constitution is supreme law; inconsistent laws are void | Supremacy clause |
| Art 162 | Existing laws construed in conformity with Constitution | Supremacy complement |
| Art 5(2) | Amendments require 2/3 elected MP majority | General procedure |
| Art 5A (in abeyance) | Tier 1: Foundational EP provisions; EP + CPA + 2/3 or referendum | Highest entrenchment |
| Art 5B (in abeyance) | Tier 2: EP discretionary powers; up to 3/4 majority or referendum override | High entrenchment |
| Art 5C (in abeyance) | Tier 3: Indirect circumvention; President may withhold assent | Anti-backdoor safeguard |
| Art 149 | Ouster clause; exempts national security from judicial review | Weakens supremacy |
| Lo Pui Sang [SGHC] | Restricted view of supremacy; “in accordance with law” affords legislative power | Formalist view of SOP as deference and a formalist view of CS – leaning to Parliamentary Supremacy |
| Teo Soh Lung [1989] SGCA | Rejected Kesavananda BFD; procedural view of amendment power | Initial rejection of BSD/BFD |
| Mohammad Faizal [2012] SGHC | SoP is basic structure of Constitution | Basic structure recognised |
| Yong Vui Kong [2015] SGCA | Test: “fundamental and essential to political system”; right to vote possibly basic | Test established |
| Ravi v AG [2017] SGHC | Thin/minimalist BFD only; no right to stand for public office as basic structure | Thin BFD/BSD |
| Jolovan Wham [2021] SGCA | Presumption of constitutionality is “no more than a starting point” | Strengthens supremacy |
| Taw Cheng Kong [1998] SGCA | Independence vested plenary powers in Legislative Assembly | Procedural explanation for constitutional supremacy |

## Representative Democracy

Democracy is an expression of popular sovereignty – the principle that government authority is based on the will of the people, as per Art. 21(3) of the Universal Declaration of Human Rights. The people are the _ultimate source_ of political and legal authority.

Democracy encompasses the broader concept of a right to political participation, which includes both voting at elections and taking part in public affairs (_Thio, Treatise_). Free and fair elections ensure that elected representatives remain accountable to the people. For a government to be politically legitimate, it must not only be elected but must also govern in accordance with democratic principles, allowing for ongoing participation and engagement beyond elections.

*   The right to political participation requires a conducive environment for citizens to be able to participate in public debates effectively. To facilitate such an environment, civil and political freedoms such as the freedom of expression, association, assembly and an independent press is necessary (_Thio, Treatise_).

### Models of Democracy

Democracy, which is fundamentally rule by the people through collective decision making, can be operationalised through different models. These models are not mutually exclusive, any given polity may employ multiple models concurrently.

**_Direct Democracy_**

Citizens participate directly in deciding laws and policies. This model characterised ancient Athens and appears today in mechanisms like referendums and initiatives (e.g., Art. 6 of the Singapore Constitution: no surrender of sovereignty except by referendum). Direct democracy maximises citizen participation but proves impractical for complex modern governance and large populations. Singapore held a referendum in 1962 on merger with Malaysia, but the Barisan Socialis criticised it as a “sham” because there was no option to reject merger entirely.

#### Representative Democracy

Citizens select representatives to govern and make decisions on their behalf. Representatives exercise delegated authority derived from electoral mandate. This model dominates modern democracies including Singapore as it allows governance at scale while maintaining democratic legitimacy through periodic elections.

*   SG’s representative democracy is party-centric, people are exhorted to choose political parties, and the policies that parties offer, instead of individual candidates.
*   Basis – political legitimacy and authority, which is granted by the people to elected officials through elections.
*   In a typical Westminster form of government, there must be a plurality of political parties, so that there is a genuine possibility in alteration of government. However, in the context of Singapore’s dominant party state, Singapore is sorely lacking in a strong opposition.

In Singapore, Parliament is the embodiment of popular sovereignty as it comprises of members who are elected by the citizens. Since 1991, the office of the (elected) President has become another locus of popular sovereignty.

*   Elected officials appear to act as experts / trustees, rather than as direct agents – MPs are expected to decide not purely based on the will of their constituents, but to assess their objective needs and do what they think is best as a whole, using their discretion.

**_Participatory/Deliberative Democracy_**

Citizens, law-makers, and political actors engage in deliberative processes before reaching decisions. This model emphasises reasoned debate and consensus-building rather than mere voting, and places emphasis on maximum participation of all citizens in political decision-making (Glenn Patmore). It aims to include diverse voices and ensure that marginalised or underrepresented groups have a say, promotes transparency, and holds elected officials accountable. Elements of deliberative democracy appear in various forms – public consultations, parliamentary debates, civil society engagement, but rarely as the sole or primary democratic model.

*   The participatory model identifies two criteria for establishing a good government – (1) whether an elected government protects individual interests by ensuring those interests are met and enhanced (the protective function – the virtue of a political system is defined in terms of the nature and quality of governmental decisions); and (2) whether an elected government promotes the personal development of each individual (the development function – the virtue of a political system is defined in terms of its effects on the character of its individuals).
*   A key tenet follows: to keep the representative government accountable, the number of opportunities for individuals to participate in political decisions should be maximised so that they may develop the necessary qualities and capacities to enable them to assess the activities of representatives and hold them accountable. Voting and discussion have significant educational value for all citizens.

#### What is SG’s model?

SG is best described as a blend of the protective and elite models.

*   The protective model ensures rulers are held accountable via regular elections, freedom of speech, of press, etc. (Patmore).
*   The elite model advocates a narrow conception – the crucial function of democracy is not to invest citizens with the power to decide political issues but to provide for the **selection of representatives**.
*   Competition by potential decision-makers is the distinctive feature; the electorate does not control the elected except by replacing them with alternative leaders at an election.

There is a possible argument that Singapore is moving away from even the key tenets of the elite model, thereby diminishing the quality of representative democracy.

### Dahl’s Five Essential Characteristics

Robert Dahl identified five essential characteristics that make democracy effective and meaningful. These provide analytical tools for evaluating whether SG’s institutional arrangements promote or hinder effective representative democracy:

1.  Effective participation – Citizens must have adequate and equal opportunities to express their preferences, place matters on the political agenda, and influence political outcomes. Participation extends beyond merely casting votes, it encompasses genuine capacity to engage in political discourse, organise, advocate and influence decision-making processes.
2.  Voting equality – When decisions are made, each citizen’s vote must count equally. No one’s vote should carry more weight than another’s, ensuring that democratic outcomes reflect the aggregated preferences of citizens as equals.
3.  Enlightened understanding – Citizens must have adequate opportunities to discover and validate their preferences through access to information, diverse perspectives, and reasoned debate. Democracy functions poorly when citizens cannot make informed judgements because information is supressed, manipulated or unavailable. This can be linked to media coverage of political issues and freedom of speech.
4.  Agenda control – Citizens must have the capacity to shape the political agenda, to determine which matters receive political attention and action. If political elites completely control which issues are considered, citizens become passive responders rather than active shapers of political life.
5.  Universal suffrage – All adult citizens, regardless of socio-economic status, gender, ethnicity, or other factors, have the right to vote and participate in public affairs. Universal suffrage prevents the disenfranchisement of marginalised communities and guarantees governance reflecting the interests of the entire population.

These characteristics ground analysis in democratic theory rather than mere assertion. For example: Do NCMPs promote effective political participation? Does the GRC system undermine voting equality? Does media regulation affect enlightened understanding?

*   The right to political participation requires a conducive environment for citizens to participate in public debates effectively. To facilitate such an environment, civil and political freedoms such as the freedom of expression, association, assembly, and an independent press are necessary (_Thio, Treatise_).

### Pitfalls of Democracy

While democracy is the foundation of legitimate governance, it is not without pitfalls. These include: (1) inefficiency, decision making can be slow due to consensus-building and debates; (2) short-term focus, politicians in competitive democracies may prioritise short-term gains to secure re-election, neglecting long-term planning; (3) lack of expertise, where elected officials may not always possess the necessary knowledge for complex policy decisions; (4) corruption, where the electoral process and campaign financing can create opportunities for undie influence; (5) voter apathy and ignorance – where low turnout and lack of understanding undermine the democratic legitimacy. This is countered in Singapore by compulsory voting, though this raises the question of whether citizens forced to vote without informed views produce meaningful democratic outcomes; (6) polarisation – where intense political divisions can lead to gridlock; and (7) fake news – where misinformation manipulates public opinion and undermines voters’ ability to make correctly informed decisions.

### Popular Sovereignty

Popular Sovereignty is the principle that ultimate political authority emanates from the people and serve as the foundation for representative democracy (the idea of government derived from the will of the people).

In constitutional democracies, government does not rule by divine right, hereditary succession or raw force. The Government’s authority derives from the people’s consent, expressed through universal suffrage. Elections serve as the mechanism through which the people delegate governing authority to representatives, creating political legitimacy – the moral authority to govern and make binding decisions on behalf of the community.

*   Popular sovereignty also operationalises as a political check on governmental power. If citizens disapprove of how government exercises authority, they can vote that government out of office at the next election. This accountability mechanism constrains governmental action – elected officials know they face electoral consequences for unpopular decisions. This check complements the legal and institutional checks provided by SOP and ROL.
*   For this political check to function effectively, citizens must have genuine choices at elections. If only one party or candidate runs, or if all realistic options represent similar positions, electoral accountability breaks down. Voters need meaningful alternatives to exercise effective choice – leading directly to the importance of competitive elections and opposition representation.

In Westminster parliamentary democracies characterised by executive-legislature fusion, the political check becomes especially important. Because the executive emerges from and typically dominates the legislature (when the governing party holds a majority), institutional checks between these branches weaken substantially. The primary remaining check operates through elections, where voters can replace the governing party with the opposition. But this check functions only if viable opposition exists and faces realistic prospects of forming an alternative government.

*   The practical operation of this check in Singapore is further complicated by what Cherian George describes as a “legal-bureaucratic machinery” that imposes asymmetric costs on opposition politicians — not through threats to life and limb, but through “criminal prosecutions and civil suits” whose chilling effect “goes beyond these individuals, and cripples the opposition’s ability to attract serious candidates” (Cherian George, 2020). Critically, George cites the US State Department’s assessment that the PAP has maintained dominance “in part by developing genuine voter support through honest, effective administration... and, in part, by manipulating the electoral framework, intimidating organised political opposition, and circumscribing the bounds of legitimate political discourse and action” (Cherian George, 2020). From a constitutional standpoint, this matters because popular sovereignty’s function as a political check presupposes not just formal electoral machinery but a competitive political environment in which the governing party faces credible accountability. Where the costs of opposition politics are structurally elevated through legal mechanisms, the electoral check is formally preserved but substantively weakened — which in turn affects how much weight the argument for political constitutionalism over legal constitutionalism can legitimately bear. If Parliament’s democratic mandate is itself produced by structurally constrained competition, deferring constitutional questions to Parliament on grounds of democratic legitimacy becomes harder to justify.

### Why Opposition Matters – Constitutional Arguments For Opposition

Opposition parties and opposition representation in Parliament serve critical constitutional functions in Westminster parliamentary democracies. The arguments for the opposition’s importance is not merely about political preference – it is connected directly to constitutionalism’s principles:

#### Accountability and SOP

Opposition MPs function as representatives of the electoral minority – citizens whose votes did not translate into government formation. In Westminster systems, where the executive and legislature are fused, opposition representation is especially critical. Without opposition, the fusion could enable executive dominance or executive aggrandisement – where executive power is unchecked by effective parliamentary scrutiny. The government commands majority votes and government MPs generally support executive proposals through party discipline.

*   Only opposition MPs, not bound by the government party whip, can provide meaningful parliamentary checks on executive power. This connects directly to SOP principles, as meaningful parliamentary opposition helps maintain the principle by ensuring some parliamentary check on executive actions, even within the context of fusion.

#### Effective Participation and Representative Democracy

Effective democracy requires more than merely casting votes. Citizens must have meaningful choices informed by diverse perspectives and genuine alternatives. Opposition provides that alternative voice and alternative policy options – credible alternatives to the government of the day, giving citizens authentic choices about who should govern and which policies should be pursued.

Without opposition, participation becomes hollow – voters go through the motions of voting without meaningful choice between the different visions of governance.

#### Representation of diverse views and perspectives (Minority Rep.)

Singapore is religiously, ethnically and ideologically diverse. Opposition representation helps ensure that diverse opinions and perspectives find voice in Parliament, rather than leaving parliamentary debate dominated by a single party’s outlook.

This representation function takes on particular importance in contexts where the governing party exercises strict party discipline – if all government MPs must toe the party line and opposition representation is weak or absent, Parliament can become an echo chamber rather than a forum for genuine deliberation.

#### Prevention of Arbitrary Decision-Making and ROL

Opposition scrutiny helps prevent arbitrary decision-making by the government. When opposition can question government policies, demand justifications and expose weaknesses in government reasoning, the quality of decision-making improves. The Government must provide reasons, respond to challenges and justify its actions.

This accountability mechanism aligns with ROL principles – the government must act according to law and reason, not their own arbitrary will. Without opposition, the risk of arbitrary action – action not grounded in legal authority or rational justification – increases.

There is no better alternative than opposition that can properly provide good checks and balances. NCMPs and NMPs may be part of a transition to a stronger opposition, but only _part_ of the transition. They can indeed increase participation, but do not really impact decision-making in the way that a genuine opposition can.

### SG’s Representative Democracy Model

#### Trustee Model of Representation

Singapore conceives of MPs as trustees of the electorate rather than pure delegates. This distinction carries significant implications. Delegates are bound to vote according to the constituents’ expressed preferences – they act as agents carrying out the principal’s instructions.

*   However, trustees exercise independent judgement about what is best for the nation, even when this conflicts with constituent preferences. As the SGCA held in _Vellama_ at \[79\], “the member is not the mouthpiece but the voice of the people of the constituency”. Instead of directly relaying all concerns of their constituents, elected representatives in Singapore are meant to exercise independent judgement, make informed decisions based on expertise, safeguard minority interests, and ensure long-term national stability over short-term populist demands.
*   The trustee model tends to be more elite-driven, since elected officials are held to a higher standard and expected to be of a certain expertise such that they can make informed decisions. This model grants MPs substantial discretion in judgement and reduces direct accountability to constituent preferences.

This trustee model finds its clearest ideological articulation in what Cherian George (2020) identifies as the PAP’s foundational “notion of trusteeship”: the government “does not view its role as being to respond to people’s expressed preferences at any one time” but rather, as Goh Chok Tong stated, “as a custodian of the people’s welfare, it exercises independent judgment on what is in the long-term interests of the people and acts on that basis” (_Air Conditioned Nation Revisited_). Constitutionally, this is significant: it means that electoral mandate in Singapore is understood not merely as authorisation to implement voter preferences, but as conferring on elected representatives the authority — and indeed the duty — to override those preferences where long-term national interests so require. This is the precise constitutional logic underpinning the _junzi_ model, and it explains why the court in _Vellama_ framed the MP’s role in terms of voice rather than mouthpiece — the trusteeship model is not merely a political preference of the PAP but has been judicially endorsed as Singapore’s constitutional conception of representative government.

*   Critically however, as George observes, the trustee model creates a structural accountability gap: where the government consistently prioritises its own assessment of long-term interests over expressed constituent preferences, **the electoral check that popular sovereignty is supposed to provide weakens** — and the legitimacy of that trusteeship depends entirely on whether the government’s independent judgment is in fact correct and honest, rather than self-serving.

#### Party-Centric System

Singapore’s trustee model is also layered with a strong emphasis on political parties rather than individual MPs. Several features illustrate this party-centricity:

**Strict party whip enforcement**

MPs cannot vote according to personal conscience when the party whip is applied. They must toe the party line or face consequences. The whip is strictly enforced and rarely lifted, preventing conscience-based voting in most situations.

**Constitutional anti-defection provisions**

Art. 46 of the Constitution makes floor-crossing illegal. If an MP ceases to be a member of the party they represented at election, they automatically vacate their parliamentary seat. This constitutionalises party loyalty above individual representative discretion.

**Electoral focus on parties**

In practice, Singaporeans vote for parties, not individuals (despite technically voting for individual candidates). Because party affiliation determines which team forms government and because party discipline is strict, voters understand that supporting a candidate means supporting that candidate’s party platform and leadership.

##### Political Parties are the Fundamental Element under SG’s model

Former PM Lee Hsien Loong drew a sharp contrast between Edmund Burke’s Westminster model and Singapore’s adaptation. Under Burke’s model, voters choose an MP as their constituency representative; MPs come together and exercise their own judgement to form the government; and the focus is on individual MPs who have the final say.

Under Singapore’s model, when choosing to form the government, political parties are the fundamental element. The MP is morally obliged to support the party he fought under (and is also obliged to, through the party whip and the anti-hopping provision). The party forms the government, not discrete individuals. The party has a mandate, given by the voters, who indirectly by voting for the MPs were also voting for that party to form the government.

Under Burke’s model, If MPs collectively withdraw support from the government and regroup to form a totally new government, they are allowed to do so without going back to the electorate. Under Singapore’s model, if an MP changs sides or is expelled from his party, he loses his seat because he no longer represents the party under whose symbol he was voted in.

*   If a seat falls vacant mid term, the PM has full discretion as to when to call the election; a vacancy does not affect the mandate of the government, nor its ability to deliver on its promises.

This system is designed to do two important things

1.  It encourages voters to think carefully during general elections, not just picking an MP but also voting for the government.
2.  It is designed to maximise the chances of a stable, effective government – whichever party wins gets a five-year term mandate to govern, giving it time to reframe policies to meet changed regional, international, social and economic circumstances. Even in the extreme situation where the MP vacating his seat results in the government losing a majority, the government continues to govern so long as it retains the confidence of the House.

#### Implication of Party-Centric Trustee Model on Representative Democracy

This party-centric trustee model has several implications.

There is reduced direct accountability: MPs answer more to party leadership than to constituents, and party discipline constrains MPs’ capacity to represent constituent interests when these conflict with party positions.

There is clarity and coherence: the model promotes clear party platforms and coherent policy implementation. However, there is also a potential for representation gaps: when parties enforce strict discipline and MPs serve as trustees rather than delegates, some constituent interests may go unrepresented if they do not align with party positions. This gap increases when opposition representation is weak.

## Right to Vote + By-Elections

### Constitutional Right to Vote

Although there is no express right to vote, the SGCA in _Daniel De Costa_ \[2020\] has recognised that the right to vote is a constitutional right, either as a matter of construing the Constitution in its entirety, or as a matter of necessary implication in light of references to elections contained in Art. 66 and 39(1). This determinatively affirmed prior judicial and ministerial statements.

*   Minister K. Shanmugam stated in Parliamentary Debates that voting is the foundation of representative democracy, which is the essence of Singapore’s political system, and that the right to vote is a constitutional right implied from various provisions including Art. 65 and 66.
*   Minister Wong Kan Seng similarly stated that the right to vote at parliamentary and presidential elections is implied within the structure of the Constitution and is fundamental to a representative democracy and parliamentary form of government.
*   The Wee Constitutional Commission described the right to vote as an inalienable right, and recommended adding it to the fundamental liberties chapter (though this recommendation was not adopted).
*   The Parliamentary Elections Act, which regulates voting and elections, further suggests an entitlement to vote.

For the right to vote to be meaningful in a representative democracy, elections must be free and fair (_Daniel De Costa_). This principle establishes baseline standards – electoral processes must be conducted fairly, without manipulation, coercion, or fraud that would undermine the authenticity of electoral outcomes. If elections are not free and fair, the resulting government cannot claim a legitimate mandate from the people, and the connection between popular sovereignty and representative democracy breaks down.

In _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] SGCA, the Court held that the right to vote is a possible example of a feature that is part of the basic structure of the Constitution, since “our constitution provides for regular general elections and establishes representative democracy, to which the right to vote is fundamental” – something “fundamental and essential to the political system that is established thereunder” (at \[71\]).

*   The right to vote is essential to operationalise the constitution and for the political system to function.

### For SMCs, there is a duty to call a by-election

Art. 49(1) of the Constitution provides that “Whenever the seat of a Member, not being a non-constituency Member, has become vacant for any reason other than a dissolution of Parliament, the vacancy shall be filled by election in the manner provided by or under any law relating to Parliamentary elections for the time being in force”.

In _Vellama_ \[2013\] SGCA, the court held that the PM has a duty to all a by-election unless he intends to dissolve Parliament if a seat is vacated in an SMC, within a “reasonable time”, having regard to the role of an MP in the Westminster form of government and the plain reading of Art. 49.

**_Vellama d/o Marie Muthu v AG_** \[2013\] 4 SLR 1

Vellama was a voter of Hougnag SMC. The SMC’s MP resigned his party, and his seat was declared vacant. Vellama instituted judicial review proceedings for a mandatory order requiring the PM to advise the President to issue a writ of election. The SGCA held that the PM has a duty to call a by-election (unless he intends to dissolve Parliament in the near future) if a seat is vacated in an SMC, within a “reasonable time”, having regard to the role of an MP in the Westminster form of government and the plain reading of Art. 49.

The SGCA articulated the essential features of the Westminster model at \[79\]: the party commanding majority support in Parliament has the mandate to form the government; the authority of the government emanates from the people, with each member representing the people of the constituency who voted him into Parliament; **voters of a constituency are entitled to have a Member representing and speaking for them in Parliament**; the MP is **not just the mouthpiece but the voice of the people**; when a candidate is elected as MP, he reflects two functions of the voter – **seeking to elect a particular person and seeking to elect a group of individuals of the same party** as the government; an MP must listen to the grievances of his constituents and seek to persuade those in authority to provide remedies; and an MP should have no regard to the political leanings of his constituents – he represents those who voted for him as much as those who voted against him or did not vote at all.

However, the PM has discretion as to when to call a by-election. The SGCA held that just because the PM is accorded a measure of latitude in deciding when to call an election does not mean he can defer the calling indefinitely. Leaving a vacancy unfilled for an unnecessarily prolonged period raises a serious risk of disenfranchising the residents of that constituency. There is a need to balance the rights of voters and the discretion vested in the PM. Judicial intervention would only be warranted in exceptional cases (consistent with the court’s SoP deference). In the current case, only two weeks had elapsed, which was clearly reasonable.

The Court also noted at \[80\] that Singapore has to an extent modified the Westminster model by introducing NCMPs, GRCs, etc. The Constitution emphasises the political party rather than individual MPs – should an MP cease to be a member of the party under whose banner he was elected, he shall also cease to be an MP, as per Art. 46(2)(b).

#### Vellama supports Constitutional Supremacy

In _Vellama_ \[2013\] SGCA, the court drew an important distinction between the UK’s parliamentary sovereignty and Singapore’s constitutional supremacy. In the UK, Parliament is supreme and courts cannot declare Acts of Parliament unconstitutional. In Singapore, the Constitution is the supreme law; courts can and must review governmental action (including by the PM) for constitutional compliance. This constitutional supremacy framework empowers and obligates courts to ensure that governmental discretion is exercised consistently with constitutional principles, including the right to vote and representative democracy.

#### Vellama affirms the rule of law but also creates concerns

From a rule of law perspective, _Vellama_ affirms that governmental discretion has legal limits – all power is subject to legal constraints, even the PM’s discretion over by-election timing. This supports the rule of law principle that government must act according to law, not arbitrary will, and that courts can examine exercises of discretion.

However, the vagueness of the “reasonable time” standard can create a rule of law concern. The standard provides limited _ex ante_ guidance – while we know for sure two weeks is reasonable, it becomes unclear what is unreasonable. This uncertainty hampers citizens’ and government’s capacity to plan conduct with clear legal standards in mind. Some critics argue the Court should have imposed a more specific timeframe (e.g. 90 days or 6 months) rather than leaving the standard entirely open-ended, as the current approach gives the executive substantial room to delay by-elections for tactical political advantage while still complying with “reasonable time”.

#### Vellama indicates a balanced view of SOP

_Vellama_ does indicate judicial willingness to enforce constitutional principles (such as representative democracy and the right to vote) against executive discretion, whilst simultaneously deferring to executive judgement about timing. The Court asserted that it has authority to review the PM’s exercise of discretion and identified a legal duty, but the duty’s content of reasonable time grants substantial discretion.

*   This represents a balance between judicial review and executive prerogative – a balance critics might view as leaning too far toward executive discretion.

### There is no duty to call a by-election for a GRC seat
