In _Wong Souk Yee_ \[2019\] SGCA, Madam Halimah Yacob, the sole minority member of the Marsiling-Yew Tee GRC, resigned to contest the presidential elections with no by-election called. The SGCA held that there is no duty to call a by-election in GRC vacancies if only one MP vacates his seat, even if that MP is the minority member, as Art. 49(1) applies only to SMC seats.

**_Wong Souk Yee v AG_** \[2019\] 1 SLR 1223

Madam Halimah Yacob, the sole minority (Malay community) PAP member in Marsiling-Yew Tee GRC, resigned her parliamentary seat to contest the 2017 Presidential Election. No by-election was called. The appellant, a resident of the GRC and member of the Singapore Democratic Party, sought leave to apply for: (a) a mandatory order compelling the remaining three MPs to vacate their seats and a by-election to be called; (b) a declaratory order that s 24(2A) of the Parliamentary Elections Act (PEA) be interpreted as requiring all remaining members to vacate when one member vacates; and (c) in the alternative, a declaration that s 24(2A) PEA was void for inconsistency with Art 49(1) of the Constitution.

The Court held that there is no duty to call a by-election when only one member of a GRC vacates his or her seat, even if that member is the sole minority member. Art 49(1) of the Constitution, on its proper interpretation, applies only to SMC seats and not GRC seats. The GRC scheme is meant to ensure a multi-racial Parliament, not a multi-racial team within each constituency. Section 24(2A) of the PEA provides that no writ of election shall be issued to fill a GRC vacancy unless all members of that GRC have vacated their seats. The rationale is to prevent one member from holding other members of the team to ransom. The GRC scheme is meant to ensure a multi-racial Parliament, not a multi-racial team.

#### Wong Souk Yee follows purposive interpretation

The SGCA considered three possible interpretations of Art. 49(1) in the GRC context:

1.  that a vacancy as and when it arises shall be filled by a by-election for all GRC seats;
2.  that a vacancy shall only be filled when all GRC seats are vacated; or
3.  that “seat of a Member” refers only to SMC seats, and Art. 49(1) does not apply to GRC seats at all.

The court adopted interpretation (3).

The SGCA did not apply literal textualism. It explicitly applied the purposive approach mandated by s 9A of the Interpretation Act and the framework from _Tan Cheng Bock_ \[2017\] SGCA. The court’s path to Interpretation (3) was the product of purposive reasoning constrained by the Constitution’s nature, not of reading the text of Art 49(1) in isolation.

##### Step 1 — Ambiguity:

The SGCA found Art 49(1) ambiguous on its face in relation to GRCs. Art 49(1) was enacted in 1965 when only SMCs existed and remained substantively unchanged since. Read together with Art 39A (which requires GRC elections to be held “on a basis of a group”) and Art 46 (which exhaustively lists grounds for vacating a seat without providing that a single GRC vacancy triggers the vacation of the remaining seats), the Constitution was conspicuously silent on how Art 49(1) applied to GRCs. It was also common ground between the parties that there had been a legislative oversight in implementing the GRC scheme. This ambiguity permitted resort to extraneous material.

##### Step 2 — Parliamentary Intention from Extraneous Material:

The SGCA consulted the 1988 Parliamentary debates. Then First Deputy Prime Minister Goh Chok Tong had expressly addressed the minority vacancy scenario, stating that GRCs are meant to ensure a multi-racial Parliament, not a multi-racial team, and that requiring a by-election when a minority member vacates would allow that member to hold the rest of the team to ransom. Parliament had thus deliberately chosen to accept the risk of reduced minority representation as an acceptable trade-off for preventing this. Parliament’s intended outcome was clear: no by-election for a single GRC vacancy.

##### Step 3 — Why Neither Rectifying nor Updating Construction Was Available:

The SGCA rejected both rectifying and updating constructions, departing from the High Court which had applied both. While Parliament’s intended outcome (no by-election) was clear from the debates, it was not clear how Parliament intended to achieve this — whether by amending Art 49(1), relying on Art 39A to govern all GRC matters, or relying on Art 49(1)’s reference to “manner provided by law” to incorporate s 24(2A) PEA. Without sufficient certainty as to what words Parliament would have inserted (required for a rectifying construction) or whether Parliament intended Art 49(1) to be amended at all (required for an updating construction), neither tool could be applied — especially given the fundamental and entrenched nature of constitutional provisions. This left only Interpretations (1) and (3) as the remaining possibilities.

##### Step 4 — Choosing Between Interpretations (1) and (3):

Interpretation (1) was antithetical to the purposive approach because it led to the one result Parliament had expressly intended to avoid — the by-election obligation triggered by a single vacancy. Interpretation (3) was not ideal in leaving the Constitution silent on GRC vacancies, but it accurately reflected the historical reality that Art 49(1) was drafted with only SMCs in mind and did not require adjustments to the constitutional text. The Court adopted (3) as the only permissible purposive interpretation.

#### The SGCA’s decision in Wong Souk Yee is problematic as it creates internal tension

The real tension lies in Parliament’s own legislative design. Parliament invoked minority representation as the constitutional justification for Art. 39A and the GRC scheme, yet simultaneously built in a mechanism that allows that minority representation to lapse mid-term without any remedy. Parliament expressly accepted this as a trade-off against the risk of members holding teammates to ransom. The GRC scheme thus carries an internal contradiction from its inception: the stated constitutional purpose (ensuring minority representation) and the operative rules (no by-election for a single vacancy) can and do conflict when the minority member is the one who vacates.

*   Cherian George’s observation is instructive here: constitutional innovations in Singapore — including the GRC system — have often served to “protect Singapore governance from the vagaries of public opinion and the popular vote”, with the stated rationale (minority representation) operating alongside unstated institutional design objectives (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, 2020). Read in this light, the GRC scheme had a dual function from inception: a stated minority representation rationale and unstated structural effects that advantaged the incumbent party. The outcome in _Wong Souk Yee_ — where the court upheld a result that hollows out the stated rationale — is therefore less a judicial failure than a reflection of the asymmetry built into the scheme’s original design.

##### Rule of Law – Clarity and Predictability Concerns

The outcome exposes a rule of law concern going to the clarity and predictability limb. The legislative oversight acknowledged by both parties — that Parliament intended no by-election for a single GRC vacancy but failed to expressly provide for this in the Constitution — means that the constitutional position on GRC vacancies was left genuinely indeterminate until this litigation. Citizens, MPs, and political parties could not readily ascertain from the constitutional text alone what would happen if a sole minority GRC member vacated mid-term. This is precisely the kind of legal uncertainty that _Chng Suan Tze_ identified as contrary to the rule of law – the law must provide clear standards allowing people to plan conduct and predict legal consequences.

The SGCA itself acknowledged this, noting that “it would obviously be more desirable for this lacuna to be addressed by an amendment to the Constitution to expressly deal with vacancies in the seats of GRC Members” (at \[72\]). The court’s endorsement of Interpretation (3) thus resolves the immediate question but does so in a manner that leaves the broader constitutional framework formally incomplete. From a thin rule of law perspective, this is defensible — the court acted within its constitutional role and applied a purposive interpretation that did not rewrite the text. But the existence of the lacuna itself, left unaddressed by Parliament since 1988, reflects a failure of rule of law values at the legislative level.

##### There is a thick RoL issue for minority representation

From a thin rule of law perspective, _Wong Souk Yee_ is formally defensible: the government acted pursuant to legal authority, followed proper procedures, and the court applied a recognised interpretive methodology. Procedural regularity is satisfied.

However, this illustrates the limits of Singapore’s formalistic approach to the rule of law. Procedural regularity is satisfied even where the substantive constitutional value underlying the scheme — minority representation — is left without protection. A thicker conception of rule of law, which requires that law conform to substantive standards of justice, would ask whether a legal framework that permits this outcome can be said to give genuine effect to Art 39A’s constitutional purpose. The court’s answer — that the scheme ensures a multi-racial Parliament, not a multi-racial team — resolves the textual question but arguably does so at the cost of the value the scheme was designed to protect.

The court’s refusal to impose a remedy — treating the matter as within Parliament’s purview to cure by constitutional amendment — reflects Singapore’s broader preference for political over legal constitutionalism. But it leaves a constitutional gap with no institutional actor obliged to fill it: Parliament has not amended Art 49(1) since 1988, and the court has now confirmed it will not read in a remedy. The minority community whose representation the scheme was designed to guarantee has no legal recourse when that guarantee lapses mid-term.

##### Separation of Powers Issue

Wong Souk Yee illustrates a recurring feature of Singapore’s political constitutionalism: courts resolving the narrow textual question before them while treating the substantive constitutional purpose underlying the broader scheme as a matter for Parliament rather than judicial resolution. The same pattern appears in _Teo Soh Lung_, where the court only asked whether amendment procedures were followed, not whether the purpose of judicial oversight was undermined. In _Tan Seng Kee_ and in _Lim Meng Suang_ the same issue applied – the court considered answering the textual question whilst treating the broader question as one of Parliament’s domain. In each case, the pattern is: narrow textual answer, followed by treatment of the substantive constitutional purpose as non-justiciable, followed by deference to Parliament. This is consistent with Singapore’s thin rule of law framework — the court asks whether the government acted within the letter of the law, not whether the outcome serves the law’s underlying constitutional purpose. The risk, as the minority representation gap in _Wong Souk Yee_ demonstrates, is that this approach can leave constitutional values formally enshrined but practically unenforceable — with no institutional actor tasked with filling the gap Parliament creates.

#### Wong Souk Yee Leads to Potential for Strategic Behaviour

The decision creates potential for strategic behaviour that sits uneasily with democratic principles. A GRC team member can resign to pursue other opportunities (which happened in _Wong Souk Yee_) without triggering a by-election, leaving the remaining team in place for the duration of the parliamentary term. Where by-elections carry political risk for the incumbent party, this effectively allows the ruling party to retain GRC control without returning to voters. The constitutional gap is therefore not politically neutral – it structurally advantages whichever party holds GRC seats, since that party bears no electoral cost from mid-term departures.

This connects to a broader pattern that Cherian George identifies in Singapore’s constitutional design. He observes that the GRC system has functioned to protect incumbent governance from the unpredictability of electoral politics, with the expansion of GRCs raising logistical barriers to opposition competition and diluting the impact of localised voter dissatisfaction (_Air Conditioned Nation Revisited_). The absence of a by-election obligation for individual GRC vacancies fits within this pattern: it is one further structural feature that insulates the incumbent from accountability between general elections. As George notes more broadly, Singapore has demonstrated that political control and economic comfort can be maintained through institutional design rather than overt coercion – old legal frameworks “underwrite the government’s dealings” even when they are rarely invoked. The _Wong Souk Yee_ lacuna operates similarly – it need not be used strategically for it to function as an entrenchment mechanism. Its mere existence shapes the incentive structure within which political decisions are made.

*   If constitutionalism at its most fundamental level is about preventing the concentration of authority and ensuring that government remains accountable, then structural features that protect the incumbent from electoral risk without any institutional actor able or willing to remedy them raise a direct challenge to that core commitment. Singapore’s non-liberal constitutionalism accepts a strong executive as a feature rather than a threat – but **even within that model, the Shared Values White Paper and the junzi tradition assume that the government exercises its authority in the long-term interests of the people rather than in its own institutional interests**. Where constitutional design features begin to look more like incumbent self-protection than good governance, that assumption becomes harder to sustain – and the **trust in government that underpins Singapore’s entire constitutional model is precisely what is put at risk**.

The SGCA was aware of these implications. Rather than treating them as irrelevant, the court explicitly acknowledged that the constitutional gap would “obviously be more desirable” to address by amendment (at \[72\]) — yet Parliament has not done so since 1988. The political constitutionalism model, which reserves such questions for Parliament, thus produces a circular result: the party best positioned to exploit the strategic gap is the same party that would have to legislate it away.

#### Thio’s Arguments on GRC By-Elections

Prof. Thio Li-Ann has argued (Parliamentary Debates, 27 Aug 2008) that by-elections should be called if half or more GRC MPs vacate their seats. Several justifications support this position. First, voters from a GRC ward should be served by a fully-staffed rather than a short-handed team – not holding by elections may compromise the right of GRC residents to be represented by a full slate of MPs. Second, setting the threshold at half addresses fairness concerns: it prevents individual MPs from holding the team to ransom, while ensuring that significant vacancies trigger replacement. Third, and most importantly, if the MP who vacates is the designated minority MP, a by-election should be required – the constitutional purpose of GRCs is to ensure minority representation in Parliament (Art. 39A(1)). The GRC scheme, Prof. Thio argues, is _prima facie_ unconstitutional since it violates Art. 12 of equal protection through discrimination based on geographical residence, but Art. 39A(3) exempts the scheme from Art. 12. If the GRC scheme as a mechanism for guaranteeing minority representation is important enough to justify derogating from a fundamental liberty, there should be a legal requirement to call a by-election when a minority member vacates.

Furthermore, such a requirement would incentivise political parties to be more deliberate and strategic in selecting their minority candidates. Knowing that a minority MP’s departure could trigger a by-election – and potentially jeopardise the party’s hold on the GRC – parties would be more inclined to field their most capable, reliable, and well-regarded minority candidates. This would discourage tokenistic selections and promote better quality minority representation and greater political stability within the GRC framework.

#### Vellama and Wong Souk Yee

SMCs vs. GRCs: representational asymmetry. In SMCs, by-elections must be called within reasonable time. In GRCs, by-elections need not be called at all for individual vacancies. SMC constituents whose MP resigns gain new representation relatively quickly, while GRC constituents whose team member resigns may remain underrepresented for years.

Deference to legislative choice. _Wong Souk Yee_ exhibits substantial deference to Parliament’s institutional design choices regarding GRCs, the Court essentially says Parliament chose not to mandate GRC by-elections for individual vacancies, and courts should respect that choice. _Vellama_ shows less deference, imposing a duty (the reasonable time obligation) not explicitly stated in the statutory text.

#### Critiques of Wong Souk Yee

**Inconsistency with GRC rationale**

The GRC scheme’s primary justification is ensuring minority representation. In _Wong Souk Yee,_ the vacating member was the minority representative. If ensuring minority representation truly matters, the minority vacancy should trigger a by-election to restore it. The decision effectively says minority representation matters enough to mandate multi-racial teams but not enough to require continuous minority presence when the minority member leaves. This suggests minority representation serves as a rationale for creating GRCs (which advantages the ruling party by raising barriers to opposition) but not as a sufficiently important principle to require actual continuous minority representation.

*   The inconsistency identified here is more than a logical tension — it reveals the asymmetric way in which constitutional justifications operate in Singapore’s political constitutionalism. The GRC scheme derogates from Art 12’s equal protection guarantee (as Prof Thio notes, the weight of one’s vote varies depending on whether one lives in a GRC or SMC), and Art 39A(3) exempts the scheme from Art 12 precisely because minority representation was deemed important enough to justify that derogation. The constitutional cost of the GRC scheme — unequal voting weight, higher barriers to opposition — is justified by the minority representation rationale. Yet when that rationale conflicts with the incumbent’s interest in avoiding a by-election, the court holds that Parliament deliberately subordinated it to the ransom prevention concern. The constitutional justification that was invoked to impose costs on everyone is not the same as the one invoked to impose any obligation on the beneficiary of the scheme.
*   George’s account gives this structural texture: he documents how GRC expansion served dual functions, with the stated minority representation rationale “operating alongside unstated institutional design objectives” that systematically advantaged the incumbent (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). Read in this light, the inconsistency in _Wong Souk Yee_ is not an anomaly but a predictable product of a scheme whose stated and operative purposes were never fully aligned. Minority representation was always the justification, not the primary driver — and the outcome in _Wong Souk Yee_ makes that asymmetry constitutionally visible.

**Representative Democracy Concerns**

The decision permits lengthy periods where GRC constituents are underrepresented – they receive less representation than they voted for and that other constituencies enjoy. Moreover, constituents have no choice about the “package deal”: they voted for a full team, and when a member leaves, they cannot decide whether to retain the remaining members or choose a new team through a by-election. This lack of choice further weakens democratic accountability.

*   The political constitutionalism model justifies judicial deference to Parliament on the ground that Parliament derives its mandate from the people through competitive elections. But the “package deal” point reveals a gap in that logic: GRC voters did not vote for a diminished team — they voted for a specific configuration of representatives, including a minority member. When that configuration changes without any electoral recourse, the mandate Parliament holds is no longer the one the electorate conferred. Political constitutionalism’s democratic legitimacy depends on a reasonably close correspondence between what voters choose and what they get; _Wong Souk Yee_ permits a significant divergence from that correspondence to persist for the remainder of a parliamentary term with no remedy available.
*   George captures the broader pattern: voters in Singapore are constrained in their ability to “learn about and discuss their choices fully” and face structural disadvantages “long before the polls”, such that the electoral check on government is already substantively weaker than its formal existence implies (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). The representative democracy concern in _Wong Souk Yee_ adds a further layer — even after votes are cast and a team elected, the composition of that team can change in ways the electorate has no opportunity to ratify. This compounds the accountability deficit that George identifies at the electoral stage: voters face barriers to exercising genuine choice at the ballot box, and then have no recourse if the team they chose is subsequently altered without their participation. Together, these features suggest that Singapore’s political constitutionalism rests on a model of democratic accountability that is more formal than substantive — and _Wong Souk Yee_ makes that tension explicit in constitutional doctrine.

#### Defence for Wong Souk Yee

**Respecting Legislative Design**

Parliament created the GRC scheme with specific structural features, including no provision for individual member by-elections. Courts should respect these legislative design choices rather than judicially rewriting institutional arrangements. If the GRC by-election rule should change, Parliament should make that change, not courts.

*   The deference rationale has genuine force in the GRC context specifically. The design of electoral systems involves polycentric trade-offs — minority representation, team stability, opposition viability, practical mechanics of by-elections — that courts are institutionally ill-equipped to balance. The SGCA’s reasoning in _Wong Souk Yee_ implicitly recognises this: it is not that minority representation does not matter, but that Parliament had already weighed it against the ransom risk and made a deliberate choice. For a court to override that choice by reading in a by-election obligation would not merely fill a legal gap — it would substitute judicial preferences for a considered legislative policy judgment on how to structure electoral democracy. On this view, deference is not abdication but appropriate institutional modesty.
*   However, the deference rationale only holds if Parliament can be expected to revisit and correct its own design choices when they prove inadequate. Singapore’s formalistic SOP stance treating the legislature-executive relationship as fused and the judiciary’s role as confined to interpretation, creates a structural problem here: the institution told to fix the gap (Parliament) is the same institution that benefits from its existence, and no other institutional actor has authority to compel a remedy. As George observes, Singapore’s checks and balances are “weak” not because of the country’s size but because of “deliberate structural design choices,” with the result that the government can insulate itself from sustained accountability without any independent institution able to push back (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). The GRC lacuna is a precise illustration — the court defers to Parliament, Parliament does not act, and the gap remains open since 1988.
*   Indeed, Singapore’s SOP has been moving from pure formalism toward greater judicial autonomy. _Tan Cheng Bock_ \[2017\] signalled greater willingness to engage with constitutional purpose; _Syed Suhail_ and subsequent constitutional law developments suggest incremental movement toward substantive review. But _Wong Souk Yee_ itself sits within the older formalist tradition — the court confirmed it would not rewrite constitutional text even where Parliament’s intended outcome was clear and the lacuna was universally acknowledged. This suggests the shift toward autonomy remains selective and incremental rather than structural. George’s point is apt here: the PAP’s “longevity in office has strengthened its hegemony” to the point where treating “party, government and republic as virtually synonymous” has become the default — and judicial deference to legislative design choices in the electoral sphere is one of the ways this conflation is legally normalised (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). Until the autonomy shift extends to electoral and constitutional design questions specifically, the defence of legislative deference will continue to carry the cost identified above: an unenforceable constitutional value, and no institutional actor tasked with restoring it.

**Practical Considerations**

GRC by-elections for individual vacancies raise practical complexities: should the entire team vacate to enable a team by-election (compounding the representation loss)? Should a by-election elect a single replacement (but GRCs are premised on team elections)? How should opposition parties respond – field full teams or single candidates?

*   These complications justify Parliament’s decision not to require GRC by-elections for individual vacancies.

**Remaining members provide representation**

Unlike SMCs where vacancy means zero representation, in GRCs the remaining team members continue representing constituents. While the team is diminished, representation is not entirely absent. This factual difference justifies different legal treatment.

## Legislature

### NCMPs

NCMPs are opposition candidates who lost in general elections but are appointed to Parliament to ensure a minimum number of opposition voices. The scheme was introduced through constitutional amendment following the 1984 general election, and finds its basis in Art. 39(1)(b read with the Fourth Schedule to the Constitution, implemented through provisions in the Parliamentary Elections Act (PEA).

The NCMP scheme guarantees that if fewer than a specified number of opposition MPs are elected (currently 12), the “best performing losers” from opposition parties are appointed as NCMPs to make up the difference. The conditions are prescribed under s. 52(3A) of the PEA – the candidate must have polled no less than 15% of the total number of votes in their contested constituency; a maximum of 2 from an eligible losing GRC team may be appointed; and a maximum of 1 from a SMC loss. Following 2016 constitutional amendments, NCMPs now possess full voting rights on most matters, including on Bills.

### Justifications for NCMP Scheme

Several justifications have been advanced for the NCMP scheme.

1.  It ensures a constitutionally guaranteed minimum number of opposition members in Parliament, preventing Parliament from becoming entirely dominated by a single party regardless of electoral outcomes.
2.  NCMPs are intended to contribute to SOP by providing parliamentary checks on the executive-dominated government – even limited opposition representation is better than none for maintaining institutional checking capacity.
3.  The scheme broadens opportunities for political participation and representation beyond strict electoral outcomes.

LKY advanced additional justifications in Parliamentary Debates (25 July 1984): transparency – having opposition members in the parliamentary chambers to ask questions would dispel suspicions of cover-ups of alleged wrongdoing; sharpening PAP MP’s debating skills and demonstrating the limits of the opposition – enabling younger Singaporeans who had not witnessed the divisive politics of the 1950s and 60s to learn about what an opposition in Parliament can do.

### Critiques of the NCMP scheme

#### Lack of Political Legitimacy

The fundamental critique centres on political legitimacy and accountability – the core principles of representative democracy. The NCMPs are essentially _unelected – as they lost elections_. They do not possess an electoral mandate from any constituency. In a representative democracy where legitimacy derives from the people’s consent expressed through voting, NCMP’s claim to represent citizens is problematic.

*   As WP chief Low Thia Kiang stated, in a democracy situated on popular sovereignty where political leaders draw their moral authority and legitimacy from the will and consent of their constituents, the NCMP has no place in Parliament – it is a “backdoor to Parliament”.

This problem is intensified following the 2016 amendments granting NCMPs full voting rights on most matters. Before 2016, NCMP’s limited voting rights partially addressed legitimacy concerns – they could speak and scrutinise but not decide on most crucial matters, leading critics to describe them as “second-class parliamentarians”. Now, most NCMPs can vote on Bills affecting all Singaporeans, despite lacking an electoral mandate from any constituency. They exercise decision-making power without electoral accountability.

#### Lack of Accountability

Representative democracy operates through accountability – representatives answer to constituents who can vote them out at the next election. NCMPs face no such accountability – they are not elected by any constituency, and they cannot be “voted out” because they were never voted in in the first place. This severs the accountability link essential to representative democracy. Moreover, it is unclear _whom_ NCMPs represent – elected MPs represent specific constituencies, but NCMPs represent an ambiguous concept of “opposition” or voters who supported losing opposition candidates nationwide.

#### Tension with Popular Sovereignty

Popular sovereignty posits that political authority emanates from the people’s will expressed through elections. NCMPs exercise parliamentary authority _despite losing_ elections – despite not being granted a mandate.

*   This leads to the question of how those who explicitly failed to win popular support legitimately exercise governing authority derived from popular sovereignty.

#### Political Co-optation Concerns

The NCMP scheme might function as a tool for political co-optation that advantages incumbents in several ways.

1.  By bringing the “best performing losers” into Parliament, the scheme gives opposition figures a stake in the existing system – some may moderate positions to maintain NCMP status, reducing their effectiveness as opposition voices. Cherian George characterises the non-constituency seat explicitly as a “consolation prize” – a framing that carries its own critical charge (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). The consolation prize metaphor is analytically significant: prizes are awarded by the party in control of the game, on terms the prize-giver sets. An opposition figure who accepts NCMP status on those terms has, in a structural sense, accepted the incumbent’s definition of what legitimate opposition looks like – a defined role, with defined powers, within a system the incumbent designed. George’s broader observation that Singapore voters want “the opposition to check the PAP, not bury it” captures the dynamic that the NCMP scheme exploits: it institutionalises the checking function at a level that satisfies the voter appetite for oversight without enabling the opposition to develop into a governing alternative. The risk is that NCMPs, operating within a scheme calibrated by the government, are structurally incentivised to perform the checking role rather than build the organisational capacity for power.
2.  NCMPs create an appearance of opposition without substance – the appearance of political competition without the reality of opposition capable of forming an alternative government. This might satisfy calls for more opposition voices while ensuring the government’s dominance remains unchallenged.
3.  The government designed and controls the NCMP scheme’s parameters, allowing it to calibrate the scheme to provide minimal opposition presence sufficient to claim democratic legitimacy while ensuring opposition remains too weak to threaten power. Critics have argued the scheme has a more insidious motivation – to dissuade people from voting for the opposition, perpetuating political hegemony.

In Singapore’s context of single-party dominance since independence, NCMPs ensure some opposition voice exists when electoral outcomes might otherwise produce no opposition MPs. Whether this justifies departing from representative democracy principles remains debatable. However, more recently, opposition electoral performance has improved significantly: the 2020 general election saw the Workers’ Party win 10 elected seats, the Leader of the Opposition position was established (requiring at least 10 opposition MPs), and the 2020 Parliament was the most politically pluralistic – 98 MPs, of which 8 were from WP and 2 NCMPs from the PSP. With realistic prospects of further opposition growth, the original justification for NCMPs – preventing zero opposition representation – has weakened. If electoral trends continue over time, the minimum opposition threshold may be reached through elected MPs, rendering NCMPs redundant.

### Nominated Members of Parliament (NMPs)

NMPs are unelected members of Parliament appointed to serve 2.5 year terms. Unlike NCMPs, NMPs are selected through a nomination and appointment process, not through electoral competition. The NMP scheme is established under Art. 39(1)(c) read with the Fourth Schedule. Up to 9 NMPs may be appointed. A Special Select Committee of Parliament, chaired by the Speaker and consisting of 7 other MPs receives nominations from both the public and specified functional groups, and selects NMPs. The President then appoints the selected nominees.

*   The scheme became a permanent feature since 2010.

Functional groups for nominations include: (a) business and industry; (b) the professions; (c) the labour movement; (d) social service organisations; (e) civic and people sector; (f) tertiary education institutions; and (g) media, arts, and sports organisations. Qualifications require nominees to have “rendered distinguished public service,” “brought honour to the Republic,” or “distinguished themselves” in relevant fields. Members are to reflect “as wide a range of independent and non-partisan views as possible.”

NMPs have more limited voting rights than NCMPs or elected MPs. Under Art. 39(2), NMPs cannot vote on: constitutional amendment bills, supply bills and money bills, votes of no confidence in the government, removing the President from office under Art. 22L and any question on which NMPs are excluded by the Constitution.

#### Justifications for NMP Scheme

The primary justification for NMPs is to inject independent, non-partisan perspectives into parliamentary debates. NMPs are not bound to political party platforms or discipline. As Goh Chok Tong state during the Second Reading, the scheme was intended “to evolve a more consensual style of government where alternative views are heard and constructive dissent accommodated”. Unlike opposition MPs, NMPs can “concentrate on the substance of the debate rather than form and rhetoric. They do not have to play to the gallery. They can be constructive while dissenting, thus contributing to good government”. Because of the party whip and anti-hopping provision, even ruling party MPs are constrained to agree with their party’s stance even if unpersuaded; NMPs face no such restriction.

*   “\[A\]s long as the whip system remains, there will not be enough scrutiny of the Government by ruling party MPs. Meanwhile, opposition gains may provide more of a check-and-balance, but they too are constrained by the need to toe party lines” (Anthea Ong).

NMPs also offer functional representation of various sectors and communities that might not be adequately represented through geographic constituency-based elections – such as professionals, academics, labour representatives, the arts community, voluntary welfare sector, etc…

*   This is not unique to Singapore, the UK HOL’s reform debates centred partly on functional representation.

NMPs have made substantial contributions over the years.

*   Prof. Walter Woon proposed and passed the Maintenance of Parents Act (1994) as a Private Member’s Bill;
*   Viswa Sadasivan proposed a motion reaffirming commitment to the national pledge (2015);
*   When Parliament debated s 377A, NMP Siew Kum Hong and NMP Thio Li-Ann led the opposing sides of the debate;
*   In 2019, A/P Walter Theseira and other NMPs notably proposed amendments to the fake news bill (POFMA).

Senior research fellow Woo Jun Jie (NUS Lee Kuan Yew School of Public Policy) argues that NMPs can continue to add value even with more opposition MPs.

1.  They raise issues that may not have been brought up by elected MPs, particularly as they pertain to vulnerable groups, helping ensure greater parliamentary representation;
2.  They provide an additional set of voices in debates – while elected MPs are necessarily driven by their party values and interests, NMPs can provide non-partisan views, introducing greater diversity and reducing the risk of groupthink.
3.  The NMP is in a unique position by virtue of not being elected, which allows NMPs to offer views that may not always be well received on the ground. This addresses the problem of populism, where some policies are not brought up as they are seen as too controversial for any elected MP to touch. Since NMPs do not have to worry about re-election, they can say the politically unsayable – for example, taking on causes such as equal rights for the LGBT community or environmental changes, where public interest is lacking or views are sharply divided.

**_BUT, the NMP scheme is subject to critiques_**

#### Critiques of NMP Scheme

NCMPs at least contested elections and demonstrated some popular support. On the other hand, NMPs have no electoral connection whatsoever. They are appointed, not elected, and possess no electoral mandate from any constituency. In a representative democracy where legitimacy derives from popular sovereignty expressed through voting, the NMP’s authority is fundamentally questionable. NMPs are not accountable to any electorate – they cannot be voted out, and they do not answer to constituents. This severs the accountability link that is definitional to representative democracy.

As Abdullah Tarmugi (PAP) stated during the Second Reading: the scheme is “tantamount to an indictment of the existing system and of the elected MPs… especially the backbenchers in the House”, “creating the impression that MPs are not really bringing up enough solid and diverse views in this Parliament that we need to being in others”. NMPs risk undermining the moral authority and legitimacy of Parliament by diluting the weight of elected representatives, suggesting Parliament requires externally appointed members to compensate for perceived gaps in expertise or diversity, thus casting doubt on whether existing MPs are sufficiently qualified to comprehensively debate national issues.

The 1966 Wee Constitutional Commission Report is particularly relevant – when presented with a proposal for non-elected minority representatives in Parliament, the Commission responded that the elected chamber should _not_ be diluted by the presence of any member who has not been elected on a general franchise” and that “the proper place for party politicians is in the elected chamber and politicians who wish to have a seat in Parliament should achieve this end by taking part in elections”. Given this strong aversion to non-elected members, the subsequent introduction of the NMP scheme appears inconsistent with the original version of parliamentary democracy, raising questions about whether such appointments weaken the democratic mandate of Parliament.

##### Questionable Neutrality and Co-optation

The claim that NMPs provide “neutral” voices is questionable empirically. In recent years, several NMPs have, after completing their terms, joined political parties (predominantly the ruling PAP) and contested elections. If NMP positions serve as stepping stones to partisan political careers, the neutrality assumption collapses. Dr Tan Cheng Bock questioned the very idea of whether it is possible to find a truly non-partisan individual in Singapore, saying the scheme was “a non-starter from the very beginning.” The biggest criticism concerns the lack of transparency in the selection process: the opacity extends from when each functional group selects their nominee, through the Special Select Committee’s deliberations, to the names submitted for presidential approval. With only one opposition member on the Committee, the chances of being outvoted are always present. The choice of functional groups and corresponding coordinators is determined by the government and not by the people, and the identification of candidates from each group is “highly opaque,” creating what ex-NMP Siew Kum Hong called a “veneer of representativeness”.

*   The core commitment of constitutionalism is limited government: that no single institution exercises unchecked authority. The NMP selection process, controlled by a committee chaired by the Speaker under a Parliament dominated by the executive, means the same political branch that NMPs are meant to scrutinise effectively decides who scrutinises it. This is a separation of powers problem — the checking function is compromised at the point of appointment. Singapore’s non-liberal constitutionalism accepts executive dominance as a feature, but even within that model the NMP scheme is presented as an institutional check that enriches parliamentary deliberation. If the selection filter removes voices likely to challenge executive positions, the check is hollow from inception. The Tang Liang Hong episode provided by Cherian George in _Air Conditioned Nation Revisited_ illustrates this structurally: the appointed channel excluded him, and when he used the electoral channel instead, the legal-bureaucratic machinery was deployed. The implication is that the constitutional system has no route through which genuinely adversarial civil society voices can enter Parliament — which is a limited government problem, not merely a political one.

This connects directly to what George identifies as the PAP’s “reboot-repair-rehabilitate” pattern of managing civil society: following periods of pressure, the government would “co-opt and reward those who were prepared to cooperate with it, thus isolating potential challengers” (Cherian George, 2020). The NMP scheme fits within this pattern precisely. By creating a formal parliamentary channel for civil society, professional, and academic voices — but on terms the government controls — the scheme incorporates the most capable and credible independent voices into the constitutional system in a way that simultaneously validates that system and neutralises those voices as independent critics. George observes that the PAP “discovered the value of an energised civil society” in the 1990s but channelled it through “acceptable channels” — “doing projects with the new Community Development Councils, headed by PAP MPs, was also very much approved of,” while the fate of “genuinely bottom-up initiatives that wanted to remain independent of government control” remained uncertain (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). The NMP scheme is the parliamentary expression of this same logic: civil society participation is encouraged, but structured so that it flows through a government-designed conduit.

*   Singapore’s constitutionalism is frequently critiqued for maintaining liberal democratic forms — elections, Parliament, an independent judiciary — while the substantive operation of those forms diverges from their stated purposes. The NMP scheme fits this pattern precisely. Formally, it broadens parliamentary participation beyond elected members and introduces non-partisan expert voices. Substantively, if it operates as George’s co-optation framework suggests — incorporating cooperative voices and isolating independent ones — it produces the form of civil society participation without the substance of independent scrutiny. This is the same critique that applies to Singapore’s constitutionalism more broadly: the constitutional value (checking executive power through parliamentary deliberation) is formally enshrined but substantively undermined by the conditions under which the mechanism operates, thus undermining the separation of powers feature, which has already been accepted as part of the basic structure of Singapore’s Constitution.

##### Further Criticisms

The scheme is elitist – only those who have “distinguished themselves” can be nominated, overlooking the concerns and viewpoints of the common person.

The scheme is unnecessary – there are other channels for political participation (civil society, public consultations, public discussions);

It could lead to polarisation along sectoral lines, provide a “backdoor entry” for opportunistic individuals, and decrease elected MP’s responsibilities (Dr Tan Cheng Bock);

The NMP scheme actually limits political participation since it dilutes voters’ right to vote by allowing unelected members to participate in legislative processes. Walter Theseira has noted that there will be bipartisan support to abolish the scheme if the day comes where PAP and opposition hold almost an equal number of seats, because unelected NMPs would then be casting the deciding votes on certain Bills.

The relevance of having NMPs to present alternative viewpoints has come under scrutiny. When the NMP scheme was debated in Parliament, there was only one elected opposition MP plus 2 NCMPs; now there are a minimum of at least 12 opposition members. Ex-NMP Siew Kum Hong advocates for the scheme to be eliminated or at least suspended so long as there are at least 10 opposition MPs, since the problem NMPs seek to address is gone – there is robust debate and diverse views in Parliament, hence no longer any need for unelected voices. The mere presence of NMPs in Parliament undercuts the foundational premise of an elected legislature. NMPs serve only two-and-a-half year terms and cannot vote on crucial matters like constitutional amendments, budgets, or confidence motions – these limitations reduce concerns about excessive unelected influence but also raise the question: if NMPs’ influence is deliberately constrained because they lack electoral legitimacy, why have them at all rather than addressing the underlying need through electoral reforms that might produce more diverse elected representation?

### Group Representation Constituencies (GRCs)

GRCs are electoral divisions where teams of candidates rather than individuals contest elections.

Currently, GRC teams comprise between 3 to 6 candidates, and at least one candidate in each team must belong to a minority racial group (Malay, Indian or other minority communities). The GRC scheme is established under Art. 39A of the Constitution, introduced through constitutional amendment in 1988.

Art. 39A(1) empowers the legislature to make provision, “in order to ensure the representation in Parliament of Members from the Malay, Indian and other minority communities,” for constituencies to be declared as group representation constituencies. Art. 39A(4) defines “person belonging to the Malay community” using a self-identification plus community acceptance test: “any person, whether of the Malay race or otherwise, who considers himself to be a member of the Malay community and who is generally accepted as a member of the Malay community by that community.” A similar definition applies for Indian or other minority communities. Under section 27C of the PEA, a Malay Community Committee and an Indian and Other Minority Communities Committee determine whether prospective candidates belong to the relevant community. Critically, decisions of these Committees are final and conclusive and not subject to judicial review – which seems to be an ouster clause that may raise RoL concerns.

Since introduction, GRCs have grown substantially – the maximum GRC size has increased from 3 to 6 members over time. As GRCs expanded, the proportion of elected MPs representing SMCs declined. Secondary objectives articulated over time include: facilitating the introduction of new candidates by pairing them with established political leaders (the “anchor minister” concept), achieving economies of scale in estate management, and promoting multi-racial politics by requiring multi-racial teams.

#### Justifications for GRC scheme

##### Ensures Minority Representation

The GRC scheme’s core justification is grounded not merely in pragmatic concern but also has a constitutional aspect – Art. 152(2) expressly requires the Government to “constantly care for the interests of the racial and religious minorities in Singapore”, making the Constitution not race-neutral (_Ravi_ \[2022\] SGHC). The GRC scheme, enacted under Art. 39A, operationalises this constitutional duty within the electoral framework. In the current electoral system with a Chinese-majority society, majority rule risks producing structurally unequal outcomes for minority candidates if racial block voting occurs. The scheme is thus a mechanism of substantive equality – it moves beyond the merely procedural guarantee that all candidates may stand for election, and proactively ensures that minority communities achieve actual representation in Parliament.

*   Critically, the 1966 Wee Constitutional Commission Report rejected nominated minority members in Parliament, reasoning that “the elected chamber should NOT be diluted by the presence of any member who has not been elected on a general franchise”. The GRC scheme thus represents a constitutionally calibrated solution: it ensures minority inclusion while preserving the legitimacy of the general franchise and the democratic character of the elected chamber.
*   As Goh Chok Tong stated in 1988, the GRC scheme will thus “secure the long-term political stability of Singapore” by ensuring Parliament is always multi-racial and representative, and by encouraging multi-racial politics by all parties – reflecting the constitutional vision of a multi-racial polity sustained through institutional design rather than mere political goodwill.

##### Inter-ethnic power-sharing as a structural constraint on majoritarian excess

Constitutionalism fundamentally entails “the constraining of the government in order to better effectuate fundamental principles of \[the\] political regime” (Whittington, 2008). A core feature of constitutionalism is the protection of minorities, recognised alongside SOP, RoL and judicial independence as essential constitutional principles (_Vellama_, _Tan Seet Eng_). The GRC scheme functions as a constitutional device that institutionalises inter-ethnic power sharing at the elite level, ensuring that political leadership structurally includes representatives from different ethnic communities.

*   By mandating multi-racial teams, the scheme constrains the majoritarian tendency inherent in democratic competition, preventing the concentration of political power exclusively within one ethnic community. This reflects the broader constitutional architecture of Singapore, which reconciles Art. 12 and 152 — equal rights for all citizens alongside a positive duty to protect minority interests — through institutional mechanisms rather than leaving minority protection solely to judicial enforcement or political goodwill. The GRC scheme thus embeds the protection of minorities as a structural feature of the electoral system itself.

##### Prevents communal fragmentation of the political process

The 1966 Wee Constitutional Commission specifically rejected proportional representation on the basis that it would lead to the “intensification of party politics along racial lines and formation of more political parties on communal lines”, creating a “strong incentive to political parties to base their appeal to electorate on purely communal lines, which would perpetuate racial differences”. The GRC scheme addresses this constitutional concern through a different mechanism: by requiring multi-racial candidate slates, it compels political parties to adopt a non-communal, multi-racial approach (Jaclyn Neo).

*   Parties cannot rely on narrow sectarian appeals targeting one ethnic community, because their teams must resonate across ethnic lines. This renders ethnic-based political parties electorally unviable and moderates ethnic discourse by insulating minority parliamentarians from having to directly appeal to their minority group for support. The scheme safeguards the integrity of representative democracy, identified as part of the basic structure of the Constitution (_Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] SGCA), by ensuring that the democratic process produces a Parliament reflective of the multi-racial character of Singapore’s society, rather than one fractured along communal lines.

#### Critiques of the GRC Scheme

##### Whether GRCs are even necessary for Minority Representation

Empirical evidence challenges the premise that minority candidates cannot win elections in SMCs. Critics argue that Singapore had not faced the issue of under-representation of minorities as of 1988 – with 19.75% of MPs being minorities at that time, rising to 27.96% in 2020.

*   Minority PAP candidates have regularly won their elections: for example, a PAP Malay Candidate, Abdullah Termugi, convincingly won an SMC seat in the 1988 general election (73.7%), and in the 2011 Punggol East election, a PAP Eurasian candidate won against two Chinese opposition candidates with 54% of the vote.

The government’s response is that the scheme was pre-emptive in light of perceived voting trends, especially among young voters. However, if minority candidates can and do win SMC elections, the necessity of GRCs specifically for minority representation becomes questionable. Alternative mechanisms, such as internal party quotas committing to field minimum numbers of minority candidates in winnable seats – could achieve the same goal without GRCs.

##### Deviation from Initial Purpose

In 1997, the maximum number of members in a GRC was increased from 4 to 6 to facilitate the optimal functioning of Community Development Councils. This had nothing to do with minority representation or multiracialism, raising questions about the instrumentalization of the scheme. The GRC system has also been used as a recruiting tool for untested candidates who are able to get into Parliament by “riding on the coat-tails of anchor Ministers.” The government’s response is that getting new MPs into Parliament is important for political renewal, and that the stakes are raised for both sides.

##### Raises BTE for Opposition

GRCs raise substantial barriers to entry for opposition parties. To contest a GRC, opposition parties must field full teams of 3–6 candidates, find qualified minority candidates willing to join, commit significantly greater campaign resources (financing multiple candidates simultaneously), and risk losing multiple candidates in a single contest (versus risking only one per SMC). These barriers advantage the incumbent ruling party, which possesses greater resources, an established talent pool, and ability to attract candidates. Opposition parties, typically operating with limited resources and smaller membership bases, struggle to field full teams for multiple GRCs. Empirically, expanding GRC size correlates with increased incumbent vote shares and decreased opposition competitiveness in several election cycles.

*   When arguing that GRCs weaken political competition, the specific mechanisms should be identified: resource barriers (opposition needs more candidates and funding); credibility asymmetries (anchor ministers vs. unknowns); structural manipulation (the Electoral Boundaries Review Committee reports to the PM, creating potential for gerrymandering); and ongoing advantages (ruling party MPs manage Town Councils for 80%+ of Singaporeans living in public housing, providing resource advantage, visibility, and residents’ attribution of estate improvements to ruling party MPs).
*   Cherian George’s analysis of the GRC system exposes a significant rule of law concern. George documents how the expansion of GRCs — from 42 single-seat wards and 13 three-member GRCs in 1988 to only nine single-seat wards by 1997, with 74 seats organised into GRCs of four to six members — was a deliberate raising of the electoral hurdle. While the rules were formally the same for all parties, they “of course put at a disadvantage those that found it harder to field large numbers of candidates.” George further identifies a structural dimension: larger GRCs dilute localised voter dissatisfaction, because the votes tend to match the national pattern favouring the incumbent, whereas in a smaller constituency, even a single pocket of unhappiness could produce a decisive swing against the PAP (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). George situates this within his broader framework of “calibrated coercion” — institutional arrangements that entrench incumbent advantage while appearing procedurally neutral, forming part of a system where the PAP “monopolised real power, and kept the barriers to entry forbiddingly high for potential adversaries.”
*   This raises a rule of law concern that persists even on the thinner conception that Singapore courts have tended to adopt. In _Tan Seet Eng_ \[2015\] SGCA, the Court of Appeal affirmed that the rule of law is “the bedrock on which our society was founded and on which it has thrived,” and reasserted the principle of legality — that the judiciary is tasked with ensuring all arms of government are subject to legal limits and act within the scope of their lawful authority. Following _Chng Suan Tze_ \[1988\], which held that unfettered executive discretion was contrary to the rule of law, the courts have generally adopted a more cautious posture, tending towards a thinner, formalistic conception that focuses on procedural regularity and whether the government has acted within its prescribed legal powers. Yet even on this thinner standard, Art. 39A(3) raises difficulties. By expressly immunising the GRC scheme from challenge under Art 12’s equal protection guarantee, Art 39A(3) functions as an ouster clause that removes the judiciary’s capacity to assess whether the scheme operates within the boundaries of its constitutional purpose — notwithstanding that the scheme seems _prima facie_ discriminatory on the basis of geographical residence, since the weight of a voter’s ballot differs depending on whether they reside in a GRC or an SMC (Prof Thio, Parliamentary Debates, 27 Aug 2008). The principle in _Chng Suan Tze_ — that there should be few, if any, situations where judicial review is excluded — is structurally undermined where an ouster clause places an entire constitutional mechanism beyond judicial scrutiny.
*   The concern deepens on a thicker conception of the rule of law, which requires not merely procedural compliance but substantive standards guarding against arbitrariness, including the protection of individual liberties. On this view, ouster clauses are fundamentally detrimental to the rule of law since they allow the executive and legislature to override judicial authority. If the GRC scheme’s actual operation deviates significantly from its stated constitutional purpose of ensuring minority representation — as evidenced by the 1997 increase in GRC size to facilitate Community Development Councils rather than to advance multiracialism, and by its documented effect of structurally disadvantaging opposition parties — a thick rule of law framework would demand precisely the kind of proportionality review that Art 39A(3) forecloses.
*   As Chan Sek Keong has argued, laws must treat all persons equally and accord them the same rights, privileges and protections, unless a state interest is served or advanced in treating them unequally. The critical question is not whether the GRC scheme served a legitimate state interest at its inception — it plainly did — but whether its evolving operation continues to do so, or whether it has been instrumentalised to serve purposes extraneous to minority representation. Because Art 39A(3) forecloses judicial scrutiny of this question, the assessment is left entirely to the political process — a process which is itself shaped by the very competitive distortions that the scheme produces. The rule of law, in this respect, is formally preserved but substantively strained: the scheme operates lawfully, but beyond the reach of the legal mechanisms that exist to ensure lawful power is exercised faithfully and proportionately to its constitutional purpose.

##### Anchor Ministers and Unequal Competition

The practice of placing senior ministers as “anchors” in GRCs creates unequal competition. Anchor ministers bring high public profile and name recognition, cabinet-level resources and credibility, and an implicit message: defeating the GRC means removing a key minister, potentially destabilising the government. Opposition teams typically cannot match this profile, tilting competition further toward incumbents and weakening the quality of political competition and electoral checks.

##### Political Inequality: Unequal Voting Weight

GRCs create a form of political inequality: voters in GRCs can return more MPs to Parliament (3–6) than voters in SMCs (1) for a single vote. While not violating formal voting equality (each person gets one vote), this creates practical inequality in political representation. GRC voters have more MPs representing them and presumably greater access to parliamentary representation than SMC voters.

##### Dilution of Voter Choice and Entrenchment of Communalism

In GRCs, voters must vote for entire teams, they cannot pick and choose among team members. If a voter supports most of a team but opposes one member, they must either accept the entire team or vote for the opposition. This dilutes voter choice and autonomy compared to SMC elections.

*   Furthermore, the GRC scheme makes ethnic identity highly salient in political competition, it cannot work without explicitly categorising candidates by race. While attempting to institutionalise multiracialism, the scheme arguably politicises race: minority candidates are confronted with questions about their identity and language fluency, and their abilities may be doubted (seen as only part of the GRC to fit the “quota”). This is arguably contrary to the goal of taking ethnicity out of politics.

#### Contextual Considerations

If identity politics and racial bloc voting have become less decisive factors in Singapore elections, as some evidence suggests, the original justification for GRCs weakens. If voters increasingly vote based on party platform, competence, and policy rather than candidates’ ethnicity, minority candidates face less systematic disadvantage in SMCs.

Opposition parties have succeeded in winning some GRCs (particularly the Workers’ Party winning Aljunied GRC in 2011, 2015, and 2020), demonstrating that GRCs do not absolutely prevent opposition success—but this does not negate the argument that GRCs raise barriers and generally advantage incumbents. If the goal is ensuring minority representation, internal party quotas or nomination rules could achieve this without multi-member constituencies and the associated political competition concerns.

#### Summary + Synthesis

Representative democracy in Singapore presents a complex picture of formal commitment to democratic principles alongside distinctive institutional modifications that generate ongoing tensions. The constitutional framework establishes representative democracy founded on popular sovereignty and the right to vote (_Daniel De Costa; Vellama; Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\]), with elections as the mechanism for delegating governing authority to representatives. Robert Dahl’s five characteristics provide the analytical tools to assess whether Singapore’s institutional arrangements genuinely promote effective participation, voting equality, enlightened understanding, agenda control, and universal suffrage.

Singapore’s party-centric trustee model grants stability and coherent governance but also constrains individual MP accountability and creates representation gaps. The strict party whip, constitutional anti-defection provisions, and focus on parties over individuals reduce the scope for constituency-level representation, making the role of opposition—as the primary non-government voice—constitutionally essential for accountability, SoP, and prevention of arbitrary decision-making.

The institutional arrangements designed to address representational gaps each face significant democratic legitimacy concerns. NCMPs provide a guaranteed minimum opposition presence but lack electoral mandate—a tension that intensified when they received full voting rights in 2016. NMPs inject functional and non-partisan perspectives but suffer an even greater legitimacy deficit, with no electoral connection whatsoever and serious transparency concerns about the selection process. The 1966 Wee Constitutional Commission’s strong opposition to non-elected members in the elected chamber underscores the tension between these schemes and Singapore’s founding democratic commitments. GRCs serve the important goal of minority representation but raise serious concerns about barriers to opposition, political instrumentalization, anchor minister advantages, unequal voting weight, and the paradoxical entrenchment of communalism.

The by-election jurisprudence further illustrates these tensions. _Vellama_ affirmed that voters are entitled to representation in a Westminster system, while _Wong Souk Yee_ held that this right does not require a by-election when a single GRC member—even the minority member—vacates, prioritising team stability over continuous minority representation.

The 2020 general election marked a significant shift—the most politically pluralistic Parliament in Singapore’s history, with 10 elected opposition MPs, a formal Leader of the Opposition, and realistic prospects for further opposition growth. This contextual evolution has weakened the original justifications for both the NCMP and NMP schemes and raises the question of whether these transitional mechanisms have served their purpose. Whether Singapore’s representative democracy will continue to evolve towards greater political competition and accountability, or whether the existing institutional architecture will preserve the dominant-party equilibrium, remains the defining question of Singapore’s constitutional development.

### Key Cases Table

| Case / Provision | Key Principle | Significance |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Art 39(1)(b) | NCMP scheme; best performing losers appointed to ensure min. 12 opposition members | NCMPs |
| Art 39(1)(c) | NMP scheme; up to 9 appointed for 2.5-year terms via Special Select Committee | NMPs |
| Art 39A | GRC scheme; teams of 3–6 with at least 1 minority candidate | GRCs |
| Art 46(2)(b) | Anti-defection: MP ceases to be MP if ceases to be member of party | Party-centric model |
| Art 49(1) | Vacancy filled by election; interpreted as applying to SMCs only | By-elections |
| s24(2A) PEA | No writ for GRC by-election unless all members vacated | GRC by-elections |
| s27C PEA | Community Committees determine minority status; decisions final (ouster clause) | GRC minority classification |
| Daniel De Costa [2020] SGCA | Right to vote is constitutional right; elections must be free and fair | Right to vote |
| Vellama [2013] SGCA | PM has duty to call SMC by-election within reasonable time; Westminster model features | By-elections / RD |
| Wong Souk Yee [2019] SGCA | No duty for GRC by-election if only 1 member vacates; no “particular form” of representation is part of basic structure | GRC by-elections |
| Yong Vui Kong [2015] SGCA | Right to vote possibly part of basic structure; “fundamental and essential” test | Basic structure |
| Wee Commission (1966) | Elected chamber should not be diluted by non-elected members | Democratic legitimacy |

## Elected Presidency

The Elected Presidency is a constitutional office created through 1991 constitutional amendments. Prior to 1991, Singapore had an appointed President with ceremonial functions. The 1991 amendments transformed the Presidency into an elected office with specific custodial powers over key governmental decisions.

#### Original Design – Ceremonial

The original design of the Presidency was ceremonial – modelled after the British constitutional monarch. Under the Westminster framework, the English constitutional order has two parts:

1.  The dignified element, which excites and preserves the reverence of the population – this includes the monarchy, traditions, ceremonies and symbols that evoke respect and loyalty.
2.  The efficient element, which is responsible for the actual working of government – the Cabinet, the PM and Parliament, which makes law and implements policies.

The efficiency of the UK system was said to lie in the close union and nearly complete fusion of executive and legislative powers.

In this framework, the head of state primarily serves as a symbol of national unity and represents the country on the world stage.

*   The initial scope of SG’s Presidential powers was accordingly narrow, encompassing three core functions: (1) the appointment of the PM (Art. 25); (2) the power to declare the office of PM vacant when PM resigns or ceases to command a majority in Parliament; and (3) the power to withhold consent to a request for dissolution of Parliament.
*   Although Art. 23(1) vests the executive authority of Singapore in the President, the general rule under Art. 21(1) is that the President acts on the advice of the Cabinet or a Minister acting under the general authority of the Cabinet – in essence, the President’s role was that of a dignified figurehead – constitutionally significant but practically limited in the exercise of independent power.

A key reason for maintaining this ceremonial posture relates to democratic legitimacy (and I think popular sovereignty?) – officials who have not been directly elected, or non-elected bodies would be placed in a highly invidious position if they attempted to block decisions of an elected government. The Westminster system thus presupposed that the head of state would not ordinarily act as an active check on the executive.

### Rationale for an Elected Presidency

The 1991 constitutional amendments fundamentally transformed the Presidency from an appointed, ceremonial office into an elected one with specific custodial powers. The original and primary justification for this transformation centred entirely on separation of powers considerations – specifically, it was the need to create an intra-executive check on the Cabinet’s exercise of power.

Since Singapore has a fusion of the executive and legislature, the Cabinet emerges from and dominates Parliament. This fusion reduces institutional checks between these two branches. In Singapore’s context, three additional factors make the case for an intra-executive check compelling:

1.  The executive-legislative fusion inherent to Westminster systems means that intra-executive checks become especially important to prevent unchecked executive power;
2.  The historical absence of an effective parliamentary opposition meant that Parliament had not provided a robust check on executive decisions, the EP was designed to partially compensate for this weakness;
3.  Single-party dominance since independence heightened concerns about the accumulation of power and potential for executive overreach.

The EP was thus designed to introduce a second executive actor – the President – with the power to block certain Cabinet decisions in defined areas.

*   Crucially, this checking function was conceived as reactive, not proactive: the President does not actively govern, initiate legislation or direct Cabinet to pursue particular policies.

Presidential power operates by blocking or approving specific decisions, distinguishing the EP from presidential systems where the Head of State holds governing authority.

Cherian George offers a more critical genealogy of this rationale. He characterises the Elected Presidency as Lee Kuan Yew’s personal “insurance policy against a so-called freak election” scenario in which a rogue government, however briefly in office, could cause permanent damage by raiding financial reserves or corrupting civil service appointments (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). George’s account underlines the deeply paternalistic logic underpinning the EP’s creation: the institution was designed not primarily out of faith in democratic checks, but out of distrust toward democracy itself — specifically, the perceived unpredictability of democratic outcomes. George argues that Lee’s constitutional innovations in this period — both the EP and the ministerial pay formula — resulted from his “peculiar obsession with protecting the state from the unpredictability of democratic politics” (ACNR, Ch. 7). From a constitutional standpoint, this is significant because it reveals an internal contradiction at the EP’s foundation: the checking function is justified by reference to democratic principles (separation of powers, accountability), but was motivated by anxiety about democratic outcomes. If the EP was born not from a desire for more democracy but from a desire to insulate governance from the electorate, it calls into question how robustly the institution was designed to empower the checking function as opposed to containing its exercise within safe limits.

#### Why Elected, not Appointed?

The decision to make the Presidency an elected office was driven by the logic that the President would need a direct mandate from the people to possess the moral authority and political legitimacy necessary to check another elected body – the Cabinet.

An appointed President would lack sufficient political authority to oppose decisions by a democratically elected Cabinet. Election provides the President with an independent democratic mandate, grounding custodial authority in popular sovereignty. This rationale connects to representative democracy: someone wielding substantial governmental authority, including the power to block Cabinet decisions, must derive that authority from the people through elections.

### President’s Custodial Powers

#### Fiscal Custodianship

The EP has the power to withhold assent to budgets and transactions that would draw down in Singapore’s past reserves – in other words, the President holds the “second key to the safe”. Without the President’s concurrence, the government cannot access the nation’s accumulated reserves.

The rationale for this power was articulated by LKY. In his 1984 National Day Rally Speech, Lee warned that years of careful savings could be squandered in just one election cycle with populist policies (which reflects his fear above) – such as free services, subsidies and handouts – leading to unsustainable spending and eventual tax hikes. He later emphasised in _The Straits Times_ (1984) that even a well-intentioned government might succumb to public pressure and use reserves indirectly through excessive subsidies. The concern was that without proper checks and balances, the executive could override fiscal prudence and engage in unsustainable spending, jeopardising Singapore’s long-term financial stability. The President’s concurrence requirement was designed as a structural safeguard against this risk.

##### These Drawdowns Have Been Used in the Real World

In practice, the President has exercised this power on significant occasions. During the 2008 Global Financial Crisis, President S.R. Nathan approved a S$150 billion guarantee on all bank deposits in Singapore to be backed by past reserves. In 2009, he approved a drawdown of S$4.9 billion to fund the Jobs Credit Scheme, which subsidised employer’s wage bills and the Special Risk-Sharing Initiative which helped viable companies gain access to credit. The government subsequently returned S$4 billion of the amount drawn in 2011.

Most recently in 2020, President Halimah assented to drawdowns totalling S$52 billion to fund the Resilience Budget, Solidarity Budget and Fortitude Budget in response to the COVID-19 pandemic.

#### Civil Service Custodianship

The President’s second custodial function is the power to withhold assent to the appointment of key civil servants and public officers. These include the Chief Justice, Judges of the Supreme Court, the Attorney-General, members of the Presidential Council for Minority Rights (PCMR) and the Presidential Council for Religious Harmony (PCRH), the ISA Advisory Board, the Public Service Commission (PSC), the Legal Service Commission (LSC), the Chief Valuer, Auditor-General, Accountant-General, the Chief of Defence Force, the Chiefs of the Air Force, Army, and Navy, the Commissioner of Police, and the Director of the CPIB. The President also has a role in the appointment of members of statutory boards and directors of government companies. This power is designed to protect the integrity of the civil service by preventing political patronage appointments to key positions.

#### Protecting the Integrity of Anti-Corruption Investigations

The President provides an additional layer of protection in relation to CPIB investigations. If the PM refuses to give consent for the CPIB to pursue an investigation, the President may grant concurrence, ensuring that politically sensitive investigations are not suppressed by the very executive they may implicate. This function underscores the President’s role as a safeguard against executive self-dealing.

#### Swing Vote

In cases where the Cabinet and the relevant Presidential Council or Advisory Board disagree, the President exercises a swing vote. This applies in two critical contexts: restraining orders under the Maintenance of Religious Harmony Act (MRHA), and restraining or detention orders under the Internal Security Act (ISA). In these situations, the President acts as a final arbiter, providing an additional safeguard for fundamental liberties against executive overreach. However, it is important to note that the President is still not an executive President in the full sense – the President’s discretionary powers are balanced against other bodies, most notably Parliament and the Council of Presidential Advisors (CPA) under Art. 37B(1).

### Amendments have trended towards reducing the EP’s powers
