Since 1991, 46 constitutional amendment bills have been passed modifying the scope of the President’s powers along a trajectory that has, on the whole, favoured the reduction of presidential powers (Thio Li-ann, _Singapore Chronicles: Presidency_). These amendments reflect an ongoing recalibration of the balance between the President’s checking function and the government’s desire for operational flexibility.

#### Exclusion of Defence and Security Spending (1994, Art. 151A)

A 1994 constitutional amendment introduced Art. 151A, which excluded defence and security spending from presidential oversight. Under this provision. Art. 22B(7), 22D(6), 148G(2)-(3) and 148H do not apply to any “defence and security measure”, defined as any liability or proposed transaction which the PM and Minister for Defence – on the recommendations of the PermSec to the Ministry of Defence and the CDF – certify to be necessary for the defence and security of Singapore. Crucially under Art. 151A(2), such certification constitutes conclusive evidence and is not subject to scrutiny before a court of law.

*   The rationale advanced for this was multi-layered. Spending on defence and security was said to be “qualitatively different” from the kinds of populist expenditure – subsiding social services or distributing handouts – that the EP mechanism was originally designed to guard against. Maintaining confidentiality around defence and security transactions was also cited as necessary to protect national security.
*   Furthermore, the PM and Cabinet are ultimately responsible for defence and security and in times of crisis, the government needs the ability to act quickly and allocate funds immediately. Requiring presidential approval could hamper rapid decision-making, making Singapore vulnerable to threats demanding an immediate response.

Significant criticism was levied against this amendment. NMP Walter Woon argued forcefully that an irresponsible government could construe “defence and security” so liberally, without being subject to any mode of accountability, as to use public money to buy votes disguised as security measures. He pointed out that national security is “such a wide thing that it would be possible to fit any sort of handout within that rubric if you are intelligent enough. All you need is a smart and crooked lawyer to help you to do this.” He concluded that “to take the power altogether from the President ... opens the door to abuse. There is no point having two keys to a door if you leave the door unlocked”.

Woon also questioned the efficacy of the alternative safeguards under Art. 151A(2), observing that the President – as the holder of the highest of the office in the land – could not easily be bribed or offered inducements, unlike the CDF and PermSec who “may be ambitious people”. The fundamental concern is that this carve-out leaves a back door open for potential abuse: if a strong-swilled government wished to spend irresponsibly and pass it off as defence and security measures, the President would be powerless to intervene. The only remaining check would be political accountability through the electorate – but if political accountability os considered sufficient, it raises the question of what purpose the President’s fiscal custodianship serves at all.

As Thio Li-ann has observed, within a dominant party state, the parliamentary scrutiny that attends bills relating to the drawdown of past reserves (which is the only remaining check once presidential concurrence is removed) is a weak check.

*   The original fear that led to the creation of the EP – that an overly strong Cabinet would have untrammelled power – re-emerges. Individual transactions drawing down on past reserves which are ministerially certified to relate to defence and security would be known only to the Cabinet, requiring trust in government leaders rather than the distrust that checks and balances are designed to address. Indeed, this lines with the _junzi_ that the SVWP emphasises, but in reality – it is a big ask of the population, no matter the circumstance.

#### Transfer Clauses

Government assets may be held by the Government, by statutory boards, or by government companies listed in the Fifth Schedule of the Constitution (such as the Public Utilities Board or Temasek Holdings). It is possible for transfers drawing down on past reserves to be made between these entities. The avowed purpose of the transfer clause amendments was to allow the Government to undertake more timely responses to changing economic and business conditions – for example, reserves might need to be quickly transferred from the Government to the Fifth Schedule government companies to enable the latter to “seize opportunities in new areas of growth”.

Under these amendments, the President is merely informed of such transfers, there is no need for presidential concurrence even where past reserves are drawn down. Concerns were raised that this opens the possibility of reserves from public statutory boards like HDB being transferred to government companies, effectively allowing the Government a free hand to bail out ailing companies without presidential approval. It was countered that internal controls within statutory boards and government companies maintain accountability, with the Confucian value of trust in government once again being a supporting factor – it is “not in the interest of the country to have the specific allocation of the reserves subject to public scrutiny.” This tension between trusting and distrusting governors is a recurring theme: where the accent is on trusting the Government, the powers and role of the Presidency are correspondingly curtailed.

#### Long-Term Real Rates of Return

In 2008, further constitutional amendments were adopted to recalibrate the reserves framework in anticipation of increased government social expenditure. The key concept introduced was the Long-Term Real Rate of Return (“LTRRR”) – estimates of the average returns expected over the next 20 years from government investments. A forward-looking approach was adopted, projecting long-term expected returns over approximately 20 years, rather than relying on historical rates (as the past is not necessarily indicative of future outcomes). The amendment changes the method of computing what is to be saved by applying the LTRRR formula to the broadened base of Net Investment Returns (NIR). Fifty percent of NIR goes to past reserves, of which the President is custodian.

This has significant implications for the President’s oversight role. The Government determines the NIR every year, a process requiring professional financial expertise and sound judgment. This raises the question of whether the President and the CPA possess sufficient information and expertise to make the complex evaluations involved. If the President disagrees with the proposed LTRRR, the default position is that the historical real rate of return over the past 20 years will be used – a “neutral and pragmatic basis.” This reflects the custodial posture of the Presidency: the President assesses whether the Government’s proposal is “reasonable and well-founded” rather than counter-proposing estimates of his own. While there is a layer of presidential oversight, it is quite weak and depends heavily on the financial expertise, prudence, and common sense of the President and the CPA.

#### Suspending Entrenchment Procedures

The initial entrenchment framework was designed to protect the President’s discretionary powers from being unilaterally diluted by Parliament. The original Art. 22H required presidential assent to any Bill passed by Parliament that circumvented or curtailed the President’s discretionary powers, with Art. 5(2A) providing a parallel requirement for constitutional amendment bills.

*   In Constitutional Reference No. 1 of 1995, the question arose whether Art. 22H also covered constitutional amendment bills, given that Art. 5(2A) was not yet in force. The tribunal adopted a purposive interpretation – Art. 5(2A) was intended to be part of the law of the land, and Art. 22H was intended only to apply to non-constitutional legislation. PM Goh acknowledged that Art. 22H had been “incorrectly drafted” and the result was that Art. 22H was narrowed to cover only non-constitutional amendment bills.

After the 2016 amendments (which are still not in force), a complex multi-layered system was introduced under Art. 5A (provisions establishing the Presidency), 5B (provisions relating to the office and discretionary powers), and 5C (other bills). The procedure under Art. 5A illustrates the complexity. An amending Bill must first be introduced in Parliament for a vote. If the President concurs, the Bill proceeds. If the President does not concur, the outcome depends on the CPA’s recommendation. Under Art. 5A(9), where the President disagrees but the CPA recommends concurrence, the Bill may nonetheless pass if it secures a two-thirds majority of the total number of elected MPs. In other scenarios, a three-quarters majority may be required, or a national referendum may be triggered.

*   This was designed to make it more difficult for Parliament to override the President on Amendments affecting the EP itself. However, the fact that these provisions have been held in abeyance and are still not in force raises questions about the practical significance of the entrenchment framework.

#### President is expected to concur with the Government’s plans

In 1999, the Government issued a White Paper on _Principles for Determining and Safeguarding Accumulate Reserves_, which emphasised that a “harmonious working relationship” between the President and the Government is essential. The White Paper set out mutually agreed rules of conduct and stated that safeguards should not “fetter normal operations”. Whilst non-binding, the Government agreed to comply until otherwise notified. The underlying expectation is clear – the President is expected to concur with the Government’s plans and is not meant to serve as an obstruction. This orientation further reduces the practical potency of the President’s checking function.

*   Cherian George provides a detailed account of the events precipitating the 1999 White Paper that sharply illuminates this dynamic. President Ong Teng Cheong took the unprecedented step of summoning the press to the Istana to publicly air his dissatisfaction with how the Cabinet and the bureaucracy had treated his office over six years, stating that he had encountered a “long list of problems” and suspected that officials “consider the elected President a nuisance — checking on them, looking over their shoulder” (ACNR, Ch. 8). George notes the political significance of this: in any country with a functioning plural democracy, such statements would have barely caused a ripple, but in Singapore, they “shook the body politic to its bones.”
*   George argues that what made Ong’s actions constitutionally remarkable was not merely the substance of his disagreements with the Government, but the fact that the President felt entitled to go public — to appeal directly to the electorate that had mandated him. It was “perhaps the direct nature of his relationship to the electorate that made the president feel that it was right and proper for him to account for his tenure directly to the people, without consulting cabinet” (ACNR, Ch. 8). This illustrates a crucial structural feature of the Elected Presidency: the direct democratic mandate creates centrifugal pressures that resist absorption into the PAP’s centralised system of governance. Lee Kuan Yew’s response — that there could be “only one centre of government in the country” — reveals the underlying tension between the government’s claim to undivided executive supremacy and the EP’s constitutional design as an independent checking institution with its own democratic mandate.
*   George further observes that the White Paper and the earlier Constitutional Reference to the Supreme Court were, in their own way, concessions to the President’s independence. Both represented recourse to “legalistic and public mechanisms” — a departure from the government’s preferred mode of “behind-the-scenes settlements” — and constituted an “implicit acknowledgment that the new-style president did not fall within its chain of command” (ACNR, Ch. 8). The White Paper’s emphasis on harmonious cooperation can thus be read as the government’s attempt to re-establish soft constraints over an institution it could no longer fully control through internal party or civil service discipline. This practical episode gives concrete weight to the theoretical critique that the EP’s checking function has been progressively constrained not through formal constitutional amendment alone, but through soft institutional norms designed to align the Presidency with Cabinet’s preferences.

#### Mandating Consultation with CPA

Since 2016, Art. 37IA(1) requires the President to consult the CPA before exercising any discretionary power conferred by the Constitution, with certain exceptions. The CPA is not elected – its members are nominated by the President, the Prime Minister, the Chief Justice, and the Chair of the Public Service Commission.

The original design of the Elected Presidency was to provide a democratic mandate to the President. However, the President is now bound to consult the CPA, and it remains unclear whether the CPA’s advice is purely advisory or whether it carries a legal effect that constrains the President’s decision-making. If the CPA effectively makes or substantially influences decisions, the core justification for electing the President is weakened: the claimed electoral legitimacy becomes questionable when the actual decision-making power shifts to an appointed body. This raises both separation of powers concerns (an appointed body wielding significant influence over decisions supposedly made by an elected official) and political legitimacy concerns (on what basis does this appointed body claim authority to make choices affecting all Singaporeans?).

*   The government appoints CPA members, the CPA advises (or constrains) the President, and the President is supposed to check the government. If the government can shape the composition of the very body that controls the President's discretion, the custodial check is effectively neutralized — the separation of powers breaks down because the checked institution indirectly controls the checker. If SG’s brand of political constitutionalism is that SG has a stronger political check, with intra-executive checks and the legislature checking itself as a justification for weaker checks by the judiciary, the logic becomes inconsistent.

Most critically, where the CPA and the President disagree, Parliament can overrule the President’s decision by passing a resolution with a two-thirds majority (Art. 37IF). The President is expected to justify why he refused to follow the CPA’s recommendation, and the dominant political culture favours consensus. The irony is striking: Parliament – the very body that the President is supposed to check – can override the President if the President does not follow the CPA. The CPA therefore possesses a power akin to an almost-veto over the President’s independent discretion, chipping away at the President’s ability to function as an effective fiscal guardian.

Since the President has the electorate’s mandate, it is arguably the President who should be entrusted with the duty to check. Having these peripheral bodies interpose themselves between the President and the exercise of custodial powers dilutes the democratic mandate that was the entire rationale for making the Presidency an elected office. The rationale advanced for the CPA reflects a technocratic, elitist model of representative democracy: even though there is direct representation through the PM, MPs, and the President, independent bodies are considered necessary to supervise their actions. The question is whether this technocratic overlay is compatible with the democratic logic that justified making the Presidency elective in the first place.

### Reserved Elections, Qualification Criteria and Multiracialism

#### Reserved Elections Mechanism (Art. 19B)

Under Art. 19B(1), an election for the office of President is reserved for a community if no person belonging to that community had held office of President for any of the five most recent terms. The three recognise communities were Chinese, Malay, and Indian or other minorities. The mechanism operates on a self-identity and community acceptance basis, similar to the GRC framework.

Where two communities qualify, priority is given to the community with the greater gap; where three qualify, priority goes to the greatest gap, then the next greatest.

*   A natural sunset mechanism exists – reserved elections will not be triggered if the EP remains multiracial through open elections.

The introduction of reserved elections represented a fundamental expansion of the Elected Presidency’s purpose. Originally, the EP was focused exclusively on technocratic custodial functions – fiscal guardianship and civil service protection. Post-2016, the EP also serves an integrative, symbolic function, representing Singapore’s multiracial character and ensuring that all communities see themselves reflected in the highest office. This shifts the EP’s purpose partially from tackling pure separation of powers concerns towards also tackling representation concerns.

##### Multiracialism Can Muddy the Role of the President

The introduction of reserved elections raises several tensions. The President has traditionally served as a unifying figure, but reserved elections now require candidates in designated years to present themselves as race-based candidates. Unlike GRCs, where teams can represent multiple racial groups and there are many parliamentary seats, there is only one office for the Presidenyc. The 2017 reserved election for Malay candidates generated problematic discourse about who is “Malay” and who is a “real Malay”, and identity-based controversies including language fluency – dynamics that risk creating communalism rather than unity. Critics have argued that the reserved elections mechanism amounts to mere tokenism and perpetuates racial stereotypes, undermining the unifying and dignified aspects of the office.

*   George offers a sustained and penetrating critique of the reserved elections mechanism that reinforces and deepens these concerns. He frames the fundamental problem as a choice between legitimacy and certainty: “you can have either legitimacy or certainty; you can’t have both. Trying to guarantee an election outcome — as the government did when it restricted the 2017 election to Malay candidates (and then disqualified all but one) — diminished the perceived legitimacy of the process” (ACNR, Ch. 9). This framing has direct constitutional implications. The EP’s checking function derives its authority from the democratic mandate conferred by popular election. An election whose outcome is pre-determined cannot generate the same quality of democratic legitimacy — and thus cannot underwrite the same strength of checking authority — as a genuinely competitive one. If reserved elections produce Presidents whose mandate is perceived as manufactured rather than earned, their ability to stand up to a Cabinet backed by a genuine popular majority is correspondingly weakened.
*   George further argues that the government’s stated justification — that Chinese Singaporeans would not vote for a minority candidate — was both empirically dubious and self-defeating. He points to the popularity of Tharman Shanmugaratnam, an Indian-Ceylonese Tamil politician who attracted overwhelming cross-racial support, as evidence that Singaporeans are capable of looking past race when presented with a specific individual of known qualities (ACNR, Ch. 9). By restricting the 2017 race to Malay candidates, the government “underestimat\[ed\] both the national appeal of outstanding minority candidates, and the capacity of most Singaporeans to see past their racial prejudices” — reflecting, in George’s analysis, a broader pattern of the government’s “distrust of the people, its insistence on getting its way, and its lack of finesse in dealing with contentious issues” (ACNR, Ch. 9).

There is also a structural tension between the representational and technocratic functions. If reserved elections limit the candidate pool to specific ethnic communities, the available technocratic talent pool narrows. This might result in electing Presidents who fulfil representational goals but lack optimal custodial competence. Whether this trade-off is justified and whether prioritising ethnic representation over technocratic ability undermines the EP’s core custodial function is still a contested question. Furthermore, reserved elections constrain popular sovereignty and voter autonomy by limiting voter choice to candidates from a designated community, creating tension with democratic principles of open electoral competition.

In _Ravi_ \[2017\], M Ravi argued that the stringent qualification criteria for qualification deprived citizens of the equal right to stand for public office, and were contrary to Art. 12 on equal protection and violated the fundamental right to vote, which was said to be part of the basic structure of the Constitution. It was further argued that reserved elections were discriminatory on the grounds of race and contrary to Art. 12(2). The SGHC rejected both arguments. On discrimination, the PCMR had noted that the reserved elections framework applies equally to all three racial communities, with the same hiatus applying to all communities without exception, and no community advantaged or disadvantages vis-avis the others. There was “clearly and categorically no ‘discrimination’ to speak of”. The court held that there is no unqualified right for any citizen to stand for public office.

No definitive ruling was made on the BSD, but even if it applied, an unqualified right to stand for public office could not be said to be “fundamental and essential” to the political system. The eligibility criteria satisfied the equal protection clause: there was an intelligible differentia and a rational relation between the criteria and the purpose of ensuring qualified candidates. The court observed that the Constitution is not racially blind, pointing to Art. 152(2), which imposes on the Government a responsibility to care for the interests of racial and religious minorities. The PCMR confirmed that reserved elections apply equally to all three communities, are not discriminatory in purpose but aimed at ensuring multiracialism, and are minimally intrusive.

In _Tan Cheng Bock_ \[2017\] SGCA, Tan Cheng Bock challenged the counting of terms of office in declaring 2017 a reserved elections year. He argued that “President” defined under Art. 2 must refer to a President elected by its citizens, which would exclude Wee Kim Wee, appointed by Parliament, but instead should be the first President to wield the powers of the EP. The court held that the provision does not restrict Parliament to specifying the first term as a President elected by citizens. Art. 19B states only that an election is reserved if no person from a racial community had “held the office of President” – it does not specify that the person must have been elected. So long as the person exercised the functions of the office, the term counted, even if the person was not elected. This was held to be consistent with Parliament’s explicit intent to specify President Wee’s last term as the first term for the purposes of Art. 19B.

#### Qualification Criteria

Candidates must satisfy the basic requirements under Art. 19(2): age (45 years), citizenship, and residency. Beyond this, the Presidential Elections Committee (PEC) must be satisfied of the candidate’s integrity, good character, and reputation. The substantive criteria are divided into public sector and private sector requirements.

On the public sector side, a candidate must have held office for three or more years in designated positions (Minister, Chief Justice, Speaker, Attorney-General, Chairman of the PSC, Auditor-General, Accountant-General, or Permanent Secretary), served as chief executive of a Fifth Schedule entity for three or more years, or otherwise demonstrated comparable experience to the PEC’s satisfaction.

On the private sector side, a candidate must have served as CEO of a company with at least S$500 million in average shareholders’ equity during their most recent three-year period of service, with the company making profit after tax during the entirety of their tenure. The threshold was raised from S$100 million (pre-2016) to S$500 million – an intentional and deliberate tightening during the 2016 amendments. In addition, the relevant public or private sector experience must have been gained within the last 20 years – another intentional design choice that was made more stringent during the 2016 amendments, further narrowing the pool of eligible candidates

#### Criticism of Qualification Criteria

The stringency of these criteria has attracted sustained criticism.

1.  They severely limit the pool of candidates, particularly on the private sector side. There is a notable **non-equivalence** between public and private sector requirements: the Speaker of Parliament automatically qualifies, yet a CEO holding less than S$500 million in shareholders’ equity does not. It is difficult to argue persuasively that a Speaker necessarily has more relevant experience for the custodianship of reserves than a CEO who has managed significant business operations but falls below the equity threshold.
2.  The pre-screening of candidates by the PEC may discourage potentially qualified candidates who do not wish to face public rejection. Reserved elections further shrink the already limited pool. The combined effect contravenes the logic of democracy, which stipulates that the electorate should have a “broad selection” of candidates and that the expression of their preferences should be unmediated (Thio Li-ann, _Singapore: (S)electing the President_, 2007).

The consequences are stark: half of the last six presidential elections have been **walkovers**. Voters were practically denied the right to exercise their democratic choice, rendering the “democratic” nature of the office illusory (Thio, 2007). A President “elected” via an uncontested election lacks a positive popular mandate, raising questions as to whether he or she truly possesses the moral authority and political legitimacy to refuse the Cabinet – the very features considered fundamental to the original rationale for the Elected Presidency (Kevin Tan, _Looking Back at the Elected Presidency_). The stringent qualification criteria have therefore not only undermined the institution’s purportedly democratic nature but also subverted its original aims.

*   Cherian George’s account of the 2017 reserved election lends empirical weight to these democratic concerns. By restricting the field to Malay candidates and then disqualifying all but one, the government produced a walkover that George characterises as an “election stripped of free choice, to install a symbol of unity in a manner that showed division” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited, Ch. 9_). What emerges is a constitutional paradox: the very mechanisms intended to serve the EP’s expanded purpose (multiracial representation) actively undermined its original purpose (democratic legitimacy). A President who enters office through an uncontested, race-restricted election lacks the competitive mandate needed to credibly exercise custodial powers against the Cabinet. The government’s response – that the election was formally lawful and served a legitimate policy objective – does not address the underlying legitimacy deficit. As George argues, “the point of elections isn’t simply to give power to winners… elections are about conferring legitimacy. That legitimacy is a product of people’s democratic choice: their freedom to stand for election and their freedom to vote. But genuine choice makes elections unpredictable”. The qualification criteria, by producing walkovers and restricting the candidate pool, systematically eliminate that element of unpredictability – and, with it, the democratic legitimacy that was the entire rationale for making the office elective.

#### The PEC Filtering Process has Rule of Law Concerns

The PEC’s filtering process has been criticised for its lack of transparency and accountability. While the PEC has occasionally volunteered brief justifications, these have been described as not “entirely convincing” and “based on a very narrow interpretation of the eligibility requirements” (CNA, Grace Yeoh, 18 Aug 2023). For instance, the CFO of JTC was rejected for lacking financial credentials, although someone in that position might reasonably be expected to have more financial experience than some automatically qualifying public offices (Thio Li-ann, 2007). Since the PEC’s decisions have significant implications for democratic choice, they should arguably be subject to legal check or at least public scrutiny. The current lack of transparency is contrary to the rule of law, which demands that all discretionary powers have legal limits (_Chng Suan Tze_).

The PEC’s decisions on Certificates of Eligibility are “conclusive” and “not subject to appeal or review in any court” (section 8C, PEA). Similarly, the Community Committee’s certification of racial community membership is not subject to judicial review (section 8J, PEA). This insulation from judicial scrutiny is itself constitutionally questionable from a constitutional supremacy and rule of law perspective.

Furthermore, the PEC has no legal duty to give applicants a chance to respond to allegations. In the 2005 Presidential Elections, presidential hopeful Andrew Kuan was subject to a “trial by media,” and his former employer was requested to submit a report to the PEC (Jack Tsen-Ta Lee). The PEC did not interview Kuan or afford him an opportunity to respond to the allegations, effectively denying him the right to be heard. This contravenes the principles of natural justice – a fundamental facet of the rule of law (Thio, 2007). Denying applicants a right to respond also deprives citizens of the opportunity to scrutinise all sides of the story, limiting the role of the people in the democratic election process. The PEC’s filtering process is therefore arguably inconsistent with the principles underpinning Singapore’s constitutional democracy.

### Structural Contradictions in the EP

#### Custodial Role vs. Ceremonial Dignity

In the Westminster form of government, the President as head of state has always borne the role of being a dignified, unifying figure above politics. However, the EP’s custodial role is inherently incongruous with this historical function (2016 Constitutional Commission Report, para 7.43). The incongruity manifests in two ways.

First, the electoral process itself politicises the office by requiring candidates to compete for votes. Competitive campaigning may be divisive, contradicting the President’s unifying aspects (Neo, “Introduction”). In Singapore’s dominant party context, the EP’s role as a check on the government fuels public perception that the office is political, potentially encouraging candidates to campaign on an anti-government platform (2016 Constitutional Commission Report, para 7.44). Second, the EP’s custodial functions are themselves inherently political: the EP can publicly oppose and veto the Cabinet’s fiscal and appointment decisions. These are political acts that contradict the apolitical character traditionally associated with the Westminster head of state, who is expected to avoid public expressions of disagreement with the government.

*   Cherian George’s account of the Ong Teng Cheong presidency vividly illustrates this tension in practice. During his tenure, Ong publicly summoned the media and aired dissatisfaction with the Government’s treatment of his office — an unprecedented act that revealed how the EP’s democratic mandate generates expectations of public accountability that are irreconcilable with the Westminster convention of dignified reticence. When Ong’s wife passed away, the spontaneous public outpouring of mourning further demonstrated that the elected presidency carries a powerful ceremonial and emotional dimension that cannot simply be “managed” by the Government (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited, Ch. 8_). George observes that the Government was reluctant to elevate the event to a state occasion, reflecting its determination to prevent the presidency from becoming a rival centre of public affection and loyalty. The PAP’s approach reflected its “strict adherence to the principle of keeping government on an even keel and free from personality politics” — even the president’s ceremonial function was “seen as a force to be closely managed, lest it undermine the PAP’s stand against **cults of personality**” (ACNR, Ch. 8).
*   George also highlights NMP Simon Tay’s observation that the labels “executive,” “ceremonial,” and “custodial” — central to constitutional debate about the office — “are nowhere to be found in the Constitution. They are handles attached by political players in advocating their preferred ways to grasp a complex institution” (ACNR, Ch. 8). This engages the principle of constitutional supremacy directly: the Constitution’s meaning must be derived from its text, interpreted purposively (_Tan Cheng Bock_), not from the executive’s preferred political characterisation. The government’s forceful insistence that the Presidency is “merely custodial” serves the functional goal of limiting presidential independence but is not mandated by the Constitution’s architecture – the text confers powers without attaching a label that subordinates their exercise to Cabinet’s expectations. If the Constitution does not confine the Presidency to a “custodial box”, then arguments about whether the President has overstepped by acting publicly or independently must be assessed against the constitutional text, not against a political framing that has no textual basis. Different political conditions — or a different President — could support a very different reading of the same constitutional provisions, precisely because the text itself is broader than the label the Government has attached to it.

#### Democratic Mandate vs Institutional Constraints

The most fundamental structural tension is between the democratic mandate that justified making the office elective and the institutional constraints that have progressively limited the President’s ability to exercise independent judgment. The President was made elected so that he or she would possess the moral authority to check the Cabinet. But the creation and empowerment of the CPA, the 1999 White Paper’s expectation of concurrence, the insulation of PEC and Community Committee decisions from judicial review, the exclusion of defence and security spending, and the transfer clause amendments have collectively diminished the President’s independent checking power. If the President’s discretion is in practice constrained by appointed bodies, parliamentary overrides, and institutional expectations of consensus, the democratic mandate becomes largely symbolic – and the original rationale for the EP is subverted.

*   George’s account of the Ong Teng Cheong presidency engages the principle of SOP. The Constitution, by creating an elected presidency with independent custodial powers, establishes a dual-mandate structure: Parliament derives its mandate from general elections, the President from a separate popular vote. This is a deliberate constitutional choice to divide sovereign authority between two institutions. Lee Kuan Yew’s insistence that there could be “only one centre of government in the country” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited, Ch. 8_) is thus not merely a political preference but a direct repudiation of the SOP logic embedded in the Constitution’s own design. If the executive treats any independent exercise of presidential authority as a threat to be contained rather than as the constitutionally intended check operating as designed, the SOP is maintained in form but negated in substance. George’s observation that the EP was the sole institution at its creation outside the PAP’s chain of command that could “lay claim to that ultimate source of authority”, the people’s mandate underscores the significance of the office – the independence is not incidental but structural.
*   The progressive reduction of presidential powers through constitutional amendment raises a further separation of powers concern. The checking institution’s powers are being curtailed by the very branch of government it is designed to check. While Parliament’s constituent power to amend the Constitution is not in doubt, the _pattern_ of amendments — consistently favouring the reduction of presidential authority — creates a structural asymmetry: the executive-dominated legislature can reshape the checking institution at will, but the President has no reciprocal power to resist. Art. 5(2A), which was designed to entrench presidential powers against unilateral parliamentary amendment through a national referendum requirement, has never been brought into force — leaving the President’s powers perpetually vulnerable to amendment by the body the President is supposed to supervise. This is a separation of powers deficiency that is structural, not merely political.
*   The institutional constraints on the Presidency also engage the theory of political constitutionalism — the view that democratic accountability through Parliament provides a sufficient check on executive power, making robust judicial or presidential checks unnecessary. George’s broader account of Singapore’s political environment complicates this argument. He describes a system in which “Parliament is dominated by a ruling party with a leadership made up of cabinet ministers”, and where non-state institutions are “relegated to subordinate positions” (ACNR, Ch. 8). In a system where executive-legislative fusion is not merely a structural feature of Westminster government but is reinforced by single-party dominance, the assumption that Parliament can serve as an effective check on the Cabinet is weakened. Political constitutionalism presupposes that the electorate can replace a government through competitive elections — but if the costs of opposition politics are structurally elevated (through defamation suits, restrictive assembly laws, and media constraints), the electoral check, while formally preserved, may not operate with the robustness that the theory requires. This does not invalidate political constitutionalism as a framework, but it does strengthen the case that in Singapore’s dominant-party context, independent legal-constitutional checks — including a genuinely empowered Presidency — serve a particularly important function that cannot safely be assumed to be redundant.

#### Limited Practical Impact of the EP

A further question concerns the empirical significance of the President’s checking power: how often does the President actually block Cabinet decisions? If the presidential veto is rarely exercised, the checking power may be more theoretical than practical. Defenders of the EP might argue that its existence serves as a deterrent – the knowledge that the President can refuse assent discourages Cabinet from proposing improper uses of reserves or unmeritorious appointments in the first place, making actual vetoes unnecessary. On this view, the rarity of presidential vetoes is a sign that the mechanism is working as intended. However, sceptics might counter that if the power is rarely (or never) used, its practical significance is minimal, and the elaborate institutional architecture of the Elected Presidency yields little real constraint on executive behaviour. The truth likely lies in between, but the difficulty of measuring deterrent effects makes this debate resistant to definitive resolution. It is submitted the deterrence argument — that the mere existence of presidential powers discourages executive overreach — assumes that the institution retains credible independence. Where that independence is structurally compromised, the deterrent value is correspondingly diminished, and the constitutional safeguard becomes, in Thio Li-ann’s words, more theoretical than practical. The legality principle established in _Chng Suan Tze_ — that all power has legal limits and the rule of law demands that discretionary power be subject to examination — has implications beyond judicial review of individual decisions. It speaks to a broader constitutional expectation: that the mechanisms designed to constrain power must operate as genuine constraints, not merely formal ones. A checking institution whose occupant is effectively pre-selected for passivity does not satisfy this expectation. This is not to suggest that the selection of any particular President is unlawful, but rather that the cumulative effect of stringent qualification criteria, insulation of PEC decisions from review (s 8C PEA), and the pattern of walkovers produces a structural condition in which the constitutional check lacks the independence the rule of law presupposes.

*   Cherian George’s account of Ong Teng Cheong’s presidency also reveals a SOP problem in the government’s response to an active presidential check. Ong took his custodial role seriously, proactively seeking to test the “second key” mechanism and publicly challenging the Government’s handling of his office. Yet, the Government’s response was not to welcome this as the checking function operating as designed, but to contain and rebuff it (_ACNR_, Ch. 8). A separation of powers framework presupposes that each institution will respect the constitutional role of the other. Where the executive treats the checking institution’s exercise of its constitutional functions as an act of institutional disloyalty rather than a legitimate exercise of assigned authority, the separation of powers is undermined — not by a formal constitutional amendment, but by an institutional culture that refuses to accept the legitimacy of the check.

#### Transparency Asymmetry

The requirement for presidential assent does create a measure of transparency, since drawdowns receive publicity. If the President withholds assent to a Supply Bill, he must provide grounds and the CPA’s recommendations to the PM, and these must be published in the Gazette (Art. 37IE). However, this transparency is one-directional: it only operates when the President withholds assent. Where both President and CPA assent, drawdowns may proceed without meaningful public scrutiny. Moreover, a significant role is given to the CPA – an unelected body with equivalent powers. The mechanism is most visible when least likely to succeed (since parliamentary override exists), and least visible when it matters most.

#### A Possible Alternative: Reversion to Ceremonial Presidency with an Upper House

Given these structural contradictions, some commentators have proposed a reversion to a ceremonial presidency, accompanied by the transfer of custodial functions to another institution such as an Upper House (Wang Ting Koh, _“An Upper House to House Government Bodies”_). By vesting custodial functions in a group of competent individuals, the government’s persistent concerns about giving too much power to a single person would be addressed (2016 Constitutional Commission Report, para 4.12). There would theoretically be less incentive to curtail custodial functions or interpose other bodies. Democratic legitimacy could be ensured by making the Upper House fully or partially elected, and since the focus would be on collective ability, qualification requirements for individual members need not be as stringent, thereby better upholding democratic choice.

*   Similarly, Cherian George argues that it would have been “far more sensible to accept the suggestion of the 2016 Constitutional Commission, that the presidency revert to its historical role of a ‘symbolic unifying figure’ appointed by parliament,” with its veto functions transferred to a separate council (ACNR, Ch. 9). The constitutional case for this rests on the proposition that the current design fails to achieve genuine separation of powers. The single office of the Presidency is asked to perform two constitutionally distinct functions — the dignified, unifying role of the head of state, and the political, adversarial role of checking Cabinet decisions — that pull in opposite directions. As the 2016 Constitutional Commission itself acknowledged (para 7.43), these functions are inherently incongruous. Separating them into distinct institutions — a ceremonial president and an independent custodial council — would allow each institution to be designed for its specific constitutional purpose. The custodial body could be structured to ensure genuine independence from the executive (through appointment mechanisms, security of tenure, and transparent reporting obligations), without the distortions introduced by subjecting the checking function to popular election, qualification criteria, and reserved elections.

### Summary + Synthesis

The Elected Presidency occupies a unique and structurally fraught position in Singapore’s constitutional architecture. Created in 1991 to introduce an intra-executive check within a Westminster system characterised by executive-legislative fusion, weak parliamentary opposition, and single-party dominance, the EP was premised on two interlocking ideas: that a presidential checking power over fiscal decisions and civil service appointments was necessary, and that this power required the democratic legitimacy conferred by popular election.

In practice, however, the trajectory of constitutional amendments since 1991 has been one of progressive reduction of presidential powers. The exclusion of defence and security spending, the transfer clauses, the LTRRR framework, the empowerment of the CPA, and the 1999 White Paper’s expectation of harmonious concurrence have collectively narrowed the President’s effective custodial authority. The 2016 amendments introducing reserved elections added a new representational dimension that was entirely absent from the original conception, potentially muddying the office’s purpose and restricting the candidate pool even further. Stringent qualification criteria and the insulation of PEC decisions from judicial review have contributed to a pattern of uncontested elections, undermining the democratic mandate that was the office’s foundational justification.

The Elected Presidency thus embodies a set of unresolved tensions: between custodial checking and ceremonial dignity, between democratic mandate and institutional constraint, between technocratic competence and racial representation, and between the logic of distrust (which demands checks and balances) and the logic of trust (which curtails those same checks in the name of governmental efficiency). How these tensions are resolved—or whether they can be—will continue to shape constitutional debate in Singapore for years to come.

# Judiciary

## Judicial Power

Judicial power is the power possessed by an independent authority – the judiciary – to decide legal disputes. The exercise of judicial power involves making findings of fact, interpreting the law, and applying the law to ensure that all laws are respected and enforced across the board.

In summary, the judiciary checks on the legality of powers exercised by the government. It is premised on the existence of a genuine controversy, either between the State and one or more of its subjects, or between two or more subjects of the state (_Mohammad Faizal_ \[2012\] SGHC at \[27\]).

The constitutional basis for vesting judicial power in the courts is from the seminal judgement of Chief Justice John Mashall in _Marbury v Madison_ (USSC, 1803) from which Art. 4 and 93 of the Singapore Constitution are derived. Marshall CJ articulated two foundational principles:

1.  Upholding constitutional supremacy – all those who framed written constitutions contemplate them as forming the fundamental and paramount law of the nation, and consequently, an act of the legislature repugnant to the Constitution is void.
2.  What the law is – it is emphatically the province and duty of the judicial department to say what the law is, and those who apply the rule to particular cases must of necessity expound and interpret that rule.

These principles – that the Constitution is paramount and that courts must interpret it — form the theoretical foundation of Singapore’s judicial power under Art. 4 and 93.

Furthermore, judicial power coheres with a richer conceptualization of separation of powers, ensuring that the court acts as an effective check on the decisions of other branches, ensuring that the other branches exercise their powers within legal limits. This also preserves the rule of law, by guarding against arbitrary decision-making by the executive.

#### Courts have the power to declare laws invalid for inconsistency with Constitution

In a constitutional supremacy system, the court possess the power to declare laws promulgated by Parliament or executive actions to be invalid for inconsistency with the Constitution. This judicial review power is inherently tied to constitutional supremacy – if the Constitution is supreme, then some institution must have the authority to enforce that supremacy by striking down laws or acts that violate constitutional provisions – which is the judiciary.

*   Judicial power thus provides a legal mechanism of accountability, complementing political mechanisms such as elections. The judiciary holds the government legally accountable, ensuring actions conform with both ordinary law and constitutional law. This accountability function is particularly critical in Westminster system like Singapore’s, where the fusion of executive and legislative power creates risks of excessive concentrations of power.
*   The judiciary, as a co-equal branch, provides the primary institutional check on this fused executive-legislative power.

### The judicial power of the court is part of the basic structure

The judicial power of the court is arguably part of the “basic structure” of the Constitution. Its exercise through judicial review has been described as the “cornerstone” of the rule of law (Chan Sek Keong, _The Rule of Law in Singapore_). This argument has been accepted in Malaysia in _Seminyih Jaya_.

In a Westminster model of government where powers are functionally divided between co-equal branches, the judicial power of the courts may be characterised as “fundamental and essential” to the political system, serving as the principal means by which legislative and executive excesses are kept in check (_Liyanage v The Queen_, _Mohammad Faizal_).

*   Even where judicial power is not explicitly vested in the Constitution, there exists a separate, inherent power in the judicature which cannot be usurped or infringed by the executive or legislature (_Liyanage v The Queen_).

### Singapore Respects the Exclusivity of Judicial Power

Art. 93 of the Constitution provides that the judicial power of Singapore shall be vested un the Supreme Court and its subordinate law as may be provided by any written law. For the Supreme Court, judicial power is clearly established by the Constitution itself – it is a constitutional institution. For subordinate courts, judicial power is established by legislation, as indicated by the phrase “as may be provided by any written law”.

*   So a critical question is whether judicial power is exclusive to the judiciary or whether Parliament can determine its scope.

Former CJ Chan Sek Keong’s writing establish an important principle – judicial power cannot vest in any entity other than a court. This protects the exclusivity of judicial power and prevents Parliament form transferring judicial functions to non-judicial bodies.

*   The basic structure doctrine strengthens this exclusivity. A line of cases beginning with _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\] and continuing through _Ramalingam_ established that separation of powers is fundamental and essential to Singapore’s political system. In _Hinds_, a UKPC case cited in Singapore, the court held that under separation of powers, judicial power is exclusive and cannot be encroached upon by other government branches because SOP demands the preservation of each branch’s autonomy, including the judiciary. In _Mohammad Faizal_ at \[17\], the exclusivity of judicial power was held to be safeguarded by the provisions in Part VIII of the Singapore Constitution, which are designed to secure the independence of the judiciary.

Thus, it is evident that in Singapore, judicial power is _exclusive_ to the judiciary. If not, it would encroach upon separation of powers.

*   That being said, caution must be taken in differentiating between things that are exclusively within the domain of the courts in exercising their judicial power, and the domain of parliament in enacting laws, such as the severity of punishment to impose on offenders (_Sabtu_), which merely prescribes factors for the courts to take into account in exercising its judicial power.

#### Unlike Malaysia, Singapore’s judicial power is constitutionally vested

Contrasting Singapore’s Art. 93 with Art. 121(1) of the Malaysian Constitution illuminates the significance of how judicial power is constitutionally vested. The Malaysian provision states that the jurisdiction and powers of courts are conferred “by or under federal law”, meaning Parliament makes federal law and thereby determines judicial powers.

*   This arrangement is problematic from a SOP perspective as it means the judiciary’s powers are subject to whatever Parliament decides, undermining the co-equality that is core to the SOP. In Singapore, by contrast, Art. 93 vests judicial power in the courts themselves, providing stronger protection for judicial independence and co-equality. The comparison shows what a more problematic constitutional arrangement looks like versus SG’s relatively stronger protection.

#### Key Cases

| Liyanage v The Queen [1967] UKPC |
| --- |
| Facts: The appellants were convicted of offences in respect of an abortive coup on 27 January 1962 in Ceylon (now Sri Lanka). They were not tried in accordance with normal criminal procedure but by three judges of the Supreme Court sitting without a jury. The central submission across all appeals was that the convictions should be quashed due to the invalidity of certain legislation passed in 1962 specifically to deal with the trial of those involved in the coup. The legislation allowed hearsay evidence to be admitted, was made retrospective to the time of the coup, and permitted the accused to be tried by a Special Tribunal of three Supreme Court judges sitting without a jury, appointed by the Law Minister.Held: The Privy Council held that the Ceylonese Parliament could not validly pass such legislation as it effectively created crimes ex post facto. The 1962 Acts amounted to a legislative direction to convict the appellants or to secure convictions with severe penalties. Even though the Constitution did not explicitly grant the courts judicial power, the courts were necessarily vested with such power by virtue of the constitutional structure. The provisions of the Constitution manifested an intention to secure in the judiciary a freedom from political, legislative, and executive control. If such Acts were valid, the judicial power could be wholly absorbed by the legislature and taken out of the hands of the judges.Significance: This was a targeted attempt to secure a conviction, as evidenced by the retroactivity of the Act. The legislation functioned as an instruction by the legislature to the judiciary, directing it on what outcome to reach — a clear and extreme usurpation of judicial power and curtailment of the separation of powers. |
| Moses Hinds & Ors v The Queen [1977] UKPC |
| --- |
| Facts: The Jamaican Gun Court Act 1975 established a special Gun Court to deal with firearm offences. This Court had three tiers: the Full Court Division (apex), the Circuit Court Division, and the Resident Magistrate’s Division. The Full Court Division was presided over by persons qualified only as Resident Magistrates, yet it exercised powers that were ordinarily exercisable only by Supreme Court judges. Furthermore, crimes previously triable only in the High Court were now also triable by the Gun Court. Critically, judges of the Full Court Division had no security of tenure or remuneration protections to safeguard their independence. Hinds argued that the provisions relating to the Full Court Division were an unconstitutional infringement of the judicial power of the Supreme Court.Held: The Privy Council held that Parliament cannot, by ordinary law, vest in a new court composed of members of the lower judiciary a jurisdiction that is characteristic of the Supreme Court. The distinction between the higher and lower judiciary is that the former are given a greater degree of security of tenure. The Constitution’s manifest intention is that any person appointed to such a court should be appointed in the same manner, and entitled to the same security of tenure, as the holder of the judicial office which entitled them to exercise the corresponding jurisdiction when the Constitution came into force.Dissenting Judgment: The dissent argued that the provisions relating to the Full Court Division did not conflict with the Constitution of Jamaica. The creation of the Full Court Division with its jurisdiction and powers did not involve any transfer of judicial power to the executive, and there was nothing in the Gun Court Act which purported to alter the Supreme Court, its jurisdiction, or its powers. |

### SG Courts articulate strong principles but apply them narrowly

A recurring pattern in Singapore constitutional jurisprudence is that courts articulate strong protective principles regarding judicial power, but apply them narrowly in practice.

In _Mohammad Faisal_ \[2012\] SGHC, a case relating to MDA provisions that appeared to interfere with judicial power by dictating to courts how they should approach sentencing, the SGHC reiterated the general principle that separation of powers is part of the basic structure and that Parliament may not enact laws contrary to it, with courts being co-equal and final arbiters of law. However, the court held that in this case, the MDA did not intrude on judicial power because sentencing is not an exclusive judicial power, since Parliament has the power to set out a range of punishment for particular offences, which does not take away the court’s power to exercise discretion in sentencing or to adjudicate the controversy. _Mohammad Faisal_ thus demonstrates that while general principles _sound_ protective, their application can be quite narrow – the court focused on how not all aspects of the judicial function are “exclusive” in the sense of being immune from legislative regulation.

*   This raises the question of what aspects of judicial power are truly exclusive and protected from parliamentary encroachment. The rule of law requires that exercises of state power, particularly those resulting in criminal penalties or significant deprivations of liberty, are not arbitrary. Ordinarily, criminal liability is imposed only after adjudication by a court following a fair trial, where reasons must be given and procedural safeguards are observed. If an executive determination – such as an order requiring attending rehabilitation – is treated by statute as equivalent to a criminal conviction, this risks bypassing those judicial safeguards. The difficulty, therefore, is that equating an executive order with a judicial finding of guilt may undermine the transparency and procedural protections that normally accompany the exercise of judicial power. This raises a normative rule of law concern about the potential expansion of executive power at the expense of judicial oversight.
*   Shah: The critical analytical skill is distinguishing between what the court says about SOP and judicial power in principle, and what the court actually does when deciding whether a specific law violates those principles.

The SGCA in _Prabargaran_ further clarified the relationship between branches in the sentencing context, holding that the sentencing function does not reside exclusively in the domain of any one institution of government. This reinforces the _Mohammad Faisal_ position that Parliament legitimately sets sentencing frameworks while courts exercise discretion within those frameworks, and neither branch may claim exclusive ownership of the sentencing power.

| Mohammad Faizal bin Sabtu v PP (“Faisal”) [2012] SGHC |
| --- |
| Facts: The Misuse of Drugs Act (MDA) contained provisions that appeared to interfere with judicial power by dictating to courts how they should approach sentencing — specifically, treating executive orders as convictions in order to trigger and enhance minimum punishments.Held: The court reiterated the general principles: separation of powers is part of the basic structure, Parliament may not enact laws contrary to it, and courts are co-equal and final arbiters of law. However, when applying these principles, the court held that the MDA did not intrude on judicial power because sentencing is not an exclusive judicial power. Parliament, under its authority to make laws, has the power to set out a range of punishment for particular offences. This legislative function does not take away the court’s power to exercise discretion in sentencing or to adjudicate the controversy. The legislature sets the sentencing framework; the courts retain the power to exercise discretion within that framework and to adjudicate cases.Significance: Faisal demonstrates that while the general principles sound protective, their application can be quite narrow. Not all aspects of the judicial function are “exclusive” in the sense of being immune from legislative regulation. Sentencing, at least, is an area where legislative specification is permissible. This raises the question of what aspects of judicial power are truly exclusive and protected from parliamentary encroachment. The critical analytical skill is distinguishing between what the court says about separation of powers and judicial power in principle, and what the court actually does when deciding whether a specific law violates those principles. |
| Semenyih Jaya Sdn Bhd v Pentadbir Tanah Daerah Hulu Langat [Malaysian Federal Court] |
| --- |
| Facts: A Land Acquisition Act provision compelled judges to adopt the opinion of lay assessors in determining compensation for land acquisitions.Held: The Malaysian Federal Court held this provision unconstitutional because it usurped the court’s judicial powers by allowing lay assessors to effectively take over judicial power in deciding compensation amounts. Determining compensation is part of judicial power in legal processes — it is a power judges must exercise. Parliament cannot transfer this power to non-judges. The rationale was explicitly grounded in separation of powers: judicial power resides exclusively in the judiciary, and the judiciary is entrusted with keeping every state organ within its legal limits.Contrast with Faisal: Both cases involve alleged legislative encroachment on judicial power and both invoke separation of powers, but the outcomes differ. In Semenyih Jaya, the court found a genuine usurpation (transferring decision-making to lay assessors), whereas in Faisal, the court found that the MDA merely set a legislative framework within which courts retained their essential adjudicative and discretionary functions. The comparison shows what successful and unsuccessful challenges to judicial power look like, and that the boundary between permissible legislative specification and impermissible usurpation is context-dependent. |

## Complexities in Determining Jurisdiction

There is an important distinction to be drawn between jurisdiction (whether the court as the power to do something – do they have the authority to hear the case at all) and admissibility (whether a court can choose to exercise that power in a particular way – is this claim defective or premature?).

The courts in Singapore exercise several distinct forms of jurisdiction, each with its own scope, rationale and limitations.

#### Development of SG’s Judicial System

In 1826, the formation of the Straits Settlements and the Second Charter of Justice established the Court of Judicature, with appeals lying to the Judicial Committee of the Privy Council. In 1868, the Straits Settlements Supreme Court and Court of Appeal were created. In 1946, the Supreme Court of the Colony of Singapore was established. Upon the formation of Malaysia in 1963, the High Court and Federal Court of Malaysia sat in Singapore. After independence in 1965, judicial power continued to be exercised by Malaysian courts in Singapore until 1969, when the Supreme Court of Singapore was established. In 1994, appeals to the United Kingdom Privy Council were abolished, and the Singapore Court of Appeal became the apex court. Most recently, in 2021, the High Court (Appellate Division) was created to handle certain categories of appeals.

This trajectory reflects Singapore’s gradual assumption of full judicial sovereignty, moving from a colonial judicial system with external appellate oversight to a fully autonomous court hierarchy. The abolition of Privy Council appeals in 1994 was a particularly significant step, as it made the Court of Appeal the final arbiter on all questions of law — reinforcing the judiciary’s position as a co-equal branch under Art. 93.

### Structure of Singapore’s Superior Courts

The Appellate hierarchy in SG runs as follows:

Magistrat’s Appeals 🡪 SGHC 🡪> SGHC (Appellate Division) or in certain case, the SGCA. The SGCA sits at the apex of the judicial hierarchy.

#### Direct Appeals to the SGCA

Where a case is decided in the SGHC is granted a direct appeal, it may bypass the SGHC (Appellate Division) and be heard directly by the SGCA. Only Schedule 6 cases qualify for direct appeal, including cases concerning constitutional or administrative law, criminal law, contempt of court, the Parliamentary Elections Act and Presidential Elections Act, patents, arbitration and certain other specified matters.

#### No Direct Appeal – Heard by SGHC Appellate Division

In cases where the Court of Appeal does not grant leave, or where the law provides that the High Court (Appellate Division) is the final court, the matter will be heard by the Appellate Division instead of the Court of Appeal. Examples include incidental directions, interim orders, and cases where the law specifically designates the Appellate Division as the final court of determination. The creation of this intermediate appellate tier in 2021 raises issues of case precedent and the relationship between the two appellate courts.

#### SICC

The Singapore International Commercial Court (SICC) is not an international court per se, but rather a division of the High Court that hears cases of an international or commercial nature. It forms part of the government’s broader effort to position Singapore as a premier arbitration and dispute resolution hub. Notably, parties appearing before the SICC need not apply the local rules of evidence, and foreign counsel may appear before the SICC provided they are registered under the Legal Profession Act. The SICC thus represents an innovative adaptation of the court structure to serve Singapore’s economic and legal policy objectives.

### Additional Functions of the Courts

#### The Court can Provide Advisory Opinions on the Constitution

Under Art. 100 of the Constitution, the President may refer any question affecting the Constitution to a special tribunal comprising not fewer than three judges of the Supreme Court. The purpose of this provision is to enable the government to obtain an authoritative opinion on constitutional questions so that it does not unintentionally violate the Constitution. This power has been exercised only once, in Constitutional Reference No. 1 of 1995. Art. 100 is an unusual provision for a common law jurisdiction such as Singapore, given that common law courts generally only adjudicate live matters and not hypothetical questions. In civil law jurisdictions, by contrast, constitutional courts routinely interpret the constitution, and their decisions are binding on all other courts. Singapore does not have a dedicated constitutional court.

#### The Court can Adjudicate on the Removal of the EP

Under Art. 22L of the Constitution, the courts may be called upon to adjudicate the removal of the Elected President. A bench of at least five judges of the Supreme Court, including the JC, may be convened for this purpose.

The grounds for removal include:

1.  Intentional violation of the Constitution;
2.  Treason;
3.  Misconduct or corruption involving abuse of powers of office;
4.  Any offence involving fraud, dishonest or moral turpitude; and
5.  Where a motion for removal is adopted by not less than half of the total number of Members of Parliament.

This judicial role in presidential removal reflects the system of checks and balances inherent in Singapore’s constitutional design.

### Composition of the Courts

The Court of Appeal comprises the Chief Justice (who serves as its President), the Vice President, Judges of Appeal, Senior Judges, and, where necessary, puisne judges of the High Court who may be drawn up to sit on the Court of Appeal, particularly where a puisne judge possesses relevant subject-matter expertise. The High Court (both General and Appellate Divisions) comprises the Chief Justice, Judges of the High Court, puisne judges, Senior Judges, Judicial Commissioners, and judges of the Singapore International Commercial Court. Notably, a drawing-down provision allows Court of Appeal judges to sit on High Court matters, providing flexibility in judicial resourcing.

### Inherent Jurisdiction

Inherent jurisdiction refers to the power of a court to hear and determine cases, derived not from any statute or rule of law but from the very nature of the court as a superior court of law. The juridical basis of this jurisdiction lies in the court’s authority to uphold, protect and fulfil the judicial function of administering justice according to law in a regular, orderly and effective manner. In _Chee Siok Chin_ \[2006\] SGHC, it was described as a “residual source of powers, which the court may draw upon as necessary whenever it is just or equitable to do so, and in particular to ensure the observance of the due process of law, to prevent improper vexation or oppression, to do justice between the parties and to secure a fair trial between them”.

*   It is a broad, virile, and necessary jurisdiction that performs the quintessential function of justice, allowing the court to dispense procedural justice as a means of achieving substantive justice between the parties.

### Review Jurisdiction

Review jurisdiction refers to the power of a court to reconsider its own previous decisions. Only the SGCA has asserted its inherent right to review its previous cases and even then, only in exceptional circumstances, as the principle of _res judicata_ requires finality in cases litigated before the courts.

*   This of course aids the rule of law, as it ensures predictability in the law which citizens and government officials can base their conduct off.

In _MCST Plan v Tat Lee_ \[2011\], Chan Sek Keong CJ held that the SGCA has inherent jurisdiction to reopen and set aside its own decisions made in breach of natural justice and to rehear the matters dealt with in such decisions. How this jurisdiction is exercised depends on the nature of the breach – a breach of the bias rule entitles the aggrieved party to have the judgement set aside and the matter reheard, while a breach of the hearing rule entitles the aggrieved party to a hearing on the matters on which they were not heard, with the court deciding only after such a hearing whether the impugned judgement should be set aside.

*   Examples of bias include a financial interest in the outcome, prejudgement of the case, or personal/professional connections with one of the parties
*   A breach of the hearing rule occurs when a party is not given notice of the proceedings or is denied an opportunity to present their case or respond.

| Kho Jabing v PP [2015] SGCA |
| --- |
| Facts: The Court of Appeal re-sentenced the applicant to death, but the applicant filed the present application to set aside the death sentence. The SGCA considered its power to review its own previous decisions.Held: The Court of Appeal, as the final appellate court in Singapore, has the inherent power to reopen concluded criminal appeals in order to prevent a miscarriage of justice. This power is a facet of the judicial power vested in the Court of Appeal by virtue of Art. 93 of the Constitution. When the court exercises this power of review, it is acting within the scope of its statutorily-conferred appellate jurisdiction, which is not completely exhausted merely by the rendering of a decision on the merits of the appeal.The current position on review jurisdiction comprises four principles: First, the final appellate court has the inherent power to correct its own mistakes to prevent miscarriages of justice. Second, this power of review is to be exercised sparingly. Third, a review by a final appellate court is distinct from an appeal. Fourth, the substratum of an application for review should be new material that was not previously canvassed in the proceedings leading to the decision being challenged.Two-stage test (at [77]): (1) Whether the applicant has discharged the burden of producing “sufficient material” to warrant the court exercising its inherent power of review. The material must be (i) “new” — not previously canvassed at any stage and not something which could, even with reasonable diligence, have been adduced earlier; and (ii) “compelling” — reliable, substantial, powerfully probative, and capable of showing almost conclusively that there has been a miscarriage of justice. (2) Whether the applicant has discharged the burden of proving a “miscarriage of justice.” |

### Judicial Review Jurisdiction comes from Art. 4 read with Art. 93

The Constitution contains no explicit provision stating that courts in Singapore have the power to conduct judicial review. Nevertheless, the power derives from the separation of powers concept read together with Art. 4, which establishes constitutional supremacy. Art. 4 provides that the Constitution is supreme law and any law inconsistent with it is void. Given this supremacy, it follows that some institution must have the authority to declare laws void for constitutional inconsistency. Under SOP principles, that institution is the judiciary. This is also confirmed by Art. 93, which provides that judicial power is vested in the Supreme Court. It is the judiciary’s province to adjudicate such questions and declare acts or laws null and void.

In other words, judicial review jurisdiction derives from Article 4 and Article 93 of the Constitution, where the court determines whether governmental actions or laws are consistent with the constitution. 

*   Constitutional review entails two possible exercises — (1) courts reviewing the constitutionality of laws passed by parliament, and (2) courts reviewing the constitutionality of government action.
*   An example would be decision to detain someone for violating (the now repealed) s377A. The detained person could: (1) Claim that s377A is unconstitutional for violating equality guarantees; and (2) Claim that the the arrest was unconstitutional for violating some fundamental rights provision in the Constitution (including equality).

Judicial review examines the legality of government action, not the merits. This extends to the decisions of all administrative bodies, because all exercises of public power have legal limits.

*   Legality is asking whether the government had the power to do this, and whether they followed the proper rules in doing it; while merits is asking was it the right decision or a good/wise decision.

Constitutional judicial review → carried out through the original jurisdiction of the High Court under Article 93 read with the relevant provisions of the Supreme Court of Judicature Act and Order 24 of the Rules of Court. A party brings an ordinary action or application arguing that a law is inconsistent with the Constitution and therefore void under Article 4

### Supervisory Jurisdiction

Supervisory Jurisdiction is the power of the High Court to determine the legality of any administrative rule, action, or decision. It encompasses the scrutiny and control exercised by the High Court over decisions of inferior courts, tribunals and other public bodies discharging public functions (_Ng Chye Huey_ \[2007\] 2 SLR 106). This jurisdiction deals with all administrative bodies and actors, including disciplinary tribunals where laypersons may sit, and ensures they act within the powers given to them. It is not self-initiated, it must be initiated by a party seeking relief by application to the court on grounds of want of jurisdiction, error of law, breach of natural justice, or _ultra vires_. It is also generally confined to questions not touching on the merits of the case.

*   Remedies available include prerogative writs: quashing orders, prohibiting orders, mandatory orders, orders for review of detention, and quo warranto.

### Revisionary Jurisdiction

Revisionary jurisdiction is the power of the High Court to review decisions of subordinate courts, confined to such courts. It deals with errors of law and/or fact and offers a wide array of remedies including retrial, overturning of decisions, and substitution of punishment. It is provided for in section 27(1) of the Supreme Court of Judicature Act (SCJA), which states that the High Court shall have general supervisory and revisionary jurisdiction over all subordinate courts, and is further defined in sections 23–26 of the SCJA.

### Distinguishing Supervisory and Revisionary Jurisdiction

Following _Ng Chye Huey_, the key differences are as follows. First, supervisory jurisdiction extends to all administrative courts and bodies, whereas revisionary jurisdiction is confined to subordinate courts. Second, supervision generally is confined to questions not touching on the merits of the case, whereas revision will lie on errors of both law and fact. Third, supervision is effected by way of prerogative writs, whereas revision is marked by complete flexibility of remedies.

### Problem of the Military Court of Appeal – Jurisdiction?

Where courts are expressly declared by statute to be superior courts, it is “beyond doubt” that the High Court has no supervisory jurisdiction over them; their decisions cannot be subject to review by the High Court (_Abdul Wahab bin Sulaiman v Commandant, Tanglin Detention Barracks_ \[1985–6\] SGHC). Where a court is not expressly declared to be a superior court, it depends on the nature, jurisdiction, and powers of the particular court.
