In _Abdul Wahab_, since the MCA is described as a superior court, it was held its jurisdiction and exercise of its full power to hear and determine appeals from a subordinate military court are matters that cannot be reviewed by the SGHC.

| Abdul Wahab bin Sulaiman v Commandant, Tanglin Detention Barracks [1985–6] SGHC |
| --- |
| Facts: A was a National Serviceman convicted by General Court Martial for unlawful possession of a controlled drug. Before the appeal against the sentence was heard by the Military Court of Appeal (MCA), A completed his sentence and was released from the army. The MCA allowed the appeal and enhanced A’s sentence to 18 months’ detention. A applied for a writ of habeas corpus, arguing that the MCA had no jurisdiction to hear the appeal or enhance the sentence once A was released from National Service.Held: No order for writ of habeas corpus was granted. As the MCA is described as a superior court, its jurisdiction and exercise of its full power to hear and determine appeals from a subordinate military court are matters that cannot be reviewed by any prerogative writs or orders of the High Court. The court relied on the fact that the MCA is presided over at least by High Court judges, the SAF Act described the MCA as a “Superior” court, and section 154 of the SAF Act states that no appeal shall lie from any decision of the MCA.Critical Evaluation: This decision is arguably problematic. The judge adopted an overly strict and narrow approach towards the separation of powers, simply deferring to the legislature’s statutory description without considering whether the arrangement made sense within the whole scheme of judicial powers in Singapore. Several factors suggest the MCA and the Supreme Court are not at parity. First, Art. 93 states that judicial power is vested in the Supreme Court and subordinate courts “as may be provided by any written law” — the MCA, created by the Military Act, appears on this basis to be a subordinate court. Second, modes of appointment for SC judges are provided for in the Constitution, while MCA judges are appointed under ordinary legislation. Third, the MCA hears only appeals from subordinate military courts, giving it limited jurisdiction compared to the High Court’s general jurisdiction. Fourth, the High Court exercises broad judicial review powers which the MCA lacks. In constitutional terms, since the MCA’s powers are granted by ordinary legislation, it is and should be subordinate to the Supreme Court, and its decisions should be subject to review by the High Court. The case may be reconciled on the basis that the MCA does not sit comfortably within the civilian framework since it governs a separate domain, but to give proper effect to this, amendments should be made to the SAF Act to provide the MCA with proper protections, independence, and features equivalent to the High Court. |

## Limitations on Judicial Review

Judicial power, through broad, is not unlimited. Singapore law recognises a number of doctrinal and structural limitations on the exercise of judicial review. These limitations arise from a combination of constitutional design, judicial self-restraint and legislative action, and they collectively shape the boundaries within which the courts operate.

*   They must be understood alongside institutional safeguards to assess whether the current regime adequately protects judicial independence while enabling effective **constitutional review**. The critical questions are: what is the scope of judicial review, what limits exist, and are those limits justified or problematic from the perspective of constitutional principles?

### Decisions of Superior Courts

Courts do not conduct judicial review on their own decisions. Judicial decisions can only be appealed against or reviewed by the court itself (_Marplan v AG_ (2013)). This limitation preserves the hierarchy of the court system and ensures that judicial review is directed outward at legislative and executive action, not inward at the judiciary’s own rulings.

### _Res Judicata, Functus Officio_, and the Principle of Finality

_Res judicata_ prevents the endless relitigation of issues by barring the resuscitation of a case that has already received a final decision.

The related doctrine of _functus officio_ provides that once the SGCA has rendered judgement in an appeal before it, it has exhausted its jurisdiction insofar as that appeal is concerned (_Ramalingam_). However, this principle does not apply where the question sought to be raised concerns not the substantive merits of the case but rather the court’s jurisdiction to hear the case in the first place. If, at the first hearing, the SGCA decided a matter which was not within its jurisdiction, there would be no decision in law on that matter, and the court would not be _functus officio_ with respect to it.

The principle of finality holds that it is generally in the public interest that court judgements should be final and that parties should not be allowed to relitigate issues already decided by the courts (_Ramalingam_ at \[11\]). However, broad exceptions do apply in criminal proceedings. In _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2009\] at \[15\], the SGCA observed that “the finality principle should not be applied strictly in criminal cases where the life or liberty of the accused is at stake, as it would subvert the true value of the judicial process, which is to ensure, as far as possible, that the guilty are convicted and the innocent are acquitted”.

### There must be a real case for the courts to exercise their jurisdiction

There must be a real lis or real case before the courts will exercise their jurisdiction. In _Tan Eng Hong_ at \[132\], the SGCA held that the need for the existence of a real controversy between the parties stems from the function of the courts to adjudicate on and determine disputes between parties. Without a _lis_, the courts may find themselves being called upon to give advisory opinions on abstract, hypothetical or academic questions instead of deciding on real disputes.

*   However, the court recognised at \[138\] that where the circumstances of a case are such that a declaration will be of value to the parties or to the public, the court may proceed to hear the case and grant declaratory relief even though the facts on which the action is based on are theoretical. Where there is a real legal interest in a case being heard, there is a real controversy to be determined.

### There must be _Locus Standi_

Locus Standi refers to the threshold requirements that determine who can bring a case for judicial review. The Constitution itself imposes no bar on _locus standi_, but principles have emerged through case law. **Singapore has three main types of standing for constitutional cases**, each progressively more difficult to establish:

The court recognizes that for constitutional guarantees to have meaning, citizens must be able to file a suit in court should there be any constitutional violation (**_Colin Chan_**).

#### Personal Constitutional Rights – Sufficiency of Interest

The applicable test for personal constitutional rights is the **_Karaha Bodas_** test, which requires (1) a real interest in bringing the action, (2) a real controversy between the parties, and (3) the right must be personal to the applicant and enforceable against the adverse party. This test will _prima facie_ be fulfilled if the plaintiff demonstrates that a constitutional right has been violated (_Tan Eng Hong_ \[2012\] SGCA, affirmed in _Lim Meng Suang_ \[2019\] SGCA).

*   The existence of an allegedly unconstitutional law on the statute books and/or a real and credible (not merely fanciful) threat of prosecution under an allegedly unconstitutional law may constitute a violation of a constitutional right (_Tan Eng Hong_).
*   However, each case turns on its own facts and this is not a general rule. Constitutional rights are personal to every Singapore citizen by virtue of citizenship, but the mere fact of citizenship in itself does not satisfy standing, there must be violation of a personal right.

| Tan Eng Hong v AG [2012] SGCA |
| --- |
| Facts and Held: The case concerned the constitutionality of section 377A of the Penal Code. The Court of Appeal found that locus standi was established as there was a real interest, a real controversy, and the matter related to a personal constitutional right, since the threat of prosecution under section 377A remained on the statute books and was not merely fanciful. |

#### Public Rights – Special Damage

Where a plaintiff seeks to vindicate a public right rather than a private right, they must demonstrate “special damage” in respect of that public right (_Vellama_ \[2013\] SGCA). In _Vellama_, a constituency voter without an MP challenged the Prime Minister’s delay in calling a by-election for parliamentary vacancy. She was not asserting a personal right to vote (as there was no election yet to vote in), but rather a public right regarding proper electoral procedure. The Court found that she had standing because she could demonstrate special damages – as a resident of the constituency, the delay meant she lacked parliamentary representation, which was a particularised harm beyond what the general public suffered.

_NB: This is a SMC case. Unlikely we have this apply for a GRC_.

#### Breach of Public Duty with No Correlative Rights

Where no correlative rights are involved, the plaintiff must show that the public duty is breached, and “special damage” in the sense that the breach must be of such an egregious manner that it is in the public interest to hear the case (_Jeyaratnam_ \[2014\] SGCA). The threshold is deliberately high – the government’s action must constitute an egregious – not merely technical or minor – breach of law, and the matter must implicate significant public interest.

| Jeyaretnam Kenneth Andrew v AG [2014] SGCA |
| --- |
| Facts: MAS announced that Singapore was extending a loan to the IMF as part of a broader effort to provide the IMF with enough resources to deal with the Eurozone financial crisis. The applicant claimed the loan contravened Art. 144 of the Constitution, which required both Parliamentary and Presidential assent, neither of which MAS had obtained.Held: The case failed at the first stage, and in obiter, the Court held that the applicant had no standing. He was unable to assert any rights, private or public, relating to the alleged breach of duty. His claim was brought purely in the public interest. The applicant had argued that the act was of “dubious utility,” but this amounted to asking the court to rule on the merits of public bodies’ actions, which is not a role the court should undertake. In the context of the statutory scheme, Parliament did not intend that persons such as the applicant would have standing to seek judicial review. The nature of the issue was entirely political and should be resolved as such. Notably, neither Parliament nor the President had thought fit to question the propriety of the promised loan. |

#### Comparison with Liberal Standing Regimes

Singapore’s locus standi standards are significantly less liberal compared to some other jurisdictions. In more liberal regimes, anyone in the public, including interest groups and civil society organisations, can bring matters for constitutional review regardless of whether they have a personal interest, whether they have suffered special damage, or whether the government committed an egregious breach. Some jurisdictions, such as India and Pakistan, go even further by permitting _suo motu_ jurisdiction, where courts on their own volition can take up a matter for constitutional review without any party bringing a case. This is extremely liberal and quite controversial, as it allows courts to set their own agenda for constitutional adjudication. Singapore lacks _suo motu_ power, meaning the judiciary cannot review what is not brought before it.

#### Evaluation of Locus Standi as Limitation

Locus standi rules directly affect the scope of judicial review because they control access to courts. Access to courts means access to justice — the capacity to bring legal proceedings and have claims heard. If requirements are very liberal, it is easy for individuals to trigger judicial review, which expands the court’s opportunity to exercise review powers. Conversely, if requirements are restrictive, the judiciary’s capacity to check executive and legislative power is diluted. The checking function that separation of powers assigns to courts is undermined when procedural barriers prevent cases from reaching courts in the first place.

From a constitutional supremacy perspective, restrictive standing rules create a similar problem. Art. 4 establishes that the Constitution is supreme and inconsistent laws are void. But constitutional supremacy only works if there is a mechanism to identify and strike down inconsistent laws. If standing rules make it difficult to bring challenges, some unconstitutional laws may never be reviewed, creating a “legal vacuum” where unconstitutional laws remain in force simply because no one can meet standing requirements. Restrictive standing compounds other limitations on judicial review: combined with ouster clauses and non-justiciability, they create zones where constitutional compliance cannot be effectively tested, even where important constitutional questions are at stake.

This restrictive approach may be explained by the Singaporean judiciary’s judicial ideology. The courts have tended to adopt a more administrative, less confrontational posture, influenced by what has been described as a Confucian governance mindset and what Chan CJ has characterised as a “green light” approach to administrative law. On this view, constitutional law is not primarily to stop bad administrative practices but to encourage good ones: good government should be sought through the political process and public avenues, rather than by redressing bad government through the courts. The courts are not seen as the first line of defence against administrative abuses of power; **instead, control comes internally from Parliament and the Executive in upholding high standards of public administration (in other words, they check each other)**. This contrasts with the “red light” approach in the United Kingdom, where the courts exist in an adversarial relationship with the executive, functioning centrally as a check on administrative power. Singapore’s communitarian culture, as emphasised by CJ Menon, stresses dialogue, tolerance, compromise, and community above self. The “green light” approach fits this ethos: courts support good governance by articulating principles and serving as the last line of defence, but work collaboratively rather than adversarially with other branches.

*   Nonetheless, it is submitted that Chan Sek Keong’s logic that control can come internally from Parliament and the Executive is flawed – due to the fusion between the executive and legislature. Because the executive emerges from and typically dominates the legislature (when the governing party holds a majority), institutional checks between these branches weaken substantially. The primary remaining check operates through elections, where voters can replace the governing party with the opposition. But this check functions only if viable opposition exists and faces realistic prospects of forming an alternative government.
*   On this, the practical operation of this check in Singapore is further complicated by what Cherian George describes as a “legal-bureaucratic machinery” that imposes asymmetric costs on opposition politicians — not through threats to life and limb, but through “criminal prosecutions and civil suits” whose chilling effect “goes beyond these individuals, and cripples the opposition’s ability to attract serious candidates” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). Critically, George cites the US State Department’s assessment that the PAP has maintained dominance “in part by developing genuine voter support through honest, effective administration... and, in part, by manipulating the electoral framework, intimidating organised political opposition, and circumscribing the bounds of legitimate political discourse and action” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_). From a constitutional standpoint, this matters because popular sovereignty’s function as a political check presupposes not just formal electoral machinery but a competitive political environment in which the governing party faces credible accountability. Where the costs of opposition politics are structurally elevated through legal mechanisms, the electoral check is formally preserved but substantively weakened — which in turn affects how much weight the argument for political constitutionalism over legal constitutionalism can legitimately bear. If Parliament’s democratic mandate is itself produced by structurally constrained competition, deferring constitutional questions to Parliament on grounds of democratic legitimacy becomes harder to justify.
*   The other side of this argument is if the judiciary steps in instead of the two bodies, expanding judicial review to compensate for a weak political opposition risks the judiciary becoming a political actor itself, which violates the Separation of Powers from the other direction.

Counterarguments also exist. Standing requirements serve as a filtering mechanism, preventing frivolous litigation and ensuring courts focus on genuine disputes. From a separation of powers standpoint emphasising autonomy, some decisions should solely belong to the legislature or executive without judicial interference. Standing rules help maintain these boundaries by ensuring courts do not adjudicate every political question. In this view, restrictive standing rules protect the autonomy of each branch by preventing over-judicialisation of governance. If locus standi requirements are not strict, there is a risk of public interest litigation which turns the court into a forum of public debate — which is the realm of Parliament.

*   George’s account of what he terms the “depoliticisation” of Singapore society provides an important empirical context for evaluating locus standi restrictions. Drawing on political scientist Chan Heng Chee’s earlier work, George observes that what was initially a strategy to suppress communist influence progressively resulted in the broader curtailment of all political activity beyond that of the ruling party. The PAP’s approach to political engagement — where the government determines who has crossed from “observer” to “participant” and treats critical commentary as a political challenge requiring a robust response — creates what George calls “a political sphere that is sterile, in both senses of the word — not just antiseptically clean, but also devoid of vibrant life” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 32). The legal controls operate alongside non-legal mechanisms — security vetting, economic carrots and sticks, and what George terms a “whole-of-government approach to retribution” — that further discourage citizens from testing the boundaries of permissible engagement (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 33).
*   This broader context is constitutionally significant for the locus standi analysis because it suggests that restrictive standing requirements do not operate in a vacuum. The argument that locus standi rules merely filter out frivolous claims while preserving access for genuine disputes assumes that citizens who have genuine constitutional grievances will come forward to litigate them. But if the broader political environment systematically discourages engagement with contentious legal questions — through what George describes as a “fog of fear” encompassing both legal penalties and extra-legal consequences — then restrictive standing rules compound the chilling effect rather than merely performing a neutral filtering function. The “legal vacuum” problem — where unconstitutional laws may remain in force because no one can meet standing requirements — is exacerbated when potential litigants are further deterred by the wider costs of challenging the state. From a constitutional supremacy standpoint, this means that the effective scope of Article 4 depends not only on the formal availability of judicial review, but on whether citizens operate in an environment where they are willing and able to invoke it.

### Doctrine of Non-Justiciability

The doctrine of non-justiciability is a self-imposed limitation by the courts, distinguishable from ouster clauses which are legitimate limitations. Courts will not adjudicate on “hypothetical or non-justiciable issues involving purely moral, political or social rights” (_Chee Siok Chin_ \[2006\] at \[108\]). However, these instances should be limited. In _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2011\], the SHCA emphasised constitutionalism’s core feature of constitutional supremacy by opining that there “will or should be few, if any, legal disputes between the State and the people from which the judicial power is excluded” (at \[31\]).

There are “clearly provinces of executive decision-making that are, and should be, immune from judicial review” (_Review Publishing_ \[2007\] SGHC at \[95\]). The Court did not assume a categorial approach to deciding which cases are not justiciable; rather, it determined it was ultimately a fact-based inquiry. It gave general guiding principles: justiciability depends on the subject-matter in question, not the source of the decision-making power. Matters of “high policy” including the conduct of foreign affairs and issues pertaining to national defence, are typically immune from judicial review (_Review Publishing_).

*   However, a seemingly non-justiciable area may nonetheless be justiciable – for example, where an issue of international law touches on the application of domestic law, or where a pure question of law can be isolated from a generally non-justiciable area. This is not a complete deference or a purely categorical approach.

The legislature may sometimes make it clear that a question is reserved to the executive. Where it is the executive that has access to the best materials, its views should be regarded as highly persuasive, if not decisive. Courts should be cautious where matters involve government policy requiring the intricate balancing of competing policy considerations that judges are ill-equipped to adjudicate, are not traditionally regarded as falling within judicial purview, or where a judicial pronouncement could embarrass another branch of government or tie its hands in the conduct of affairs traditionally within its purview. Consciousness of the autonomy and deferential conception of the separation of powers in Westminster governance, and the judiciary’s lack of competence and political legitimacy, underpin this cautiousness. In all cases of judicial review, the court should exercise restraint and take cognisance of the fact that the system of government operates within the framework of three co-equal branches (**_Review Publishing_**).

*   This doctrine reflects SOP as deference, grounded in concerns about judicial competence and political legitimacy. However, it creates substantial grey areas where governmental action may escape meaningful review. The _Review Publishing_ emphasis on restraint where pronouncements could “embarrass” other branches or where courts lack “competence” is particularly problematic in a dominant party system. Deference through the separation of powers could allow governmental overreach in areas properly subject to review. Non-justiciability risks being applied too broadly, creating zones where constitutional compliance cannot be effectively tested. If the Constitution is truly supreme under Art. 4, one might argue that all governmental action should in principle be subject to review to ensure conformity, and that a broad non-justiciability doctrine sits uneasily with this commitment.
*   Cherian George’s analysis of the Elected Presidency provides a concrete illustration of this concern. When President Ong Teng Cheong encountered difficulties exercising his custodial powers over the reserves, the resulting disputes were resolved through a Constitutional tribunal and a White Paper rather than through ordinary judicial review — keeping the substantive constitutional questions largely outside the courts (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 8). The grey areas around presidential powers remain unresolved, with terms like “executive,” “ceremonial,” and “custodial” appearing nowhere in the Constitution itself but being deployed as political arguments by competing institutional actors.
*   The critical question is how the _Review Publishing_ framework applies to disputes of this kind. _Review Publishing_ holds that justiciability depends on subject-matter rather than source of decision-making power, and identifies matters of “high policy” — foreign affairs, national defence — as typically immune from review. But disputes about whether presidential custodial powers can be exercised meaningfully are not “high policy” in this sense. They are questions of constitutional interpretation: what powers does the Constitution confer on the presidency, and what are their limits? These are precisely the questions that Article 4 and Article 93 assign to the judiciary. The difficulty is that the political sensitivity of the presidency — particularly where it concerns the relationship between the president and the cabinet — creates pressure to treat these disputes as political rather than legal, even though the underlying questions are about the meaning of constitutional provisions. If non-justiciability is applied broadly to disputes involving the allocation of power between constitutional institutions, significant ambiguities will be resolved through political negotiation rather than principled legal adjudication. In such negotiations, the executive holds an overwhelming structural advantage: George notes that when Ong Teng Cheong attempted to exercise his custodial functions, Lee Kuan Yew insisted that “there can be only one centre of government in the country” — and the executive’s position prevailed. The _Review Publishing_ framework, properly applied, should distinguish between policy questions where courts genuinely lack competence and constitutional interpretation questions where the judiciary’s competence is not merely adequate but exclusive under Article 93. Non-justiciability, as a self-imposed judicial limitation, is most problematic precisely where it is most likely to be invoked: in politically charged disputes involving the balance of power among constitutional institutions, where the subject-matter is constitutional meaning rather than high policy, but where the political stakes create the strongest temptation toward deference.
*   _Note: probs can bring in some parts of the Cherian George and CSK eval from above_.

### Ouster Clauses

Ouster clauses are statutory provisions that purport to exclude or limit the jurisdiction of the courts to review executive or legislative decisions. They represent a legislative limitation on judicial power. An ouster clause, in essence, is a statutory provision that purports to prevent the courts from performing judicial review — or, as one might say, to oust the courts’ power of judicial review.

*   Examples in Singapore include Art. 149(3) of the Constitution (protecting anti-subversion legislation from challenge), section 104 of the Foreign Interference Countermeasures Act 2021 (“Limited Judicial Review”), section 8B of the Internal Security Act (limiting judicial review to procedural compliance), section 47 of the Industrial Relations Act 1960 (making arbitral awards final and not subject to prerogative writs), and section 30 of the Criminal Law (Temporary Provisions) Act (making the Minister’s detention decision final).
*   It is important to distinguish ouster clauses from statutory immunity clauses. In _Nagaenthran_ at \[50\], statutory immunity clauses were described as: (a) exceptional, in that they preclude claims being brought against certain classes of persons under prescribed conditions where such persons might otherwise be subject to some liability; (b) commonly seeking to protect persons carrying out public functions, justified by the desire not to hinder the discharge of such functions; and (c) generally not extending to the misuse or abuse of the public function in question, nor applying where the beneficiary exceeded the proper ambit of their office.

#### Arguments Against Ouster Clauses

Art. 93 of the Constitution vests judicial power in the judiciary. An ouster clause may therefore be inconsistent with Art. 93 as it amounts to an intrusion of the legislative branch into the powers of the judiciary, curtailing its power. Since judicial review examines the legality rather than the merits of government action, it serves as the essential mechanism for ensuring that other branches act within the scope of their legal power and adhere to the Constitution. Ouster clauses thus strip away the judiciary’s core functions and run counter to the SOP principle, which is likely part of the basic structure of Singapore’s Westminster form of government as recognised in _Yong Vui Kong_ \[2015\]: the separation of powers is fundamental and essential for the system of government. Even adopting Chan CJ’s “green light” approach, if judicial review is meant to contribute to good governance, it should not be prevented. As _Chng Suan Tze_ established, all powers have legal limits – ouster clauses conflict with this rule of law principle.

In practice, the courts often omit to give effect to ouster clauses and therefore hold that they can perform judicial review of the executive action in question.

1.  A court can hold that the provision, properly interpreted, doesn’t actually have the effect of ousting judicial review. For example, a court may hold that the word “decision” only refers to lawfully-made decisions and not unlawful ones;
2.  A court can hold that the provision is unconstitutional. The Singapore courts have not yet explicitly said such a thing, but it is likely that they will. In _Nagaenthran_ \[2019\] at \[74\], the Court of Appeal described an ouster clause as being “constitutionally suspect for being in violation of Art 93 of the Singapore Constitution as well as the principle of the separation of powers”.

Benjamin Ong (2019) in _The constitutionality of ouster clauses_ also supports this position.

1.  Art. 93 says that the Supreme Court (part of which is the High Court) have judicial power. The power to perform judicial review is, by definition (through conventional usage), part of the High Court’s judicial power. (_Tan Seet Eng_ at \[1\] says so: “Judges are entrusted with the task of ensuring that any exercise of state power is done within legal limits.”) So an ouster clause would stop the High Court from exercising the judicial power that the High Court, by definition, has.
2.  If some authority’s decisions are immune from judicial review, then they have the final power to determine what their legal limits are. If someone has the final power to determine what their legal limits are, then they, in effect, exercise judicial power. In other words, if a statutory provision prevents judicial review of some executive authority’s decision, then its effect is to confer judicial power on that executive authority. That would run contrary to Art. 93, which, properly interpreted, vests judicial power exclusively in the courts — for an executive authority is not a court.
3.  Ong argues that, because the courts alone possess the judicial power to authoritatively state the law under Art. 93, all errors of law committed by the executive should be considered jurisdictional errors. He also argues that the High Court in _Nagaenthran_ \[2018\] distorted the “green-light” theory by Chan Sek Keong to justify its stance that some errors of law cannot be reviewed – when in fact, this model does not grant the judiciary a license to stand idly by in the face of unlawful administrative action. Ong clarifies that, at most, the “green-light” approach advocates for a greater degree of curial deference when the court is considering whether an error of law has actually been committed, but it absolutely cannot justify a total refusal to consider the question in the first place. Indeed, he posits that the fundamental objective of the “green-light” theory – which is to help articulate clear rules and principles to guide good government practices – would be far better served if ouster clauses were rendered ineffective, thereby allowing the courts to pronounce on the legality of executive actions and provide necessary guidance on proper procedures and relevant considerations.
    1.  CSK: “I do not wish to suggest an answer but will provide an academic argument (on which I express no view), viz, that an ouster clause may be inconsistent with Art 93 of the Constitution, which vests the judicial power of Singapore in the Supreme Court. If this argument is correct (and it has not been tested in a court of law), it would follow that the supervisory jurisdiction of the courts cannot be ousted, and therefore there is no need for our courts to draw the distinction between jurisdictional and non-jurisdictional errors of law. As I said earlier,I express no opinion on this issue, but this should be a good examination question. You can see that adopting the analysis in Anisminic would adroitly bypass this thorny issue.”

Ultimately, Ong maintains that upholding ouster clauses undermines the rule of law, subverts constitutional supremacy, and disables the Judiciary from pronouncing on the legality of demonstrably unlawful government action.

#### Arguments For Ouster Clauses

Adopting a strict conception of the separation of powers rooted in deference and competence, certain matters may be better placed within the purview of the legislature or executive, such as matters of national security. The court may lack the competence to deal with polycentric issues (**_Teo Soh Lung_**). Furthermore, if the basic structure must be something “fundamental and essential” to the Westminster form of government, certain ouster clauses may be justified on the basis that they do not concern matters that are truly “fundamental and essential” (**_Yong Vui Kong_**).

### Methods of Circumvention (for Ouster Clauses)

Courts may circumvent ouster clauses in two principal ways. The first is to “read down” the clause: for example, if a statute provides that a Minister’s decision is “final,” the court may interpret this as precluding only an appeal while still permitting judicial review. The second is to construe the provision as an immunity clause rather than an ouster clause (as in section 33B(4) of the MDA) – which is what the SGCA did in _Nagaenthran_ \[2019\] SGCA.

| Nagaenthran a/l K Darmalingam v PP & Anor [2019] 2 SLR 216 (SGCA) |
| --- |
| Facts: The Public Prosecutor informed the appellant that he would not be issuing a certificate of substantive assistance under section 33B(2)(b) of the MDA. The appellant sought leave to commence judicial review proceedings against the PP’s non-certification decision.Held: The Court of Appeal held that on its face, section 33B(4) does not purport to exclude the jurisdiction of the courts to supervise the legality of the PP’s determination. It immunises the PP from suit save on stated grounds (at [51]). The process by which the PP arrives at his decision cannot be subject to the court’s determination, because this might require the disclosure of confidential information and otherwise inadmissible evidence including intelligence and operational details, which might jeopardise the CNB’s effectiveness (at [57]). The courts are “simply ill-equipped and ill-placed to undertake such an inquiry” (at [58]). Crucially, the provisions do not oust the court’s power of judicial review over the legality of executive actions (at [68]). If the PP had disregarded relevant considerations and/or failed to take relevant considerations into account, it would be inconceivable that the aggrieved person would be left without a remedy (at [69]).At [71], the Court emphasised that “the court’s power of judicial review, which is a core aspect of the judicial power and function, would not ordinarily be capable of being excluded by ordinary legislation such as the MDA.” This follows inevitably from Singapore’s system of constitutional governance, where the Constitution is the supreme law as stated in Art. 4. At [74], the Court stated that if section 33B(4) really did oust the court’s power of judicial review, it “would be constitutionally suspect for being in violation of Art 93 of the Singapore Constitution as well as the principle of separation of powers.” This dicta endorses the view that ouster clauses may be inherently inconsistent with Art. 93.Significance: The interpretive strategy preserved both parliamentary intent and constitutional principles by construing the provision as not ousting review of legality (as opposed to merits). However, this case also illustrates the “strong principles, narrow application” pattern: while Nagaenthran articulated powerful protective principles, the court held the clause was an immunity clause, not an ouster clause, and therefore the principles did not directly apply. The clause remained in effect. Basic structure arguments could potentially be invoked against ouster clauses, but their application to ordinary legislation remains uncertain. |

##### Partial Ouster Clauses?

Ong strongly disagrees with the High Court’s view in _Nagaenthran_ \[2018\] that a “partial” ouster clause is valid simply because it leaves a few grounds for review open (like bad faith or malice) to strike a “reasonable balance”. Ong counters that judicial power is granted by the Constitution in absolute terms, meaning it cannot be treated as a right subject to proportionality or balancing tests.

*   Ong mitigates the severe implications of the High Court’s ruling by pointing to an underdeveloped, yet potentially powerful, aspect of Singaporean public law: an expanded definition of “unconstitutionality”. Even though the partial ouster clause in Section 33B(4) expressly preserves judicial review only for bad faith, malice, and unconstitutionality , Ong demonstrates how existing case law, such as _Tan Guat Neo Phyllis_ and _Yong Vui Kong_, has implicitly broadened what it means for an executive act to be unconstitutional. In these cases, the courts have treated unconstitutionality as encompassing not only direct violations of explicit constitutional rights (like the rights to life or equality before the law), but also breaches of broader, unwritten constitutional norms. Under this expanded definition, executive decisions tainted by arbitrariness, bias, or a failure to direct the decision-maker’s mind to a pertinent issue can be struck down as unconstitutional. Ultimately, Ong concludes that these unwritten norms establish an immutable minimum standard for executive conduct, ensuring that even in the presence of an ouster clause, a wider avenue for judicial review remains available to hold public power accountable.

The cumulative effect of ouster clauses, broad non-justiciability doctrines, and restrictive standing rules creates tension with de jure constitutional supremacy. If the Constitution is truly supreme, all governmental action should in principle be subject to review ensuring constitutional conformity. Extensive ouster clauses create zones where constitutional compliance cannot be effectively tested. Even the strong presumption of constitutionality — though now weakened to “no more than a starting point” by _Jolovan Wham_ — sits uneasily with constitutional supremacy, since all powers should be actively examined for constitutional compliance rather than presumed valid until proven otherwise. While _Nagaenthran_ provides reassurance through a narrow interpretation, the broader question remains whether the combined effect of these limitations unduly constrains the judiciary’s constitutional role.

## Prerogative Powers and Prosecutorial Discretion

### Prerogative Powers

Prerogative powers are those belonging to the executive realm that are not ordinarily subject to judicial review. These include the President’s discretion to appoint a Prime Minister, the Cabinet’s decision to recommend a grant of pardon to the President, and the Attorney-General’s power to prosecute an individual and determine what charges are to be preferred.

| Mathavakannan s/o Kalimuthu v AG [2012] SGHC |
| --- |
| Held: The basis of section 238 of the CPC is section 28 of the Republic of Singapore Independence Act (RSIA), read with the grant of pardon under section 8 of the RSIA. It is clear from section 8(1)(b) that the President’s power of clemency is untrammelled: the President, acting on the advice of the Cabinet, may grant a pardon, free or subject to lawful conditions, or any reprieve or respite, either indefinitely or for such period as the President may think fit. The President is empowered to commute a capital sentence to, inter alia, an imprisonment term of any period and is not bound, when commuting a capital sentence, to impose life imprisonment in accordance with how that term was understood under law at the time of commutation. It is possible to commute to a sentence lower than life imprisonment. |

### Prosecutorial Discretion

Prosecutorial power is a constitutional power under Art. 35(8) of the Constitution, affirmed in _Ramalingam_ \[2012\] SGCA. It is constitutionally equal in status to the judicial power set out in Art. 93 and forms part of the executive power. Both Parliament and the Executive have equal status under the Constitution; neither may interfere with the other’s functions or intrude upon its powers, subject only to the constitutional power of the court to prevent the prosecutorial power from being exercised unconstitutionally (**_Phyllis Tan_** at \[144\]).

#### Prosecutorial Discretion Subject to Judicial Review in Two Situations

The prosecutorial power however is not immune to judicial correction. It is subject to judicial review in two situations (_Phyllis Tan_ at \[149\]).

#### (1) Where it is exercised arbitrarily, in bad faith, or ultra vires.

In _Ramalingam_, the SGHC found that in the context of Art. 12(1), the AG in his capacity as Public Prosecutor must give unbiased consideration to every offender and avoid taking into account any irrelevant consideration (_Ramalingam_ at \[51\]). Where several offenders are involved in the same criminal enterprise, the Prosecutor must compare and treat like with like, and must not unlawfully discriminate against one offender as compared to another (at \[52\]). Multiple factors may legitimately be taken into account, including the sufficiency of evidence, personal circumstances, the willingness of one offender to testify against co-offenders, and other policy factors (at \[52\]). The AG’s final decision will be constrained by what the public interest requires (at \[53\]).

#### (2) Where its exercise contravenes constitutional rights and protections

Prosecutorial power is reviewable where its exercise contravenes constitutional rights and protections, thus making its exercise unconstitutional – for example, a discriminatory prosecution that deprives an accused of their right to equality under Art. 12

##### Different Charges Does Not Violate Art. 12

The SGHC in _Ramalingam_ found that Art. 12 is not infringed if there are grounds for the prosecutor to bring different charges against different accused persons. As long as the prosecutor can articulate some rational basis for treating two accused persons differently (e.g. played a more significant role, cooperated with authorities), Art. 12 is not violated – it has a very low threshold. The accused faces a heavy burden – essentially, the prosecutor only needs to offer some explanation for the differential treatment, and the explanation need not be particularly compelling.

| Ramalingam Ravinthran v AG [2012] 2 SLR 49 (SGCA) |
| --- |
| Facts: The applicant alleged a violation of Art. 12(1) arising from the AG’s decision, in his capacity as PP, to charge the applicant with capital offences while charging another person (Sundar), involved in the same criminal enterprise, with non-capital offences.Procedural issue: The substantive issue concerned a constitutional point (the interaction between prosecutorial discretion in Art 35(8) and the right to equality under Art 12(1)) which the applicant could have raised during trial and on appeal (at [16]). However, since this motion involved capital offences, the Court decided to hear it as the substantive issue needed to be examined in greater detail and clarified in the public interest (at [17]).Held: Sundar was a prosecution witness against the applicant at trial. It is a common and well-known practice for the Prosecution to take into account an offender’s willingness to testify against co-offenders when determining what charges to bring. The motion was accordingly dismissed. |
| Quek Hock Lye v PP [2012] 2 SLR 1012 (SGCA) |
| --- |
| Facts: The appellant was convicted of conspiring with his accomplice Winai to traffic in stolen controlled drugs and was sentenced to the mandatory death penalty. He appealed against the sentence, arguing that the PP had violated Art. 12 by exercising prosecutorial discretion in formulating an “artificial charge” against him.Held (Art 12 argument): The applicant had not discharged his burden of establishing a prima facie case that the PP had infringed Art. 12(1) by charging him with a capital charge while sparing Winai. The applicant was the brain behind the criminal enterprise and thus the main culprit. Winai’s willingness to testify was a relevant consideration (at [25]).Held (Art 93 argument): The applicant also argued that the PP’s actions in formulating a drug trafficking charge just under the amount necessary to trigger the mandatory death penalty contravened the separation of powers and breached Art. 93 by usurping the judicial function of sentencing. The Court rejected this: Art. 35(8) confers upon the AG a constitutional power to exercise discretion which impacts the sentencing range available to the court. The judiciary does not possess the power or jurisdiction to formulate or prefer charges against accused persons — that is the constitutional provenance of the AG (at [29]). Art. 93 and 35(8) should be construed harmoniously, with neither subordinate to the other (at [29]). |

##### The Prosecutor has Discretion to Bring the Charge – it is his Constitutional Power

In _Quek Hock Lye_ \[2012\], the SGCA found that the PP’s actions in formulating a lower charge to avoid the mandatory death penalty was **not** a breach of SOP and did not breach Art. 93 of the Constitution as it did not usurp the judicial function of sentencing – rather, the AG had the constitutional power to exercise discretion with charging the offence, and the judiciary does not have the jurisdiction to formulate charges anyway.

| Quek Hock Lye v PP [2012] 2 SLR 1012 (SGCA) |
| --- |
| Facts: The appellant was convicted of conspiring with his accomplice Winai to traffic in stolen controlled drugs and was sentenced to the mandatory death penalty. He appealed against the sentence, arguing that the PP had violated Art. 12 by exercising prosecutorial discretion in formulating an “artificial charge” against him.Held (Art 12 argument): The applicant had not discharged his burden of establishing a prima facie case that the PP had infringed Art. 12(1) by charging him with a capital charge while sparing Winai. The applicant was the brain behind the criminal enterprise and thus the main culprit. Winai’s willingness to testify was a relevant consideration (at [25]).Held (Art 93 argument): The applicant also argued that the PP’s actions in formulating a drug trafficking charge just under the amount necessary to trigger the mandatory death penalty contravened the separation of powers and breached Art. 93 by usurping the judicial function of sentencing. The Court rejected this: Art. 35(8) confers upon the AG a constitutional power to exercise discretion which impacts the sentencing range available to the court. The judiciary does not possess the power or jurisdiction to formulate or prefer charges against accused persons — that is the constitutional provenance of the AG (at [29]). Art. 93 and 35(8) should be construed harmoniously, with neither subordinate to the other (at [29]). |

##### What if Prosecutor moves cases between different courts?

In the case where the Prosecutor tries to move the case between different courts, it is likely that the Courts will find this is a contravention of SOP, as it is an encroachment on judicial power (_Dato Yap Peng_). This is because the ability to select the court in which the case is heard might effectively allow the prosecution to influence outcomes by choosing more favourable adjudicative forums, which is indeed a potential encroachment on judicial power following the court in _Dato Yap Peng_.

#### Prosecutorial Decisions have a Presumption of Constitutionality

The courts presume that the Attorney-General’s prosecutorial decisions are constitutional or lawful until shown to be otherwise (_Ramalingam_ at \[44\]). This accords with the established principle that acts of high officials of state should be accorded a presumption of legality or regularity, especially where carried out in the exercise of constitutional powers (at \[46\]). The presumption extends in the same way as the presumption of constitutionality accorded to statutes (at \[48\]). The applicant must specifically produce prima facie evidence of bias or the taking into account of irrelevant considerations. Mere differentiation of charges between co-offenders is not sufficient to constitute prima facie evidence of bias (at \[70\]).

*   Nonetheless, it is likely that the presumption of constitutionality will now be treated as “no more than a starting point”, given the SGCA’s decision in _Jolovan Wham_ \[2021\].

### Prosecutorial Discretion Structurally Limits Judicial Power

Prosecutorial discretion limits judicial review through a separation of powers mechanism. The AG’s power to decide which cases to bring and which charges to file directly affects the judiciary’s opportunity to hear cases and adjudicate disputes. If the prosecutor decides not to bring charges against person B (even though B was involved in the same criminal enterprise as person A who is being prosecuted), the judiciary has no case to hear regarding person B. The fundamental judicial function — adjudication of disputes, finding facts, applying law — is not triggered. Prosecution decisions thus control the judiciary’s docket and limit judicial oversight.

The case of **_Quah Wee Kee v PP_** raised this argument directly: the appellant contended that if prosecutorial discretion is so broad that courts effectively cannot review a significant category of executive decisions (decisions about whom to prosecute), this may be inconsistent with Art. 93’s vesting of judicial power. The court rejected this, holding that the exercise of judicial power on sentencing follows the charge put forward by the prosecution. It is the prosecution’s role to decide on charges, and the judge merely applies the law to sentencing. This is framed as respecting separation of powers — each branch has its role. But it also means judicial power is constrained by prosecutorial choices.

*   From a SOP perspective that emphasises deference or even autonomy (as SG courts have recently shifted to), broad prosecutorial discretion is justified. The AG’s high constitutional office, on par with the judiciary, warrants deference in the case of charges. However, this arrangement does create rule of law concerns. RoL requires that all powers be subject to legal limits and not be exercised arbitrarily. Broad, largely unreviewable prosecutorial discretion raises concerns about arbitrary exercise of power. The combination of low thresholds for rebutting Art. 12 challenges, high burdens on challengers to show an abuse of power, and the presumption of constitutionality means that prosecutorial discretion is insulated from meaningful judicial review. If the Constitution is truly supreme, this structural limitation on the judiciary’s capacity to check executive action is a source of tension. There are also Art. 93 concerns, similar to those raised regarding ouster clauses: if prosecutorial discretion is so broad that courts effectively cannot review a significant category of executive decisions, this may be inconsistent with Art. 93’s vesting of judicial power in the courts.
*   Cherian George’s account of how the PAP manages political opposition provides concrete illustrations of why rule of law concerns around prosecutorial discretion are not merely theoretical. George describes a pattern whereby the government deploys legal proceedings — both criminal prosecutions and civil defamation suits — as targeted instruments against political opponents and critical voices. The authorities’ preferred response to political challenges, George argues, is not the blunt instrument of detention without trial but rather the precise deployment of legal processes: the government “would settle scores later, in the courts rather than on the streets” (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 3). George describes this as the core of “calibrated coercion” — a system in which the legal-bureaucratic machinery imposes asymmetric costs on those who challenge the dominant party, not through threats to physical safety but through the weight and expense of legal proceedings.
*   The constitutional significance of this for the prosecutorial discretion analysis is twofold. First, it demonstrates that the AG’s prosecutorial power does not merely determine the judiciary’s docket in a neutral sense; it also determines which political actors face the costs and reputational consequences of criminal proceedings. If prosecutorial decisions are effectively unreviewable — owing to the high presumption of constitutionality, the low threshold for rebutting Article 12 challenges, and the heavy evidential burden on the accused — then the AG’s constitutional power under Article 35(8) operates as a largely unchecked mechanism for imposing legal costs on selected individuals. Second, it complicates the separation of powers justification for broad prosecutorial discretion. The autonomy argument holds that the AG’s constitutional office warrants deference because it is a co-equal branch exercising its proper function. But if prosecutorial power is exercised within a broader political strategy of calibrated legal action against opponents — even if each individual prosecution is technically lawful — the aggregate effect is that the executive deploys judicial processes instrumentally, and the judiciary’s checking function is structurally constrained by the cases the executive chooses to bring before it.

### Doctrine of Prospective Overruling

Where a statute is declared unconstitutional, that part which is unconstitutional is void. It is regarded as being void _ab initio_ and theoretically, the effect is as if the provision had never existed. The difficulty with such retrospective application is that certain statutes may have stood for many years before being declared unconstitutional. Many individuals, lawmakers, and administrators would have relied on the impugned provision and regulated their activities accordingly. To circumvent the problem of retrospectively reversing a whole series of acts and decisions, the doctrine of prospective overruling was developed by the United States Supreme Court in _Linkletter v Walker_ (1965) 381 US 618. In Singapore, the doctrine was first applied in _PP v Manogaran_. The rationale is straightforward: if a past case is overruled, crimes may effectively be created retroactively, prejudicing those who conducted themselves in reliance on past decisions. Through prospective overruling, such prejudice is limited. All acts subsequent to the judgement overruling the law will be governed by the new law, while all acts prior would be governed by the older law (_PP v Manogaran_).

*   The SGCA refused to apply these amendments retrospectively in _Abdul Nasir_. If the first time interpretation of a particular punishment would result in an expanded meaning, contrary to what people reasonably and legitimately expected all along, then such a judicial pronouncement must be given prospective effect to prevent effect to prevent prejudice and injustice (at \[58\]). After all, the accused persons in _Abdul Nasir_ who pleaded guilty expecting life imprisonment to be 20 years, not actually for life. It would be markedly unfair.

| PP v Manogaran s/o R Ramu (SGCA) |
| --- |
| Facts: The respondent was charged with possession of cannabis mixture under the MDA. A major issue was the applicability of the Court of Appeal’s decision in Abdul Raman bin Yusof v PP [1996] 2 SLR(R) 538 which held that the words “cannabis mixture” contemplated the co-existence of two or more separate vegetable matters, of both cannabis and non-cannabis. The Court overruled its own decision in Abdul Raman prospectively.Key Principles: Art. 11(1) embodies the nullum crimen sine lege principle: conduct cannot be punished as criminal unless some rule of law has already declared it criminal and punishable as such (at [61]). By judicial overruling, new law may be pronounced for the first time. If a person organises their affairs in accordance with an existing judicial pronouncement, their actions should not be impugned retrospectively by a subsequent pronouncement, without them being given the opportunity to reorganise. This seeks to protect reasonable and legitimate expectations (at [74]). In addition, in the case of new enacted legislation which creates criminal liability, proper notification must be given prior to its commencement (at [74]). The nullum principle requires that citizens must be “able, as far as possible, to foresee the consequences of their conduct in order that persons be given fair notice of what to avoid” (at [74]). Hence, courts are precluded from retrospectively reversing a previous interpretation of a criminal statutory provision where the new interpretation creates criminal liability for the first time, and where it would operate to the prejudice of an accused (at [75]).Application: The effect of overruling Abdul Raman was to extend the scope of criminal liability in respect of trafficking in cannabis mixture. If liability were imposed retrospectively, the guilt or innocence of accused persons may well be affected by arbitrary factors such as administrative efficiency or prosecutorial discretion (at [77]). Based on Art. 11(1) and the nullum principle, prospective overruling was mandated. All acts subsequent to the judgment would be governed by the new law; all acts prior would be governed by the law as stated in Abdul Raman (at [86]). The respondent’s acquittal was upheld on the premise that the applicable law at the time of his alleged offence was as stated in Abdul Raman (at [88]). |
| Abdul Nasir bin Amer Hamsah v PP (SGCA) |
| --- |
| Issue: Whether a first-time judicial pronouncement on the meaning of life imprisonment should be given retroactive or prospective effect, given that existing practice had been to equate life imprisonment with 20 years’ imprisonment.Arguments for retrospective application: It could be argued that the declaration would not actually pronounce any new law, but merely maintain and expound an old one — the court would merely be making clear what has always been the law (at [45]). It could also be argued that those found guilty and sentenced to life imprisonment must take the risk that “life” could mean until the end of their natural life (at [46]).Held: These arguments could not be accepted. If the first-time interpretation of a particular punishment would result in an expanded meaning, contrary to what could be reasonably and legitimately expected all along — such as by accused persons who decided to plead guilty on the basis that life imprisonment meant 20 years, or prisoners presently serving life sentences who had been assured by lawyers accordingly — then such a judicial pronouncement must be given prospective effect to prevent prejudice and injustice (at [58]). |

## Judicial Independence

Judicial independence is essential for the judiciary to effectively perform its constitutional functions. Independence has multiple dimensions: independence from other branches of government, from external pressures (parties, pressure groups), from internal pressures within the judiciary, and personal independence of individual judges from their own biases.

*   Understanding judicial independence requires examining why it is important, how constitutional design can ensure it, and whether current mechanisms are effective.

### Why is Judicial Indep. Important?

Judicial Independence is tied to both SOP and Constitutional Supremacy.

Under SOP, the judiciary is a **co-equal** branch whose function is to check whether the other two branches have acted in accordance with the law and the Constitution. If the judiciary is not independent, then it cannot perform its checking function credibly.

*   An executive-dependent judiciary cannot effectively check executive actions; a legislature-dependent judiciary cannot effectively review legislation. Co-equality requires autonomy, and autonomy requires independence.

For Constitutional Supremacy, if the Constitution is truly supreme, all governmental action should in principle be subject to review ensuring constitutional conformity. As such, if the Judiciary has this power – they must be independent to ensure that this process has integrity.

Independence is also essential for the integrity of legal processes and the judicial system. Justice must not only be done but must be seen to be done. If judges are subject to external pressures from parties, interest groups, or government officials, the integrity of adjudication is compromised. If judges are subject to internal pressures from senior judges or judicial hierarchies, individual judges cannot exercise independent judgment. If judges cannot set aside personal views and apply law impartially, the system loses legitimacy. All three types of independence — from external actors, from internal hierarchies, from personal biases — are necessary for a legitimate legal system.

Finally, judicial independence maintains public confidence in the legal system, which enhances the rule of law. Respect for and subjection to law can only be sustained if a neutral institution exists to ensure that law is respected and enforced against all – and that institution is the judiciary (Chan Sek Keong, 2010). When the public trusts that courts are independent and impartial, they are more likely to accept judicial decisions even when they disagree with outcomes. This public confidence is essential for the rule of law to function.

### Constitutional Mechanisms for Judicial Independence

Judicial Independence in Singapore is ensured and maintained through constitutional design mechanisms. These relate to appointment processes, remuneration, security of tenure, and removal processes.

*   Evaluate SG’s arrangements against theoretical best practice sand identify potential vulnerabilities.

#### (a) Appointment Mechanisms of SC Judges

Under Art. 95(1) and (2) of the Constitution, all Supreme Court Judges, Judicial Commissioners, and Senior Judges are appointed by the President acting in his discretion, provided he concurs with the advice of the Prime Minister. Before tendering advice, the PM must consult the Chief Justice (unless the CJ is being appointed) under Art. 95(6). To qualify as a Supreme Court judge, an individual must, for an aggregate period of not less than ten years, have been a qualified person under section 2 of the Legal Professions Act or a member of the Singapore Legal Service (Art. 96).

In many Westminster systems, judges are formally appointed by the head of state upon the advice of the Prime Minister. This means the PM (and CJ) effectively chooses judges. Concentrating appointment power in one executive official raises concerns: the PM could appoint loyalists or ideologically aligned judges, undermining independence. The theoretical best practice is to diffuse or share appointment power among multiple branches. For example, in Indonesia, the Constitutional Court has nine judges: three nominated by the judiciary, three by the executive, and three by the legislature. This ensures no single branch controls judicial appointments.

#### (b) Judicial Appointments Commissions – Alternative

Some jurisdictions use judicial appointments commissions (or judicial services commissions) as an additional layer in the appointment process. The commission typically screens candidates and provides a shortlist to the executive, which then selects from that list. This can strengthen independence by adding expert scrutiny and reducing unilateral executive control. The UK and Malaysia have such commissions. However, commissions do not automatically ensure independence. It matters who appoints commission members and who has final say. If the executive appoints all commission members, the commission may simply rubber-stamp executive preferences. If its recommendations are non-binding and the executive can reject lists and demand new ones (as has happened in Malaysia), the commission’s filtering function is weakened. Whether a judicial appointments commission enhances independence therefore depends on its design: composition, how members are selected, and whether recommendations are binding.

#### (c) Security of Tenure and Remuneration Maintains Independence of Supreme Court

Unless they resign or are removed, Supreme Court Judges hold office until the age of 65 years (Art. 98(1A)). The office of a Supreme Court Judge shall not be abolished during their continuance in office (Art. 95(3)). The Legislature is required by law to provide for the remuneration of Supreme Court Judges, and such remuneration shall be charged on the Consolidated Fund (Art. 98(6)). The remuneration and other terms of office, including any pension or gratuity, shall not be altered to a judge’s disadvantage after appointment (Art. 98(7)). These provisions bolster judges’ confidence, reduce the risk of corruption, and serve as recognition of the judiciary as an independent, co-equal arm of government (Chan Sek Keong, 2010). Because Supreme Court judges know they are unlikely to be removed and have guaranteed remuneration, they are more likely to maintain independence and resist external influence.

#### (d) Remuneration and Financial Control lies with Executive – Issue

Who controls judicial finances and resources matters for independence. If one branch controls judicial budgets, this creates potential for pressure or leverage. In Singapore, the executive and Parliament control judicial finances: the Ministry of Law proposes budgets that Parliament passes. Currently, the government has been relatively generous with judicial resources. However, Chan Sek Keong’s writings caution against funding prerogatives lying with other branches, particularly in Westminster systems where executive and legislative powers are fused. Giving one fused power centre control over judicial resources poses separation of powers risks.

#### (e) Exceptions: Senior Judges and Judicial Commissioners

Senior Judges are appointed under Art. 95(2), and the Chief Justice may, from time to time, require a Senior Judge appointed for a specified period to hear and determine any specific case or classes of cases (Art. 95(9)).

Judicial Commissioners are appointed under Art. 95(4) on fixed terms by the President on the advice of the PM. A Judicial Commissioner may be appointed to hear and determine a specific case only, or for a specified period (Art. 95(5)). Unlike Supreme Court Judges, Judicial Commissioners do not have security of tenure, and their privileges and immunities last only for the duration of their appointment.

*   This creates potential for cherry-picking judges for sensitive cases — the government could theoretically assign specific cases to a Judicial Commissioner and then allow them to return to practice.

The lower judiciary also does not have security of tenure, and career advancement depends on the Legal Services Commission, of which the Attorney-General (as part of the Executive) is a member.

*   However, the Chief Justice heads the Legal Services Commission and has the final say (Chan Sek Keong, 2010). Furthermore, as a small state, it may not be practical for Singapore to maintain a separate judicial and legal service. Nevertheless, the structural connection between judicial career progression and executive input creates at least an appearance of potential influence.

#### (f) Process of Removal of Supreme Court Judges

Under Art. 98(3), the Prime Minister, or the Chief Justice after consulting the Prime Minister, may represent to the President that a judge should be removed on grounds of misbehaviour or inability from infirmity of body or mind, or any other cause, to properly discharge the functions of office. The President appoints a tribunal, and may on the recommendation of the tribunal remove the person from office.

The tribunal must consist of not fewer than five persons who hold or have held office as Supreme Court Judge or any person with equivalent office in any part of the Commonwealth, presided by the Chief Justice or the next most senior judge (Art. 98(4)). Pending reference and report from the tribunal, the President, acting in his discretion and concurring with the PM or CJ’s advice, may suspend the judge from the exercise of their functions (Art. 98(5)). The conduct of a Supreme Court judge cannot be discussed in Parliament except on a substantive motion of which notice has been given by not less than one-quarter of the total number of Members of Parliament (Art. 99).

#### (g) Removal Process May be Weaponised: The Malaysian Judicial Crisis of 1988

The Malaysian Judicial Crisis of 1988 provides a stark illustration of how the removal process can be weaponised to undermine judicial independence. Following elections in which certain branches of UMNO (the ruling political party) were found to be unregistered, the party was deemed illegal. This triggered a broader political conflict that escalated into attacks on the judiciary. When the Lord President (the Malaysian equivalent of Chief Justice) wrote a letter to the Yang di-Pertuan Agong protesting executive interference with the judiciary, the Prime Minister initiated removal proceedings against him. The Lord President was suspended, and a tribunal was convened. Several other Supreme Court judges who attempted to form an emergency panel to challenge the tribunal’s jurisdiction were themselves removed.

The grounds for removal were deemed to be the writing of the letter itself and the judges’ absence from their posts to form the special tribunal. This episode demonstrates the critical importance of robust removal procedures with multiple institutional checks. Where the power to initiate and control the removal process is concentrated in the executive, the formal protections of security of tenure can be rendered illusory. The Malaysian crisis underscores why Singapore’s requirement for a tribunal of at least five judicial-equivalent persons, presided by the Chief Justice, represents an important safeguard — though its effectiveness ultimately depends on the willingness of the individuals involved to resist executive pressure.

### Leadership of the Judiciary

The Chief Justice sets the tone for judicial discretion and conduct. One potential issue is the CJ’s role in assigning cases, which could in theory raise concerns about manipulation of case allocation. However, in the Singapore context, where Confucian values are widely accepted, leaders are perceived to have great moral authority.

### Behaviour of Individual Judges

Constitutional Design cannot fully determine judicial behaviour. Even with perfect design mechanisms for independence, how judges actually behave may diverge from expectations. Theories of judicial behaviour show this complexity.

#### Attitudinal Model

The attitudinal model posits that judges decide cases purely according to their personal or ideological preferences and motivations. In this view, judges do not objectively apply law but rather implement their own values through judicial decision-making.

*   This raises questions about personal independence – if judges simply follow their ideologies, are they truly independent from their own biases? Formal independence from external actors may be insufficient if judges are captive to their own ideological commitments.

#### The formalistic model

The formalistic model represents the opposite extreme – judges simply apply the law mechanically and act completely apolitically. They interpret laws literally, divorced from substantive considerations or policy implications. While this is the idealised judicial role in many system, it has been criticised as unrealistic.

*   Legal texts are often ambiguous, and interpretation necessarily involves judgement calls that cannot be entirely divorced from judges’ understandings of law’s purposes and effects.

#### Strategic Model

The strategic model offers a more complex view: law matters and judges do apply law, but in the process of interpretation and application, judges strategically consider how other branches of government will react. Faced with a choice between interpretation A and interpretation B, judges may avoid A if they anticipate that it will provoke retaliation from the executive or legislature (e.g., constitutional amendments to override the decision, budget cuts, public attacks on the judiciary). Judges instead choose the safer interpretation B.

*   Why? Because judges want to build institutional power and legitimacy over time. By avoiding confrontations in the short term, they preserve their authority for future cases. The strategic model sees judges as rational actors who calculate costs and benefits in both legal and political-institutional terms.

#### Implication of Judge Behaviour

These behavioural theories complicate the assessment of judicial independence. Even if constitutional design perfectly insulates judges from formal removal or budget pressures, judges might still be influenced by ideological preferences (attitudinal model) or by strategic calculations about institutional survival (strategic model). True independence would require both structural protections (constitutional design) and behavioural integrity (judges who can set aside biases and resist strategic pressures). The difficulty is that behavioural factors are harder to observe and regulate than formal constitutional structures.

*   The common claim that “Singapore judges are not independent because they always rule for the government” is too simplistic. Judges could be structurally independent from the executive but still reach decisions that align with government preferences because: (1) the law genuinely supports the government’s position; (2) judges share philosophical or ideological perspectives with the government (attitudinal model); or (3) judges are behaving strategically to preserve institutional power (strategic model). One must distinguish between **formal independence** (structural protections against interference) and **substantive independence** (willingness to rule against government when the law requires).
*   Cherian George’s broader analysis of Singapore’s political system provides further reason for caution. The strategic model of judicial behaviour — where judges calibrate their interpretations to avoid provoking retaliation from the political branches — takes on particular salience in what George describes as a system of “calibrated coercion” where each institution is progressively brought under executive influence through targeted legal and structural adjustments. George documents how the PAP systematically neutralised potential countervailing institutions — labour unions, the press, universities, the legal profession, religious organisations — not primarily through frontal assaults, but by restructuring each sector’s internal governance, co-opting its leadership, and installing behind-the-scenes controls (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 3). The pattern is consistent: an initial use of overt force or legal action, followed by structural reforms that make future confrontations unnecessary. The judiciary has not been subjected to this pattern in the same overt manner, but the broader institutional environment — where every other potential countervailing institution has been brought within the orbit of executive influence — creates a context in which judicial strategic behaviour becomes more, not less, likely. If judges observe that other institutions that attempted to assert independence were restructured or brought to heel, the rational calculation under the strategic model counsels caution. This does not prove that Singapore’s judiciary lacks independence — formal protections remain robust — but it underscores why the question of substantive independence cannot be resolved by pointing to formal design alone.

Shah: Evaluating actual independence requires close reading of cases, attention to how judges explain their reasoning, and awareness of the political and institutional context in which courts operate.

### Other Doctrinal Protections

Beyond institutional design, several doctrinal developments have served to protect judicial independence.

The recognition of the separation of powers as a basic feature of the Constitution under the basic features doctrine provides structural protection. The scope and intensity of judicial review — including pronouncements on how the separation of powers should be conceptualised, the willingness to exercise judicial review in the face of ouster clauses (as in **_Anisminic_** and **_Nagaenthran_**), the weakening of the presumption of constitutionality (**_Jolovan Wham_** \[2021\] SGCA), and the weakening of the presumption of constitutionality of executive action — all contribute to judicial autonomy.

*   However, the doctrine of non-justiciability and the judiciary’s deferential response to legislative overruling raise questions about whether judicial autonomy is truly robust. Ultimately, one can never know for certain whether independence has been compromised; one can only assess from the judicial design when independence is more or less likely to be compromised.

Despite limitations, courts have ruled against the government when warranted. In **_Tan Seet Eng_**, the Court held that CLTPA detention exceeded the Minister’s power, emphasising the judiciary’s task of ensuring government arms are subject to legal limits. Though ultimately overruled when the Minister amended terms, the initial ruling demonstrated independence. Courts have also shown evolution toward greater scrutiny: shifting from a strong presumption of constitutionality (**_Chee Siok Chin_**) to “no more than a starting point” (**_Jolovan Wham_**). In **_Tan Seng Kee_**, the Court emphasised that branches are “co-equal” and courts “will not defer” where constitutionality or legality is challenged, as determination falls exclusively within judicial competence.
