The section 377A litigation also demonstrates this dynamic: courts elaborated on the discriminatory aspects of the provision while leaving repeal to Parliament, which eventually occurred.

### Eval – Are the Current Safeguards for Judicial Independence Enough?

#### Yes – Currently Regime is Satisfactory

There are robust institutional safeguards. Supreme Court judges enjoy security of tenure until age 65 under Article 98(1A), protection against office abolition (Article 95(3)), and guaranteed remuneration charged on the Consolidated Fund that cannot be altered to their disadvantage (Articles 98(6) – (7)). Appointment involves the President acting on the PM’s advice after consulting the CJ (Article 95), ensuring multiple stakeholders. Removal requires a tribunal of at least five judicial-equivalent persons presided by the CJ (Article 98(4)). These safeguards bolster confidence, reduce external pressure risks, and recognise the judiciary’s co-equal status.

The limits imposed on the judiciary’s reach may be understood as appropriate. Non-justiciability reflects genuine judicial competence limitations in technical and polycentric matters. Courts acknowledging limits and deferring to coordinate branches on foreign affairs, national security, or complex policy demonstrates appropriate humility. Singapore’s communitarian culture (CJ Menon) emphasises dialogue, tolerance, compromise, and community above self. The “green light” approach fits this ethos: courts support good governance by articulating principles, serving as the last line of defence, but working collaboratively rather than adversarially with other branches. This has proven effective practically, as with the section 377A litigation where courts elaborated on discriminatory aspects while leaving repeal to Parliament, which eventually occurred.

#### No – The Current Regime is Insufficient

While there are formal safeguards, the appointment process concentrates significant power in the executive. The President acts on the PM’s advice, and while the CJ’s consultation is required, he holds no veto power. In a dominant party system, the ruling party effectively controls appointments. Over time, this could lead to judges whose judicial philosophy aligns with government preferences, subtly affecting willingness to rule against the state. Judicial Commissioners’ lack of security of tenure and the possibility of appointment for specific cases or periods (Article 95(4)–(5)) creates potential for cherry-picking judges for sensitive cases. For the lower judiciary, career advancement depends on the Legal Services Commission where the AG is a member. Although the CJ heads the LSC with final say, the structural connection between judicial career progression and executive input creates at least an appearance of potential influence.

There are also overly broad substantive limits. Combined limitations may excessively constrain the judiciary’s constitutional role. Ouster clauses like Article 149(3) and ISA section 8B create areas largely immune from review. While **_Nagaenthran_** provides assurance through a narrow interpretation, the mere existence of such clauses signals that certain powers are meant to operate beyond ordinary supervision. Non-justiciability risks being applied too broadly. Restrictive locus standi requirements compound this: public rights require “special damage” (**_Vellama_**) or egregious breach for public duties (**_Jeyaretnam_**), setting a high bar that filters out many potential challenges. While preventing frivolous litigation, actions may go unchallenged simply because no individual can meet standing thresholds, even where important constitutional questions are at stake.

The cumulative effect of limitations on judicial review creates tension with de jure constitutional supremacy. If the Constitution is truly supreme, all governmental action should be subject to review ensuring constitutional conformity. Extensive ouster clauses, broad non-justiciability doctrines, and restrictive standing rules undermine this supremacy by creating zones where constitutional compliance cannot be effectively tested. The strong presumption of constitutionality — even if now weakened to a “starting point” — sits uneasily with constitutional supremacy, since all powers should be actively examined for constitutional compliance rather than presumed valid until proven otherwise.

## Contempt of Court – to protect Judicial Independence

The Law of Contempt serves as the principal substantive mechanism for protecting judicial independence from public speech.

The underlying logic of the Administration of Justice (Protection) Act (“AJA”) is that the judiciary cannot function effectively as an independent institution if it is routinely subjected to attacks on its integrity. When politicians, commentators or ordinary citizens make statements that case doubt on the honesty or impartiality of the courts, this has the potential to erode public confidence in the administration of justice – and if public confidence is eroded, the judiciary loses the legitimacy that makes its decisions effective and respected.

*   The AJA therefore criminalises a specific category of speech: speech that poses a risk of scandalising the judiciary.

**Rationale of Contempt of Court Laws**

The law should be limited to what is reasonably necessary for the purpose of preventing interference with the administration of justice (_AG v Times Newspaper_ \[1974\]). Its rationale is rooted in public interest, it aims to protect the administration of justice and public confidence in it, which is crucial for rule of law and maintenance of law and order.

*   It is not meant to protect the dignity of courts or judges (_AG v Hertberg Daniel_ \[2008\]).

There are two types of contempt. **Contempt by interference** includes disrupting court processes, the sub judice rule, and scandalising the judiciary. **Contempt by disobedience** involves actual disobedience of court orders or breach of undertakings.

*   The State Courts Act (s 8) and the Supreme Court of Judicature Act (s 7) both confer the power to punish for contempt.

### Historical Development of the Test for Scandalising the Court

#### Pre-2010: The “Inherent Tendency” Test

Up to 2010, the standard was that it was sufficient to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the words complained of had an inherent tendency to interfere with the administration of justice (**_AG v Barry Wain_** (1991) SGHC). There was no need to prove a real risk of prejudicing the administration of justice, and there was no defence of fair comment. This was an almost strict liability standard.

#### Post-2010: “Real Risk” Test

In _AG v Shadrake Alan_ \[2010\] SGHC (affirmed by SGCA in 2011), the SGHC reframed the test. The _mens rea_ of the offence was satisfied as long as the publication was intentional. The substantive question became whether there was a “real risk that the impugned statement has undermined — or might undermine — public confidence in the administration of justice in Singapore.” The risk could not be a fanciful or remote possibility, yet nor could it only be established in the most serious situations.

Fair criticism was not a defence against a charge of contempt but only a mitigating factor in determining liability and sentencing, assessed by reference to four factors: whether the statement was (1) made in good faith, (2) based on reasoning, (3) expressed respectfully and in a temperate manner, and (4) whether it was directed at the personal character, integrity, or impartiality of judges or courts.

### Post-2016 Test Under AJA and Legislative Clawback – “Risk” Test

The AJA introduced a statutory regime that replaced the judicially developed “real risk” test with a lower “risk” test. This represented a clear legislative clawback from the SGCA’s position in _AG v Shadrake_ \[2011\]. Then Law Minister K Shanmugam told Parliament **that the integrity of the judiciary should be pristine, providing Singapore with a strong anchoring in the rule of law and supporting its status as a pre-eminent legal centre in the region**. The broader policy perspective demanded the institution of a lower threshold than what the courts themselves had been prepared to enforce.

Parliament’s intention in replacing “real risk” with “risk” was to introduce a standard that could be applied more expediently and pragmatically (_Jolovan Wham_ \[2020\] SGCA). Parliament intended to pre-empt hair-splitting or fine distinctions as to the level of risk. It was part of a considered policy choice to come out strongly in favour of upholding and protecting the integrity of the judiciary.

#### Criticism of the Current “Risk” Standard

However, this legislative intervention with the AJA sits uneasily with the judiciary’s own articulation of the proper scope of contempt powers. In 2010, Chief Justice Chan Sek Keong emphasised that contempt mechanisms “should not be used to stifle fair and reasonable criticism of the work of the Judiciary and also judicial decisions.” He explained that the right to criticise is part of the freedom of speech and expression the citizen enjoys in a democracy, and its exercise will encourage or ensure that judges are independent in their decision-making. CJ Chan described this as **“a form of public review similar to the judicial review of executive acts, where judges look over the shoulder of the Executive to correct its mistakes.”** He concluded that the doctrine of judicial independence calls for the judicious use of the contempt power and that courts must ensure “judicial self-restraint in the use of this power.” The 2016 Act’s lower threshold appears to **contradict this understanding**, replacing judicial self-restraint with a broader legislative standard.

#### s. 3(1) AJA – Current Statutory Framework

s. 3(1)(a) of the AJA provides that any person who scandalises the court by intentionally publishing any matter or doing any act that (i) imputes improper motives to or impugns the integrity, propriety, or impartiality of any court, and (ii) poses a risk that public confidence in the administration of justice would be undermined, commits contempt of court.

The Act further addresses prejudgment of pending proceedings (s 3(1)(b)), interference with witnesses or parties (s 3(1)(c)), insulting or obstructing judges (s 3(1)(d)), and other acts that interfere with the administration of justice (s 3(1)(e)).

The 2024 amendments introduced provisions concerning proceedings that involve deception, are fictitious or a sham, or are manifestly groundless (s 3(1)(da) and (db)).

Fair criticism of a court is explicitly stated not to constitute contempt by scandalising (Explanation 1).

#### The Three Core Elements and the Risk Test

The AJA identifies three substantive elements which are closely interrelated rather than entirely distinct.

1.  Intention – the _mens rea_, which the prosecution must establish to some degree
2.  Imptuing improper motives to the judiciary – this cover statements that attribute corrupt, dishonest, or self-interested reasoning to judges in the way they decided a case.
3.  Impugning the integrity of the judiciary more broadly, which covers attacks on the standing or moral character of the courts.

The “risk” test is satisfied, and the statement scandalises the judiciary, if it poses the risk of undermining public confidence in the administration of justice. The risk is assessed objectively from the standpoint of the average reasonable person. This is not a subjective inquiry into the speaker’s intent, nor into the actual impact the statement had on public opinion. The question is whether a national reasonable person would view the statement as posing a risk.

*   The prosecution need only establish this risk – not actual harm to judicial credibility or actual erosion of public confidence, and not an intent to cause such harm. Risk alone is sufficient.
*   The test from _Jolovan Wham_ \[2020\] SGCA is whether the risk is one that the reasonable person coming across the contemptuous statement would think needs guarding against so as to avoid undermining public confidence in the administration of justice? Considering both the content and context of the alleged statement.

#### Fair Criticism Exception

Fair criticism is not a defence available to the accused in the conventional sense. Rather, the sequence is: the prosecution must first establish that the statement posed the risk of undermining public confidence. Once that risk has been established, the court then separately assesses whether the statement constituted fair criticism. ~The legal burden falls on the prosecution to demonstrate that the statement was not, in fact, fair criticism~. The accused does not raise fair criticism and thereby escape liability; instead, the court independently considers whether the statement crossed the line from permissible criticism into impermissible scandalising. This means that the court retains a very substantial degree of control over what is and is not acceptable speech about the judiciary.

To show that a comment constituted fair criticism, the accused must, at the minimum, prove that there was at least an objective basis upon which the criticism was reasonably put forward. (_Jolovan Wham_ \[2020\] SGCA). If the court determines that there was an objective or rational basis for the statement, they might find that it was fair criticism. Then, the burden of proof shifts to the PP to disprove this.

*   For ordinary citizens without legal training, meeting this objective rationality standard is extremely difficult, effectively insulating the judiciary from most public critique

| Wham Kwok Han Jolovan & Anor v Attorney-General [2020] SGCA |
| --- |
| Facts: Wham and another (Tan Liang Joo, “TLJ”) were charged with contempt of court under section 3(1) of the AJPA for two Facebook posts. Wham posted on his public Facebook account that Malaysian judges were more independent than Singapore’s for cases with political implications. Subsequently, TLJ posted that by charging Wham, the AGC had only confirmed that what Wham said was true.Held (s 3(1)(a)(i)): The objective interpretation of Wham’s post was that Singapore judges would decide on the basis of something other than the merits of the case, and were therefore not independent. This assertion was among the most serious attacks that one can make against courts and the administration of justice. TLJ’s post affirmed the allegation; it was irrelevant that TLJ’s post might have resulted from an outburst of anger or emotion.Held (s 3(1)(a)(ii)): The reasonable person reading it would conclude that it posed a risk of undermining public confidence in the administration of justice. The post constituted a direct attack on the independence and integrity of Singapore’s judiciary. It was published on a public Facebook page with over 7,000 followers, and Wham held himself out as a commentator on social affairs and someone knowledgeable on such matters. Having regard to his conduct and manner of posting, it was clear that he intended his post to be taken seriously. Wham’s assertion that he subjectively did not intend to scandalise the court was ultimately irrelevant (at [51]).Fair criticism not made out: Wham claimed his post was informed by three specific case comparisons between Malaysian and Singaporean courts, but the court found that he did not in fact have the case pairs in mind when preparing his post. Even assuming he had, the comparison would not constitute fair criticism as there was no rational basis for comparing those cases — they dealt with wholly different subject matters, entirely different legislation, or compared legal provisions applied to different facts. |

#### SO – The Actual Legal Test

The actus reus is constituted by intentionally publishing any matter or doing any act that imputes improper motives to or impugns the integrity, propriety, or impartiality of any court. The mens rea requirement is satisfied where the publication or statement was intentional. There is no requirement to prove an intention to scandalise or to undermine the court; the intention to publish suffices.

The central inquiry is whether the impugned statement or conduct poses a “risk” of undermining public confidence in the administration of justice, assessed objectively from the standpoint of the average reasonable person. The operative question, per _Jolovan Wham v AG_ \[2020\] SGCA, is whether a reasonable person coming across the statement would think the risk is one that needs guarding against so as to avoid undermining public confidence in the administration of justice. Both the content and context of the statement are relevant to this inquiry. Per _Jolovan Wham_, this statutory threshold is lower than the pre-AJA “real risk” standard in three material respects: the risk need not be substantial or serious; courts are not to engage in fine distinctions or hair-splitting as to the precise degree of risk; and the threshold is to be applied expediently and pragmatically, consistent with Parliament’s policy of protecting judicial integrity robustly. The risk must nonetheless be more than purely theoretical or speculative, such that the standard does not collapse into strict liability.

Once the prosecution has established that the statement posed the requisite risk, the court separately considers whether the statement constituted fair criticism. The burden lies on the accused to prove that there was at least an objective rational basis upon which the criticism was reasonably put forward (_Jolovan Wham_ \[2020\] SGCA). Bare or conclusory assertions will not suffice to meet this threshold. Fair criticism is assessed by reference to whether the statement was made in good faith, whether it was grounded in reasoning rather than bare assertion, whether it was expressed in a temperate and respectful manner, and whether it was directed at the conduct or reasoning of the court as opposed to the personal character, integrity, or impartiality of individual judges. At minimum, there must be an objective basis upon which the criticism was reasonably put forward. If the court determines that there was an objective or rational basis for the statement, they might find that it was fair criticism. Then, the burden of proof shifts to the PP to disprove this.

The charge must be proved beyond a reasonable doubt. The current test may therefore be stated as follows: a person commits contempt by scandalising the court where they intentionally publish a statement that poses a risk — assessed objectively and pragmatically from the standpoint of the reasonable person, without fine gradations — of undermining public confidence in the administration of justice in Singapore, and where that statement does not constitute fair criticism.

### Evaluating the AJA – Does It Actually Protect Judicial Independence?

#### (a) The Chilling Effect as Enhancement of Independence

Because the risk test sets a low threshold for liability — requiring only that a statement pose a risk of undermining confidence, assessed from the standpoint of the average reasonable person — the range of statements that potentially attract criminal liability is very wide. This has an important practical consequence: people who might otherwise criticise the judiciary, whether fairly or unfairly, are deterred from doing so. They know that even if they believe their criticism to be well-founded, the prosecution need only show that it carries a risk in the mind of the reasonable person. This deterrent effect — this chilling of speech about the judiciary — is what makes the AJA’s low standard an enhancement of judicial independence rather than a weakening of it. If people are deterred from making statements that might undermine confidence, the courts are insulated from frivolous, politically motivated, or malicious attacks. The conclusion, counterintuitive at first, is that a low legal threshold makes it easier to prosecute, which deters would-be critics, which protects the courts, which bolsters judicial independence.

Contempt laws are meant to protect judicial independence, which should strengthen judicial review. If judges can operate without fear of intimidation or attacks, they can more confidently review government action. In this sense, contempt laws support rather than limit judicial review, ensuring that judges do not need to cave in to public pressure and are able to make independent, properly reasoned decisions.

#### (b) Fair Criticism and the Risk of Over-Deterrence

However, the picture is more nuanced. Courts can and do make mistakes; judicial reasoning can be genuinely poor. The ability of lawyers, academics, journalists, and citizens to fairly criticise judicial decisions is itself a form of accountability, and stifling all criticism in the name of protecting independence could ironically undermine the very values that judicial independence is meant to serve — namely, the just and accountable administration of justice.

Crucially, the judiciary itself has articulated that fair criticism is essential to, rather than destructive of, judicial independence. In 2010, Chief Justice Chan Sek Keong emphasised that contempt mechanisms “should not be used to stifle fair and reasonable criticism of the work of the Judiciary and also judicial decisions.” He explained that the right to criticise “will encourage or ensure that judges are independent in their decision-making.” CJ Chan described this as “a form of public review similar to the judicial review of executive acts, where judges look over the shoulder of the Executive to correct its mistakes.” He concluded that the doctrine of judicial independence calls for “judicial self-restraint in the use of this power.” This authoritative statement from the head of the judiciary suggests that robust public discourse, including criticism, supports rather than undermines judicial independence by keeping judges accountable and ensuring they maintain public confidence through the quality of their work rather than through insulation from scrutiny.

An additional dimension is the potential effect of fair criticism on independence from judges’ own personal ideologies. If fair criticism is permitted, judges are exposed to a range of external viewpoints that may challenge their own assumptions and predispositions. To the extent that this prevents judicial decision-making from becoming insular or ideologically rigid, the fair criticism doctrine enhances the deeper meaning of independence — independence not just from external pressure, but from internal bias.

George’s work illustrates the practical operation of over-deterrence with considerable specificity. He describes a broader culture of self-censorship in which citizens, academics, and even establishment figures calibrate their public speech to avoid consequences that extend well beyond formal legal sanctions. The “fog of fear” that George identifies does not depend on the actual prosecution of contempt charges; it operates through the uncertainty surrounding where the boundaries of permissible speech lie and what consequences — legal and extra-legal — may follow from crossing them (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 33). George recounts his own experience of withdrawing an expert affidavit in a contempt case — not because he feared prosecution, but because the timing coincided with his own career vulnerability, and even a small risk of adverse consequences was enough to deter participation. If an academic with relevant expertise and a professional duty to contribute to legal proceedings is deterred by this calculus, the chilling effect on ordinary citizens — who lack legal training, institutional support, and the resources to absorb adverse consequences — is considerably greater. Kevin Tan, who in 1999 was publicly rebuked by Lee Kuan Yew for commenting on the ambiguities of the Elected Presidency’s institutional design is another example. Lee stated he was “surprised to see this academic constitutional lawyer from our university” and “alarmed” that Tan was teaching constitutional law — remarks that George argues carried unmistakable implications for Tan’s career (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 33). Tan subsequently left his university position and was reportedly blocked from returning to full-time academia, despite being one of Singapore’s few internationally recognised constitutional law scholars. Critically, Tan’s commentary was sober academic analysis of a constitutional institution — it fell nowhere near the threshold for contempt of court. Yet the professional consequences were severe. The signal this sent to other legal academics, lawyers, and informed commentators considering public engagement with constitutional questions was clear

This connects directly to the AJA analysis. The statutory framework need not produce many actual prosecutions to achieve its deterrent purpose. The combination of a low “risk” threshold, the difficulty of establishing “fair criticism” without specialised legal knowledge, and the awareness that extra-legal consequences may follow public criticism of the judiciary creates a system in which self-censorship is the rational response for most citizens. The question is whether this degree of insulation serves judicial independence or undermines the accountability dimension of the rule of law. As George observes, the government’s OB markers operate precisely through their ambiguity — the inability to know in advance where the line falls is itself the disciplinary mechanism (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 32). If the AJA’s “risk” test operates similarly — where citizens cannot confidently assess whether their criticism will be treated as “fair” until after a court has ruled — then the regime’s chilling effect is a feature, not a bug, of the statutory design. Whether this is constitutionally appropriate depends on whether one views judicial independence as primarily requiring insulation from criticism, or as requiring both insulation from improper interference and accountability through informed public discourse.

#### (c) The Self-Protection Irony and Separation of Powers

There is an important separation of powers dimension. Article 14(2) of the Constitution expressly permits Parliament to pass laws restricting freedom of speech for the purpose of protecting against contempt of court. When Parliament enacts the AJA, it exercises a constitutionally authorised lawmaking power. From a formalistic separation of powers perspective, this is entirely proper: Parliament makes the law within the boundaries set by the Constitution. However, there is a self-protection irony: it is the courts themselves that determine what constitutes fair criticism in any given case. The very institution being protected from scandalising statements decides which statements scandalise it and which do not. This gives the courts substantial latitude to define the outer bounds of permissible commentary on their own conduct — a form of institutional self-dealing that sits in some tension with the rule of law ideal of impartial adjudication. Whether this is an acceptable feature of the regime or an excessive grant of power depends on the perspective one adopts.

*   Cherian George’s analysis of POFMA (the Protection from Online Falsehoods and Manipulation Act 2019) reveals a parallel pattern of institutional self-dealing that illuminates the AJA’s self-protection irony. Under POFMA, ministers are empowered to issue correction and takedown orders against online speech deemed to contain false statements of fact, with “public confidence in the government’s performance” explicitly listed as a protected interest. As the International Commission of Jurists observed, members of a ruling government cannot be deemed impartial arbiters of what constitutes legitimate criticism of their own performance (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 21). George argues that POFMA entrenches the principle of executive dominance by elevating the interests of government bodies to the level of “public interest,” formalising what had previously been an unspoken conflation of party, government, and state.
*   The structural parallel with the AJA is instructive. Just as POFMA gives ministers the exclusive power to determine which “facts” merit legal protection — with no equivalent power granted to opposition politicians, journalists, or citizens — the AJA gives courts the exclusive power to determine which criticism of the judiciary is “fair.” In both cases, the institution being protected from criticism is also the institution that defines the boundaries of permissible criticism. The difference is that POFMA’s self-dealing quality has been widely remarked upon, while the AJA’s equivalent dynamic — where the judiciary determines what constitutes scandalising the judiciary — has attracted less scrutiny, perhaps because the judiciary’s institutional claim to impartiality is stronger than the executive’s. Nevertheless, the underlying structural concern is the same: when any institution is both the subject and the arbiter of speech restrictions, the risk of self-serving boundary-drawing is inherent. From a separation of powers perspective, this suggests that the AJA and POFMA together represent a broader pattern in which each branch of government has acquired tools to insulate itself from critical speech — a pattern that sits in tension with the democratic accountability principles that underpin Singapore’s constitutional system
*   RISKY EVAL BTW

#### (d) Rule of Law and Judicial Independence: A Dual Objective

The AJA is best understood as serving both rule of law and judicial independence objectives simultaneously. The rule of law requires that judicial decisions be respected and that the administration of justice be conducted with integrity. Protecting the judiciary from attacks that undermine public confidence is therefore simultaneously a rule of law objective and a judicial independence objective. Recognising this overlap strengthens rather than undermines the analysis.

The 2016 intervention prioritises judicial integrity over expansive free speech. While maintaining public confidence is essential for the rule of law, the lower “risk” threshold creates potentially chilling effects on legitimate criticism. Well-meaning citizens may be deterred even from constructive critique. This creates a fundamental tension: contempt laws preserve trust and independence, but may discourage the healthy criticism necessary in a democracy. For ordinary citizens without legal training, meeting the objective rationality standard for the “fair criticism” exception is extremely difficult, effectively insulating the judiciary from most public critique.

The 2016 reduction from “real risk” to “risk” represents a concerning tightening of an already stringent regime. _Jolovan Wham_ \[2020\] SGCA illustrates this: even with specific case examples, criticism failed because comparisons lacked “rational foundation.” In a healthy democracy, the judiciary should be subject to robust public scrutiny like other branches. While baseless attacks should not be tolerated, the current regime may sweep too broadly, capturing within “contempt” criticism that, though imperfectly articulated, serves important democratic discourse functions. The tension between CJ Chan’s 2010 articulation of judicial self-restraint and Parliament’s 2016 lowering of the threshold underscores a deeper ambiguity in Singapore’s constitutional framework: whether judicial independence is best served by insulation from criticism, or by accountability through it.

## What is the Cumulative Effect of J. Review Limitations?

The various limitations on judicial review— non-justiciability, ouster clauses, restrictive locus standi, prosecutorial discretion, and the contempt regime — should not be assessed in isolation. Their cumulative effect is what ultimately shapes the constitutional role of the judiciary in Singapore.

On one reading, the current regime is well-calibrated. The institutional safeguards for judicial independence are robust: security of tenure, remuneration guarantees, and independent removal processes all insulate judges from direct executive or legislative pressure. The limits on judicial review reflect a considered allocation of functions among co-equal branches, rooted in a recognition that courts lack the competence, information, and democratic legitimacy to decide all questions of public policy. The “green light” approach, whereby courts support good governance through principled articulation of the law rather than adversarial checking of the political branches, has proven workable. Courts have demonstrated independence when warranted, as in **_Tan Seet Eng_**, and have evolved toward greater scrutiny, as in the shift from a strong presumption of constitutionality to “no more than a starting point” (**_Jolovan Wham_**). In **_Tan Seng Kee_**, the Court forcefully emphasised co-equality and non-deference where constitutionality or legality is at issue. The “strong principles, narrow application” pattern, while cautious, may represent pragmatic institution-building: by articulating protective principles while avoiding confrontation, courts build institutional power and legitimacy for future cases (consistent with the strategic model of judicial behaviour).

On the other reading, the cumulative effect creates tension with constitutional supremacy under Article 4. If the Constitution is truly supreme, all governmental action should in principle be subject to judicial review to ensure constitutional conformity. But the combination of broad non-justiciability doctrines, extensive ouster clauses, restrictive standing rules, structural limits from prosecutorial discretion, and a low contempt threshold creates zones where constitutional compliance cannot be effectively tested and where criticism of the judiciary is deterred. The “legal vacuum” problem — where unconstitutional laws may remain in force simply because no one can meet standing requirements to challenge them — is a direct consequence of restrictive access to courts. In a dominant party system, the risk is heightened: the ruling party effectively controls judicial appointments through the PM’s advisory role, deference through non-justiciability may allow governmental overreach, and the high bar for standing filters out many potential challenges.

George’s work suggests that these limitations are not merely additive but structurally linked. The individual constraints catalogued above — restrictive standing, non-justiciability, ouster clauses, prosecutorial discretion, contempt — share a common feature that is constitutionally significant: the very legal processes that constitute the judiciary’s domain are simultaneously the mechanisms through which the executive exercises political discipline. George documents how the PAP progressively replaced blunt instruments of control — detention without trial, newspaper closures — with precisely calibrated legal tools: defamation suits, contempt proceedings, prosecutions under purpose-built legislation (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 3). The government, he argues, came to prefer settling scores “in the courts rather than on the streets.” This reveals a paradox at the heart of judicial power in Singapore. If judicial power is meant to check the executive, yet the executive strategically deploys legal proceedings as instruments of political management, the judiciary is at once the institution tasked with ensuring legality and the arena through which the executive extends its disciplinary reach. The courts may be applying the law correctly in each individual case — but the systemic effect is that judicial processes, and the threat of judicial processes, function as part of a broader architecture of control. An institutionally independent judiciary applying laws that themselves serve as instruments of political management may satisfy the formal requirements of the rule of law while falling short of its substantive aspirations.

This systemic character has direct implications for the political constitutionalism versus legal constitutionalism debate that runs through these notes. Political constitutionalism rests on the premise that Parliament, as the democratically elected institution, possesses the legitimacy to make final determinations on constitutional questions — and that judicial deference to Parliament is therefore appropriate. But this premise presupposes what George’s analysis calls into question: that the democratic process itself is sufficiently competitive to produce a Parliament that is subject to meaningful electoral accountability. George notes that between 25 and 40 per cent of voters have supported the opposition in recent general elections, yet the opposition’s share of directly elected seats has never reached even 10 per cent — a distortion significantly greater than in comparable first-past-the-post systems (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 16). The US State Department’s own assessment — which George cites — captures the duality: the PAP has maintained dominance partly through genuine voter support and effective governance, and partly through manipulating the electoral framework, intimidating organised opposition, and circumscribing the bounds of legitimate political discourse (_Air-Conditioned Nation Revisited_, Ch. 12). Where the costs of opposition politics are structurally elevated — through the very defamation suits, prosecutions, contempt proceedings, and broader “fog of fear” that these notes have examined — the electoral check is formally preserved but substantively weakened. The “green light” approach to administrative law, which justifies judicial restraint on the basis that good government should be sought through the political process, assumes a political process capable of performing this corrective function. If that process is itself constrained by the legal mechanisms that the judiciary administers, the normative foundation for judicial deference is undermined. Whether this architecture is best understood as pragmatic and principled or as structurally compromising the rule of law depends, ultimately, on whether one believes that the substantive conditions for constitutional democracy are met — not merely its formal requirements

The question for constitutional analysis is whether these limitations represent a pragmatic and principled allocation of functions in a small, efficient state — or whether they cumulatively compromise the judiciary’s ability to fulfil its constitutional role as the guardian of the supreme law. The answer depends in part on the conception of the separation of powers one adopts: if it is understood as strict functional division with mutual checking, the limitations are a source of concern; if understood as flexible allocation with mutual deference, they may be entirely appropriate.

*   A sophisticated analysis must engage with the tension between these perspectives, rather than resolving it prematurely in one direction.
*   Shah: The critical analytical skill is distinguishing between what courts say about judicial power in principle and what they actually do when applying those principles — and evaluating whether the gap between aspiration and application is constitutionally justified or constitutionally troubling.
